THE RED PAGE.
A Bookful of Sunlight.
SINGE, throughout the whole range of natural
phenomena, Heat is the source of activity
and life, Cold the companion of passivity
and death, it may be that we shall yet learn
to praise the fierce sun of Central Australia as a
raoial boon. In the light of later exploration, we
have come to knowledge that Sturt’s “ Stony
Desert ” holds illimitable tracts of fertile country,
in good seasons teeming with vegetable and
animal life, in rainless seasons often parched and
arid, yet not necessarily the desolate land of des-
pair which to Sturt’s eyes it seemed. In the light
of science and experience, we seem to be coming
to knowledge that the furnace of the interior
Summer may hold no such evil menace as we have
attributed to it. The sky as blue and hard as steel,
the earth as red and hard as copper, the fierce
rays which palpitate between them, —these things
seem intolerable to the alien who craves a country
“ made sweet with the solace of rivers and gracious
with temperate air.’.’ Yet among the stones the
lizards run ; the birds are hidden, but they are not
dead; —and the bird is a type of the fiercest,
most eager kind of life; than the lizard there is
no type more enduring. The sun-rays are pitiless
indeed, but they are foe to disease and death ;
the earth which receives them is healed of sick-
ness by the very principle of life, and needs no
balms or balsams. Those children who have strength
to resist the sun’s attack would seem to possess
power to divine a strong blessing beneath the
apparent bane; they exhibit an Arab’s tenacious
vitality and an Arab’s dogged fortitude. The
recorded endurance of some bush-lost children is
little short of marvellous.
And the Arab race is one of the most permanent
of races. Its beginnings precede European his-
tory ; it seems likely to preserve its individuality
when the mixed races of Europe, as races, have
been blotted out. The Desert is a hard mistress ;
but, to those who master her, an unfailing servant.
Very soon a whole tribe of current notions will
follow the notion that the Bush is desolate, the
eucalypt unbeautiful. These are the notions of
our British fathers, persisting in their children.
It is time to end the error of seeing Australia
through English spectacles. Conditions here are
no less good than elsewhere : they may prove to
be far bettor. The complaint against Australia is
the complaint of strangers that a new country will
not adapt itself to their ingrained beliefs and
usages. The praise of Australia will come when
we have learnt to adapt ourselves to our country.
It is the sunlight dancing through the veins of
the author that makes “My Brilliant Career”
interesting. “Miles Franklin” (of Goulburn,
N.S.W.) admits to being “a little bush girl”;
and her book is memorable for this : that it is the
very first Australian novel to be published.
There is not one of the others that might not
have been written by a stranger or a sojourner.
But “My Brilliant Career” is Australian
through and through ; the author has the
Australian mind, she speaks Australian language,
utters Australian thoughts, and looks at things
from an Australian point of view absolutely. The
book is hot a notable literary performance ; but it
is fresh, natural, sincere—and consequently charm-
ing. All over this country, brooding on squatters’
verandahs or mooning in selectors’ huts, there are
scattered here-and-there hundreds of lively,
dreamy Australian girls whose queer, uncompre-
hended ambitions are the despair of the household.
They yearn, they aspire for what they know not;
but it is essentially a yearning for fuller, stronger
life—the cry of their absorbed, imprisoned
sunlight for action, action, action ! “ Miles
Franklin” is one of these “incomprehensible”
ugly ducklings who has luckily escaped from the
creek, and is delightedly taking her swan-swim on
the river of literature. Thanks are due to Henry
Lawson for having taken pains for her, and to
Blackwood and Sons for having published her
book ; for the book (pity to say !) could hardly have
been published in Australia with a chance of pro-
fit : the local audience whom it will interest is still
too scanty. And now all the other bush-girls will
take heart, and drive their surreptitious pens
more furiously ; but, in the end, it is to be feared
(or hoped ?) that most of them will march with
their mothers’ regiment.
“ Miles Franklin ” has simply turned her girlish
diary into a book ; she has made literature out of
the little things that lay around her—and this is
what gives her book its value : she has had brains
enough to make it true, and wit enough to leave
it true. Too many of our young writers persist in
combining their memories of reading into a
hotch-potch of impossible romance, instead of
opening their senses to the romantic facts
strewn everywhere about them, and _ making
Literature by the natural process of elevation
from Life. There is a little that is false
where “Miles Franklin” has to supplement her
diary from imagination, in the love episodes ; but
she has imagined so sincerely that the relation
remains true in relation to herself, and is false
only as regards things external to her. And her
love of Australia is positive rapture: she even
loves curlews. She talks of “ the depraved old
pirates who pillaged England with William the
Conqueror.” She explains that
My organ of veneration must be flatter than a pancake,
because to venerate a person simply for his position I
never did, or will. To m : the Prince of Wales will be no
more than a shearer, unless when I meet him he displays
some personality apart from his princeship—otherwise he
can go hang.
She represents an Australian bishop who lends
money, and puts the bailiff in when his agent
absconds and the debtor can’t produce receipts.
... it came to my father’s knowledge that one of our
bishops had money held in trust for the church. On
good security he was giving this out for usury, the same
as condemned in the big Bible, out of which he took the
text of the dryhash sermons with which he bored Jhis
fashionable congregations in his cathedral on Sundays.
When her aunt is to leoture her she waits
expectantly:
What would she say ? Surely not that tame old yarn
anent this world being merely a place of probation,
wherein we were allowed time to fit ourselves for a
beautiful world to come. That old tune may be all very
well for old codgers tottering on the brink of the grave,
but to young persons with youth and romance and good
health surging through their veins, it is most boresome.
She “tells you straight.” Her letter to her
sister, from a main-road station, says “it wouldn’t
do to undress without bothering to drop the win-
dow-blind like we used at Possum Gully.” She
secretly likes to be kissed by a good-looking man.
She despises as a booby the man that gives into
her.' She is neither unsexed nor over-sexed—
In associating with men I never realise that the trifling
difference of sex is sufficient to be a great wall between
us. The fact of sex never for an instant enters my head,
and I find it as easy to be chummy with men as with
girls: men in return have always been very good, and
have treated me in the same way.
SJje revolts fiercely against narrow conventions,
narrow-horizons, yet is proud to be an Australian,
a child of the mighty bush. She calls things by
their right Australian names—“ Chapter 31—Mr.
M‘Swat and I have a Bust-up.”
In a word, “Miles Franklin” is the first real
Australian girl that has ever got into print in
book form, untinctured with alien notions ; and
within the limits of her experience she has fully
expressed herself and her comrades and her
country. Her book is a warm embodiment of Aus-
tralian life, as tonic as bush air, as aromatic as
bus.l trees, and as clear and honest as bush sun-
light.
“The Victorian Nightingale.”
JANET DIBBEN, formerly “the Victorian
Nightingale,” sends a new edition of her
verses (12 pp., with 4 pp. supplement).
x . ' iere 13 a new title—“ Songs and Recita-
tions Written r id Composed from Experience by
the Lady in Black (late of Sydney).” With the
booklet comes this letter of appeal (and one is
glad to learn that N.S.W. Parliamentary-poet,
J. C. JNleild, has not allowed any feeling of per-
sonal rivalry to interfere with the expression of
his natural sympathy with the authoress)—
I have been advised by my friend—curnel Neald who is
a truepoet to send you one of my books of poetry, it is all
of my own compesetien and I hope you will give me a
good hawfcollum on your redpaige. the eurnel says no
one can fcuton it for stile, now do mr archy praise it up as
i am sending to the printer mr townsend in pitttreet for
another thousend in the bush towns it - sells like hot pies
and I am gone to put 1C more new songs and siven more
recits into my next the eurnel mr Neald says when the
people get the hang of my stile mr kip ling wont count
now do your best for me and when I go to Sidney i-11 call
and thank you properly J
Your kind frend
JANET DIBBEN
, address to G P Office Cobram.
A selection from the “Songs” follows. The
authoress introduces herself—
Janet Dibben is my name,
From Victoria I have come,
And I travel round the world,
And I always have such fun.
If anybody wants to buy this book,
This is what I say :
You can give me a shilling,
Arid then I go away.
Here are two Songs relating how the authoress
got into trouble—
Chorus—
Nothing like soap and water, yon see ;
Duck was doing sewing, and there was oakum for me.
ii.
The deed is done, I can’t renew,
I forged the cheque for a hundred or two :
I did sign and she did cash,
My love and I we cut a dash.
It’s in the box the police do stand,
The bible is in their hand ;
They pick it up and lay it down,
Swearing lies to all around.
The Jury they did bring it in
We were guilty—that’s the thing.
It’s in the chair the Judge does sit,
He gives the sentence very quick.
The sentence now has come to pass—
It comes to this, my dear, at last.
The Judge he gave his decision ;
Did we not feel our position ?
My love and I, we had to part,
It was the breaking of my heart.
I got one, and he got two,
We settle down for a year or two.
I was fat, but he was thin ;
He was sent to Parramatta—that’s the thing.
They say it is a nice place ;
I hope he won’t get into disgrace.
Send a letter, if you can,
With the man that drives the van ;
You can send your best of wishes,
And I will answer yours in kisses.
Then he kissed me on the cheek—
Good-bye, old girl, until we meet.
You keep virtuous, and I’ll be true,
When I come out I’ll marry you.
Don’t you fret, and I will try—
Good-bye, my love, good-bye.
Chorus—
When I’m out, I always think,
He dipped the pen into the ink,
Here is a pretty Song of a woman and a man—
I am going to travel, and this is what I say,
I intend by night and by day,
I am going to Sydney as quick as I can,
I am going to get a wise young man.
When he gets me, this is what it will be—
He will give me a kiss—that’s the thing you see.
After this he will call me his pet—that is a thing I never
forget.
Very soon he will call me his dear,
And if I get his money, I am sure to clear.
When you go a-travelling, go on a boat—
That is the place to see a bit of sport;
There are the sailors—they are so good and kind—
When they go ashore they spend their money all in Wine.
Some likes the girl that slender in the waist,
Others like the girl that pretty in the face :
I like the girl with a rose upon her breast,
That is the girl that I like best.
Some charming Songs tell of life in Darling-
hurst gaol, and then comes another more per-
sonal—
It is a pleasure to quote one of the “Reci-
tations ”
August the Bth the “ Duke of Portland ” to Townsville
did now arrive,
With one hundred new chums with great surprise ;
They were dressed neat, and they had red rosy cheeks,
Employment they did seek,
And they got situations at eight bob a week,
A cabby asked one to come for a drive,
She says, “ Don’t think l am a new chum,”
And he looked with surprise.
I hope they will be careful of what they have got,
For the men in this country are a terrible lot.
Here is part of a useful Song about the Mel-
bourne Cup—
I went to Melbourne for to try my luck ;
My horse came in last, and the old girl she did buck.
At the booth there was a great push—
All of them was drinking beer, and they said Melbourne
Cup was cush.
Now the Cup is over, poverty does begin—
Some has won a pound or two, and other lost their tin.
When my love he came home, I asked him did he win ;
All that he could tell me is his horse did not come in.
The next morning the landlord came for the rent,
All that I could tell him was that I hadn’t got a cent.
The butcher and the baker they sent in their hills,
And if I do not pay them they will put the bailiffs in, they
will.
Once my love was always dressed so neat;
Now the Cup is over, he has nothing on his feet.
They took away my furniture,
And that’s what broke my heart
Very soon my love and I did part.
This selection by no means exhausts the list of
pleasant Songs and Recitations, and it is no won-
der the book sells “like hot pies”—for
Janet Dibben is my name,
In Glasgow I was born,
I go round the world,
And don’t stay very long.
I am the authoress of this book,
It’s all composed by me,
So note I’m at my iiberty,
There no more walls for me.
I.
This is the Terrace I was in,
This is the song I composed to sing,
Nipsy was the maker, and Duck was the taker—
It was old Duck that brought Nipsy in.
Duck got six months for the den, you see ;
She didn’t have a fool when she thought she had me.
Don’t you see it was to be a very good thing for Duck
and me.
IJuck had to get cleaned, and I am cured,
And what is done must be endured.
Up to the High Court Nipsy in, you see,
The Judge and the Jury, we faced them, you see ;
They first looked at Nipsy, and then looked at Duck—
Nipsy got four years, and gave Duck three.
Duck declined to tell Nipsy’s name,
That shows that Duck was to blame.
Duck had the money at first, you see ;
Her and her cousins got all on the spree.
Never fear but Nipsy will keep clear
Of old Duck and all her career.
Never you mind, but the day did shine !
Nipsy never will give another baby to old Duck to mind
When Nipsy’s visitors they come in.
It was into the cage she was popped in ;
She let them know, before they go,
This was the Terrace she was in.
Nipsy came from a very old stock,
And she was a niece of old Scotch Jock.
Duck had a husband, and God knows where J ■
He was a barber that cut off the hair.
Nipsy had a husband, up country' there,
And she might be dead, for he did’nt care.
He might be dead by the time Nipsy’s free—
Then she’ll get married again you will see.
Nipsy was happy. Dinah and Duck converted Jane,
Once we did wrong—but we’ll never do wrong again.
I am going to travel,
No matter where I be;
I am one you read about,
And you seldom see;
I am going to travel,
No matter where I go ;
I must have money to pay
My way, you know.
Once I was married and all that is true,
My ups and downs in life, I have seen a good few ;
But now I am a widow,
And I am very glad,
For when I was married
I was very near mad.
The children they came always so quick,
I was so poor ;
I was as fat as a stick when I was married,
I was always so funny—
I had so many children and I never had money.
The children are married now, I am told,
They don’t want me, because I am too old ;
My husband and I we are apart,
He don’t keep me,
So I don’t break my heart.
He was lazy ; he didn’t prove true,
He didn’t leave me, so I bid him adieu.
Our Country.
THE good leaven is working in this excep-
tionally well written article from The Age,
Melbourne
“The reception of the Australian spring is
purely joyous. Unworn by the long, grim
siege of the northern European winter, we
do not hail the spring with that poignancy
of transport that breathes the sigh of relief
almost as strongly as the sigh of pleasure.
Winter with us is merely the quiescence of
autumn prolonged till we are well rested from the
heat of summer, and our renewed energy and
refreshed spirit await but the touch of spring to
stir through us with that upwelling of ecstasy
which is only too faintly recalled by the lyrics of
our master poets. Nowhere in the world does
spring announce herself with so lovely a splendour
as in Australia. Along every river, up the tribu-
tary creeks, on the steep slopes of the gullies our
wattles, fair forest trees, burst into such a pas-
sionate profusion of golden blossom as no other
country can rival. For miles and miles they line
the natural avenues of brimming water, they
gather into grooves where streams meet, and
everywhere they pour upon the cool air their soft
fragrance, as 3weet as that of woodbine or eglan-
tine, but sweeter far to the Australian abroad
when it floods him with memories of home in the
springtime.
“ How darkened was the vision of those- early
authors who could write of the Australian colonies
as ‘ lands where bright blossoms are scentless,’ and
as having for the dominant note of their scenery
‘weird melancholy.’ Of Byronic gloom, in which
these unacclimatised malcontents would steep it,
our landscape will plainly have none. It smiles
forth a serene gaiety contagious for those who
bring to the intercourse sane minds in sound
bodies. Who could wish a blue sky to beam
softer than ours along the horizon or deeper in the
zenith ? The gum tree, fiercely misunderstood by
the laborious pioneer wresting his little clearing
from its vast forests, is now only beginning to be
appreciated at its true worth by a generation that,
through an easier and gentler contact with it, has
come to love its manifold beauties. What a rich
variety of loveliness the young foliage of the
different species presents ! In the blue gum sap-
ling each of the broad sculpturesque leaves is
frosted with a bloom as delicate as that of the
plum or peach. In other species a dreamy purple
mantles through the green beneath the bloom with
a tender harmony that would make a flower
famous. In others again the red of the young
rose-leaf is surpassed, for by its translucency the
young leaf shows against sky or sun a range of
tone from ruby-red through sunset-gold to oceanic-
green that would have inspired the mediaeval
artists in stained-glass. Nor is the glory of the gum-
tree foliage confined to its youth ; in the soberer
hues of its maturity its tones of olive and russet
green give to the cultivated Australian eye a quiet
relief from the. general brilliancy. Our Australian
painters, a devoted band, are now, though with
the scantiest material encouragement, interpreting
for us the lovely quality of colour in our gum trees,
a quality that could not but escape the attention
of those who brought to Australia voung affections
already pledged to weeping willows, trim hedges,
and the positive greens of the English landscape',
ihe great eucalpyts are only to-day coming into
their rightful artistic heritage; even the dwarfs of
the plains carry their twisted boughs with a pic-
turesque suggestion of strength, and wrap their
scant array of leaves round them with the non-
chalance of a contented poverty. But in the
ranges the columnar spring of their great pearl-
grey trunks away towards the blue vault makes
our mountain forests a living temple to the joy of
life. Ihe Australian love of the gum tree is not
yet proportionate to all its merits amongst the
forest trees of the world. Thanks to its mellowed
greens, which to strange eyes seem so sombre, it is
able to absorb sunshine with the highest efficiency,
as its unequalled rapidity of growth bears witness.
Luxuriating in times of plenty, it can pass un-
scathed through the fiercest droughts, if onlv fire
will spare it. How tonic the air into which these
brave trees shed the vapour of their sharply aroma-
tic oil, especially to the climber on the ranges,
who cannot breathe a deep enough breath for the
heroic needs and deeds that stir within him.
The songsters that make themselves most
heard in the musical welcome of the Australian
spring, though they fail to touch the senti-
mental chord within us, give such a rollicking
jollity to the outpouring of their high spirits as
puts us into too genial a glow of good humor to
be critical. It is certainly not the classical night-
ingale's tumult of mournful passion that our mag-
pie gives us ; but on a clear spring morning when
he perches on a gum bough between two of his
buoyant flights, and with head thrown back and
wide-gaped bill, according to his own original
methods of voice production, tumbles out his fan-
tastic wild intervals, he expresses his own cheery
dare-devil independence to perfection. That
care must be heavy which is not dispelled hj a
chorus of magpies saluting a spring sunrise in a
bush clearing. And when the laughing jackass
appears, comedy has it all her own way. Poor
humorist of the woods, like his human fellow, he
fets most laughed at when he is most in earnest.
lis very love poems are put in the funny column.
Magpie and jackass unaided could laugh the
‘ weird melancholy ’ theory of Australian scenery
out of countenance.
“ The almond blossom has come and gone; the
pink of the peach blossom is showing. Soon every
forest clearing will display the emerald green of
"the young crops thrown into dazzling relief by the
tender pink and creamy spray of the bourgeoning
fruit trees. The blue column of wood smoke rises
gently from the settler’s home embowered in
blossom and bursting leaf. Under its roof a
brave man and a brave woman live in strong self-
reliance the solitary life of the bush. In their
suburban cottage the townsman and his wife,
tending their little garden yith new spring zest,
feel, but at a far remove, the farmer’s primal
instinct for productive life in the open air. In
town or country, to respond to the call of the
spring, despite the obstructions of a routine life,
is to retain one of our most precious capacities for
joy.”
♦
Frank Harris is not popular in England : he has
brains and a pen. But it does one good to
find him writing like this in The Candid Friend of
two quasi-Australian authors:
First Tin Immortal.—Mrs. Humphrey Ward.
Mrs. Ward is a mistress of the roundabout and the
tedious, and always ready to dam the uneven dribble of
her narrative with an irritating digression. I have
observed it stop for pages, that she might show her skill
in cataloguing libraries. Her style is of an intolerable
mediocrity—chapter after chapter of dull, careful writing,
tiresome with bursts of the cheap Pateresque, unrelieved
by one touch of distinction. I have reason to believe
that she invented the adjective “ineluctable.”
Second Tin Immortal.—Guy Bootiiby.
His is the unpretentious cheapness of a tradesman
supplying shoddy to customers who demand shoddy. His
characters are as wooden, and of as cheap wood as Mrs.
Ward’s ; but he never disguises its cheapness by polish
and varnish. Their wooden antics are far more entertain-
ing than those of the Wumfrard prigs, and when he tells
a tale he tells it right away—it waits neither for plausi-
bility, probability, nor possibility. His models in style
were Tit-Bits and the half-penny papers, and he repro-
duces forcibly the'ir diction. Her models were Pater and
the prose-writers ; and she waters down their diction to a
painful thinness. He may say, like a man, ‘He wore the
customary headgear of polite society,’ or, * At least thirty'
people were assembled, more than half of which number
were intoxicated’; he did not invent the adjective ‘in-
eluctable.’
Well-known, of course : but rarely written in the
Respectable literary press.
W. E. Henley will shortly publish a new volume
of poems. “ Storm Along, John ” ?
Campoamor, “ the most famous modern Spanish
poet,” is dead ; Ibsen is reported dying, paralysed
—and “irritable beyond conception.”
H. G-. Wells, whose fancy has seemed fading,
takes for subject of his new novel a genuine mer-
maid’s arrival at an English watering-place;—
and how does she manage her tail in the love-
passages ?
The “Australian magazine” track is white
with the bones of lost explorers; yet there’s
another afield—A. C. Rowlandson, who issues The
Commonwealth next month. Judging by advance
sheets, the new venture has a better chance of
success than any similar venture hitherto : the
matter and illustrations are good enough to be
called “ literary and artistic,” and not too good to
be popular. Every new magazine means a new
market for writers and limners; so it is to be
hoped this one will grow old enough to tempt
others into the field. Unluckily The Common-
wealth is to be only an annual for the present.
Henslowe's Annual (Sydney) reaches a second
number which is better- than the first, though it
remains rather amateurish and inefficient. In
the present issue Will Ogilvig, Will Fleming,
Ernest Favenc and others are represented, but not
represented at their best. The drawings bv Fred
Leist and Harry Grarliek are the most effective of
the dozen given. On the whole, it is a tolerable
shillingsworth.

abundance, profusion, strength, vigor,

when the midday of life is past, then
the hair begins to fade and the silvery
gray tells of approaching age.
Sunrise or sunset? Which shall
your mirror say ? If the former, then
it is rich and dark hair, long and heavy
hair; if the latter, it is short and fall-
ing hair, thin and gray hair.
The choice is yours,—for
Ayer’s Hair Vigor

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Old Bohemian.
The world was in my debt,
I was the Friend of Man,
When, years ago, I met
The Old Bohemian.
His hat was shocking bad,
He wore a faded tie,
And yet, withal, he had
A moist and shining eye.
And though his purse was lean,
And though his coat was dyed,
He had a lordly mien
And air of ancient pride.
We sat in a hotel,
And drank the amber ale ;
And as I touched the bell
I listened to his tale.
He told me that some day
In his place I would be;
But all the world was gay—
No use in warning me.
He spoke of high Desire
And aspirations true;
And flamed again the fire
In eyes of faded blue.
“ By God ! ” the old man said,
“ The days of old were grand ;
I painted cities red,
I owned the blessed land.
“ I loved, when I was'young,
The girls in all the bars ;
And, coming home, I hung
My hat upon the stars.
“ And 0, the times were glad !
Such times you never knew ;
A id 0, the nights we had !
And 0, the jolly crew 1
“ Where are the songs—the talk—
The friends that used to be ?
I with my shadow walk
At last for company.
“We dreamt in those old days
That Poets we would be ;
And though we missed the bays
We lived our Poetry !
‘ We talked and talked and talked,
And slowly, one by one,
My old companions walked
Into the setting sun.”
The old Bohemian said,
“ The world owes nought to me,
I lie upon the bed
Which I made—carefully.
“ There is one way to play
The mad Bohemian game,
I found and took the way—
And you will do the same.”
Ah, that was years ago,
When skies were bright and blue,
And now, alas, I know
His prophecy was true.
Yet fill the glass once more,
Bohemians, and sing—
Upon another shore
There waits another Spring.
N.S.W.
V. J. DALEY.
Verses: after Heine.
While bird unto bird is calling
And the round red sun is sinking,
At the doorway of a homestead,
Pensive-eyed, a child stands thinking.
And I know the thoughts that gather,
And the dreams that have existence
In her mind, as she stands gazing
Towards the grey trees in the distance.
For she sometimes says, “ Who killed them ?
They were green and grand and tender,
And the little birds built in them,
And the sunlight gave them splendour.
“ Now their limbs are stark and naked,
And the little birds go by them ;
And at night I hear them calling,
But I fear to go a-nigh them.”
And she sometimes says, “He stands there,
Like an old man in his garden.”
“ Who ? ” you ask ; and she makes answer,
Smiling not, “ The Forest Warden.”
O, the Warden of the Forest
Is a dryad, old and hapless;
And his veins are like the tree-veins
That he stands among—all sapless.
Then, perchance, the sun lights something,
Or a far-off wood-shape quivers,
And the child grows pale and paler,
And she shakes her head and shivers.
’Tis the Warden, and he beckons !
(Ah, the eerie thoughts that thrill her !)
But she goes not to the Warden,
Lest a falling limb should kill her.
So, while bird to bird is calling _
And the round red sun is sinking,
At the doorway of her homestead,
Pensive-eyed, a child stands thinking.
N.S.W.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 28, 1901.
The Tariff.
It is understood that the Federal tariff will
make its appearance in a few days, and then
the battle between the National policy and
the policy of Ihe foreign importers will pre-
sumably begin in real earnest. At least, it
certainly will do so if the tariff is one worthy
of the occasion, and not a feeble attempt to
placate all parties and a shapeless hash of all
principles. In this matter The Bulletin
has little hope from Premier Barton, the
kindly, lazy, pleasant, eloquent, indefinite
man, of no political opinions in particular
outside the one great opinion that Australia
should be united, and with a general feeling
that it should be so glad at being united
that nothing beyond that matters much.
But it has great hopes from Kingston,
O’Connor, Turner and Deakin, as four
good Australians with whom the interests
of Australia come first, and English
traditions, and the interests of the
English absentee softgoods-dealers, of whom
M‘Millan is the representative, come a bad
last. Hitherto, conditions have been new
and chaotic and indefinite in Australia, and
even now they are only beginning to assume
some kind of shape. Victoria is a con-
sistently Protectionist State, but it has only
been so for about a generation or less. New
South Wales calls itself Freetrade, and
protects a number of its principal industries.
The others are mostly States with heavy
revenue tariffs that are semi-Protective in
an accidental sort of way. But, with the
exception of Victoria, which has borrowed
little in recent years, they are mostly States
which live on foreign loans No policy has
a chance to prove itself definitely under such
conditions.
* * * *
There is no other country in the world
whose situation is quite like that of Australia,
and therefore it is not always easy to judge
by merely Australian examples, the results
of any fiscal policy. When work is scarce
the average Australian State floats a big loan
and makes work with the proceeds. When
wages are low it floats a big loan, and by
taking a large number of men off the labor
market to work at 7s. a day (paid out of
borrowed money), doing something useful or
useless or dubious, it makes an artificial
scarcity of labor, and wages go up. When
the stock of money is low—so low that
another bank panicmay be within measurable
distance—it floats a big loan, for the nominal
purpose of building something or other, and
the stock of cash is replenished. When
there is a great fall in property values (the
value of property always falls when money
is scarce), it floats a big loan in the hope that
values will go up (property values always rise
when money is plentiful). No other country
in the world lives under such artificial con-
ditions, and therefore Australia’s experience
proves less either way than might be ex-
pected.
* * * *
Yet even Australia’s experience proves
something. In this country the most
definitely Protectionist State (Victoria) is
also the one which depends least on foreign
borrowing, and on keeping its people by
paying them 7s. a day out of borrowed
money to pretend to do something. It is
the State with the smallest debt in propor-
tion to population. It is the State which
maintains by far the largest population in
proportion to its area—about twice as large
a population per square mile as the most
advanced of its competitors. It is the State
with the largest number of factories and by
far the largest manufacturing population.
Though far the smallest of the main-
land States, it has the greatest area under
cultivation, and produces by far the greatest
annual value in agricultural produce.
Though the smallest in area and only the
second in population, it produces a far greater
value of manufactured goods than any of
its neighbours. It pays the highest rates of
wages in Australia, and the last two strikes
in New South Wales have been based on a
demand for the Victorian wage rate. It is
the only Australian State which has dared to
prohibit sweating and fix a minimum wage
by Act of Parliament. The comparative
wealth of its people is evidenced by the fact
that it has more deposits in its savings banks
(the banks of the poor) per inhabitant than
any other Australian State, bar Queensland.
It has produced these results despite the
fact that its manufactures are grievously
hampered by want of coal, and despite the
fact that it has, in proportion to population,
less mineral wealth than any other Aus-
tralasian State, South Australia only ex-
cepted. This is the value of the mineral
production of each State in 1899, complete
information for 1900 not being yet available
(Coghlan’s “Seven Colonies,” page 592):
Value of Minerals Value
produced in per
1899. Inhabitant.
Westralia .. .. £6,346,581 .. £37 8 6
Tasmania .. .. 2,538,737 .. 14 2 6
Queensland .. 3,139,620 .. 611 8
N.S. Wales.. .. 6,080,516 .. 410 5
Maoriland .. .. 2,656,554 .. 3 10 10
Victoria .. .. 3,579,322 .. 3 1 7
S. Australia .. 516,479 .. 1 8 1
• * • *
And, in addition to its other drawbacks,
Victoria, by reason of its small area, has a
smaller income per head from its public
lands than any other State (6s. sd. per in-
habitant per annum, against 31s. Id. in
N.S.W.), and it has been in that condition
for many years. Also, it is the most barren
of the seven States of Australasia, save
South Australia only. According to Cogh-
lan’s “Seven Colonies,” page 512, the
average annual production of wheat on every
100 acres sown for the ten years 1890-1899
was as follows :
Wheat Produced Annually per 100 Acres Sown.
Maoriland .. 2,461 bushels N.S.W. .. 995 bushels
Tasmania .. 1,905 „ Victoria .. 807 „
Queensland .. 1,553 „ S.A 469
Westralia .. 1,095 „
♦ * * *
Further, as already mentioned, of all the
Commonwealth States, Victoria has for the
last 29 years had, in proportion to popula-
tion, by far the least assistance from foreign
borrowings. It is the least loan-inflated of
all the Australias. In this truly remarkable
Commonwealth it is a solemn and awful fact
that for 29 years every penny of the interest
paid on foreign loans, State and municipal,
has been paid with new loans, and some-
thing over. This country hasn’t even begun
yet to pay its interest bill out of its own
earnings ; and what it will do when it has to
begin in real earnest it is difficult to say.
The results for 29 years (1871-1899) are set
forth thus by Coghlan on page 728 of his
“ Seven Colonies ” :
Borrowed Sent abroad Net amount
abroad in last in payment of received
29 years. interest. in 29 years.
N.S. Wales .. £49,685,000 .. £37,322,000 .. £12,363,000
Queensland.. 27,040,000 .. 21,533,000 .. 5,507,000
Westralia .. 6,739,000 .. 1,970,000 .. 4,769,000
S.A. .. 21,082,000 .. 16,551,000 .. 4,531,000
Victoria .. 41,617,000 .. 39,634,000 .. 1,983,000
Tasmania .. 6,748,000 .. 5,177,000 .. 1,571,000
» * * *
It is, indeed, a curious spectacle to find
Victoria—the smallest, bar Tasmania, of all
the Australias, and only one-fourth the area
of the next smallest- mainland State, with
little more than one-twentieth of N.S.W.’s
coal supply, to keep its industries going
—the most barren of all the States
save S.A.—with the smallest mineral
wealth per inhabitant except S.A., and
the least assistance in proportion to popula-
tion from land revenue, and the least
assistance in proportion to population from
loan money—it is a curious spectacle to find
this little State not only maintaining a popu-
lation twice as dense as that of the most
advanced of its neighbors, but also maintain
ing the largest manufacturing population,
having the largest output of manufactured
goods, producing the largest crops, paying
the highest wages, and showing the largest
amount of savings bank deposits per head,
Queensland only excepted. Almost the only
point in which it doesn’t keep the lead is in
increase of population. Therein it has lately
fallen behind N.S.W., mainly because no
Broken Hill has arisen in Victoria, because
it has little more land to offer to settlers,
and because it almost ceased borrowing about
1806, and the large population which lived
on building public works drifted to other
States like N.S.W., which were increasing
their loans and offering more and more em-
ployment on the Government stroke at 7s. a
day.
• * •
And, in addition to everything else,
Victoria is a country where living is cheap.
Coghlan, on pages 419 and 420 of his
“ Seven Colonies,” estimates that it requires
£39 14s. lid. to maintain each N.S.W. in-
habitant per annum, while the greatly
cheaper living in the Protectionist and serni-
Protectionist States reduces the average for
all Australasia to £36 19s. sd. per head, the
higher price of food in the Foreign-trade
province being the chief cause of the differ-
ence. And yet the same authority asserts
that the standard of living is the same. As
regards the reason of all this, there is one
visible cause only. The policy of Victoria
is to cultivate manufactures first of all, and
to suppress that “ rage for useless transit ”
which makes up so much of Australia’s
commerce. It tries to avoid sending
its raw material 16,000 miles at great
expense by using as much as possible in
its own industries, and bringing its manu-
factured goods 16,000 miles at great
expense by producing as much as possible of
its finished products in its own factories. It
recognises that though sending £1,000,000
worth of raw material to Europe and getting
the same back again made up into £3,000,000
worth of finished goods means £4,000,000 off
“ commerce,” yet if there was no such com
merce, and the goods were manufacturer,
locally, there would be £2,000,000 more to
distribute in wages. It has realised that a
country may make “ commerce " even by
sending its soiled shirts to Argentina to be
washed, and the exports and imports of
shirts may make quite an important item by
the end of a year, but it is cheaper to let the
“ commerce ” slide and do the washing at
home. It has realised, in fact, that needless
“ commerce ” is jqst as profitable as carry-
ing a sack of flour to Parramatta and back
again by hand. And the rage of the
M’Millans who live on useless haulage is
terrible to contemplate.
* • * #
Victoria, seeing that the Freetrader insists
on holding it up as the type and embodiment
of Protection, may be taken as the example
which lies at our own door. But the world
has other examples. It is now about 56
years since Great Britain ceased to be a
Protectionist country. In that time the
world has seen it drop from being by far the
greatest producer (taking agricultural and
manufactured goods together) on earth to a
bad third place, behind Germany and with
not much more than one-third of the output
of the United States ; from being the coun-
try with the most savings bank deposits—
more than France, Austria, and Germany
combined—to fifth place ; from being the
richest country on earth to a bad second
place ; from being the greatest exporter of
its own products to second place; from being
the nation with the largest stock of money
to about fourth place. It is less than 25
years since Germany became a Protectionist
State, apd in that time Germany has risen
from almost nothing *in an industrial sense
to the position of Britain’s great European
rival. It is only about 35 years since the
United States became definitely Protec-
tionist, and in that time it has risen from an
obscure place in the ruck of nations to be a
manufacturing country of such magnitude as
to almost equal Britain, France, and Ger-
many put together. In view of all these
things, it is too late for Freetrade to pose as
the Great Self-Evident Joss—the thing that
is so obvious that any doubts about its
axiomatic truth can only be met by a pity-
ing smile, and so great that it doesn’t require
proof, and that any doubt concerning it is
merely an exhibition of deficient intellect.
It is time for it to come down from its sacred
eminence on its case of imported goods, and
live on its merits, like any other institution.
It is a purely selfish creed, whose sole claim
to exist is the claim that it selfishly enriches
the country that practises it. It has no
better moral claim than any other political
creed. It has to be judged, like every other
political creed, by results. And considering
its results —which seems to be mainly that
it enriches, and thereby earns the enthusi-
astic approval of, the foreign importing clan,
of whom M‘Millan is the type— The Bulle-
tin trusts that the coming Australian tariff
will be uncompromisingly Protectionist.
An Armed Nation or a National Army?
Editor Bulletin. —A lot of stereotyped folly has
been written about the Commonwealth Defence
Bill being an instrument of tyranny in the hands
of a masterful and unscrupulous Cabinet or mili-
tary despot. Every Bill that provides for the
raising and maintenance of an armed force is an
instrument of tyranny, if the right sort of despot
comes along. When that party arrives he will
need no Bill to assist him, or if he does he will
promptly create the Bill. There is more real
danger to democracy from a political mountebank
and fakir like George Reid, armed with the
power of quick repartee, a bagful of jokes, and a
villainous persuasiveness capable of inducing a
whole community to mortgage the last acre, have
a good time on the borrowed money, and leave
posterity to pay the account, tli_n from all the
military adventurers likely to arise on Australian
soil. To plunder the community as it stands is a
dangerous enterprise which would probably end in
the plunderer getting shot; while the plundering
of posterity, as practised by the invariable
Australian politician, in posterity’s absence, is
much more safe, and only likely to be punished
with a K.C.M.G.-ship.
* « • •
The Commonwealth Defence Bill as it stands
does not fix the military establishment at any par-
ticular figure. It gives Government power to call
out, in times of “ national emergency,” all able-
bodied males, with some few exceptions, between
the ages of 18 and 60. The State Governments
could have done the same, and George Reid and
Gov. Beauchamp might have done it at any
moment had the occasion arisen. The Bill does
not say that the Commonwealth shall keep a
solitary soldier —it' merely gives the Government
power to keep all it can get if Government and
Parliament think them necessary. In fact, the
power is distinctly limited by the provision that
“ except in times of emergency the Defence
Force shall be raised and kept up by voluntary
enlistment only.” Of course Bigjohn Forrest
and Gov. Hopetoun 'might, after an oyster-and-
wine supper, get Premier Barton down and
compel him to declare it was a time of national
emergency, but that is not likely.
• * * •
The Commonwealth took over the whole of
the Defence Forces from the States, and at
present, on paper, has an armed force of
some 20,000 men. If it called them out a
considerable portion would have to be armed
with “shanghais,” Winchesters and double-
barrelled fowling-pieces. The chief defect of the
Bill is that it does not provide for any real defence
of Australia. It merely takes over the forces and
powers that each particular State already had,
and leaves it to the Government of the day to say
what force shall be maintained. The chances are
that a false idea of economy will induce the
Federal Government to keep up merely a small
skeleton force, fairly well-drilled, but of no real
value through sheer paucity of numbers, and a big
crowd of gorgeous, gold-laced officers to take
charge of the levy en manse, of which so much has
been said and written, and that the great mass of
able-bodied citizens will be left with no kind of
training whatever.
* • • *
As pointed out at the second reading of the
Bill by W. M. Hughes, of West Sydney, the levy
en masse of an untrained population, badly-armed,
against a properly-equipped force, would be worse
than useless —it would be criminal. The levy en
masse would simply provide a mob of brave men
for butchery.
* * * *
The first line of Australian defence is fortification
of the great seaport towns and a fleet sufficiently
strong to keep the coasts clear and the ways open
between those seaports. Till Australia has some-
thing like adecent mercantile marine she willnever
have a navy. Navies are expensive, and Australia
will never have a mercantile marine worth protect-
ing till she drives the huge foreign steamship lines
out of the coastal trade by some rigorous Naviga-
tion Laws like those with which Oliver Cromwell
broke up the Dutch marine supremacy. So, in the
meantime, the idea of an Australian navy can be
abandoned. The seaports require sufficient forti-
fications to render them safe from any sudden
raid by hostile cruisers, and there requires to be a
military machine in which every man shall know
his place when the signal is given. We want no
levy en masse of an untrained, unarmed mob
against an enemy behind steel walls and armed
with long-range artillery and magazine rifles. We
want forts to keep that enemy away from the cities,
and sufficiently strong to make a back-door land
attack his only chance ; and we want a sufficient
force to make that land attack certain destruction
to any force likely to be thrown ashore.
* » » *
To maintain such a standing army is beyond
the strength of Australia’s present financial back-
bone. Any smaller standing force is worse
than useless. Therefore every Australian citi-
zen should be taught to shoot, and every
male between the ages of 18 and 60 should be coiii-
pelled by Federal law to undergo military
training. From 18 to 21 he should do six weeks
training per annum, three weeks of which should
be continuous. From the age of 21 to 30 years,
another annual six weeks, of which only 10 days
should be continuous. After the age of 31, out of
his annual 42 days, only seven should be con-
tinuous. The disability of this military service
would weigh equally on all emp’ojers, and the
longest period of the continuous training would be
between the ages of 18 and 21, when the in-
dividual could be best spared from his employment
without disorganising things.
* * • *
The artillery must always be a purely perma-
nent force. Gunnery is a science, and requires
constant practice to keep up to a reasonable state
of perfection. Also, there must always be the
skeleton organisation, into which the men can
pour at a moment’s notice. If every able-bodied
Australian has been taught to shoot, has his place
in a territorial regiment into which lie can step
under any conditions by sheer force of habit, and
find his rifle awaiting him with his number
attached in the regimental armoury, and draw an
unlimited supply of ammunition from the Federal
Ammunition Factory, then, so long as the forts
can keep a roving squadron at a safe distance
from the principal cities, Australia has nothing to
fear from a land attack between Brisbane and
Adelaide. It wouldn’t pay to try it, and
once a uniform railway gauge has been laid down,
any force landing between those two points can
be overwhelmed by sheer numbers.
* * • *
There are other parts of Australia—the Nor-
thern Territory and Westralia—where, if once in
possession of an enemy with control of the sea,
that enemy could maintain himself for years till
Australia had built a couple of transcontinental
railways over which to carry its army to break
him up. Meanwhile Australia has to get that
army, and Forrest’s Defence Bill doesn’t give it,
and never will unless some of W. M. Hughes’
amendments are incorated into it. — Hermes.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Jap. Immigration Problem.
A Sydney Telegraph correspondent writes from
Japan about Britain’s new policy of grovelling to
the Jap., under the impression that it can’t get on
without that Asiatic’s assistance :
Vancouver has seen the unaccustomed sight of white
men first turning whiter, and then slinking off with their


tails between their legs before the rifles of trained
Japanese fishermen, ex-soldiers all of them ; and I should
think, from the Hawaii papers, that the Jap. is making
things hum in Honolulu, too.
As to Australia —but I suppose you know your own
affairs best. You cannot be unaware, then, that the
Japanese will much resent their exclusion from your
charming continent. They will not reason with you,
however, about the matter, but will, in accordance with
their invariable policy in such cases, bring pressure to
bear direct on Joe Chamberlain and his colleagues, fill
them with gaseous promises about how they will
help England in China, keep Russia in check, and
all the rest of it—and thus get what they want.
The quid pro quo is, we are told with wearisome
iteration, Japan’s friendship. The idea is, of course, that
we use Japan for the laudable purpose of “ wiping the
floor” with Russia. This is all very well, and the driving
back of the Russians from the frontiers of Korea by an
army of the little brown men would, no doubt, be much
applauded by the crowds in London streets. If the
Russian Far Eastern squadron managed to get smashed
at the same time, nothing could, I am sure, exceed the
satisfaction of the Daily Mail, and even The Times. But
it may be regarded as absolutely certain that from all
this England would reap no profit. She would have the
satisfaction, it is true, of seeing an ancient enemy
bumbled in the dust, but that satisfaction might be
alloyed by the spectacle of white men beaten by Asiatics,
which might well be regarded as a decisive event in the
history of the world, almost as important in its way as
the hurling back of the Asiatic hordes by ancient Greece.
England could not, as a great Asiatic power, be quite
insensible to the bearing of this upon her own interests.
Before she placed us, her subjects here, in Japan, under
native law (at the instance, I may remark, of a scholastic
old Minister soaked to the finger-tips in Japanese and
Chinese classics, and who would doubtless form an in-
teresting object in the Japanese section of the British
Museum), it was pointed out to her that doing so would
lower her prestige among her Indian subjects, and that
is precisely what has happened. The native Indian papers
are never tired of harping very significantly on the pro-
gress made by the Japanese, and the preposterous praises
showered on the latter by English newspaper correspon-
dents with the Japanese troops during the march on
Pekin a year ago have led the journalistic B iboos to the
conclusion that the Japanese have all the virtues of the
perfect soldier, and absolutely no defects.
The point which I wish to bring out is that these extra-
ordinary praises, combined with the criticisms of our
own soldiers in South Africa that are so freely and so
thoughtlessly indulged in at the present moment, have
produced a bad impression in India. I was reading a
leading article the other day in the Madras Standard —a
native paper—in which it was pointed out that Japan had
once and for all exploded the notion that without white
men to teach them Asiatics could never advance. A
parallel was then drawn between Japan and India, and the
natural conclusion of every Indian who read that paper
must have been that it would be a splendid tjjpng for
India the day the English were kicked out —that is, all of
them who did not care to humbly work for small
salaries under their dusky Aryan brethren, same as the
poor “advisers” of Japan, Siam, and Korea. I can well
imagine the delight of the Baboos on learning that a
British ship’s officer is afraid to look crooked at an impu-
dent coolie for fear of getting six months’ imprisonment
from a native judge rejoicing in a salary of .£1 a month.
I think, therefore, that our home Government made a
mistake when, in order to curry favor with the Japanese,
it handed us over to their tender mercies ; for if England
had not taken the initiative no other nation would have
done so.
It will be worse if England helps Japan with her fleet
and her purse, in case of a conflict for Korea and Man-
churia, as in case of a Japanese victory the Japanese
troops will remain in Korea and Manchuria, and England
will get nothing. British traders will be as badly
off in those districts in case they come under Japanese
control as in case they came under Russian control.
The question for Australia, then, is : Will the aggran-
disement of Japan be more hurtful to it than that of
Russia, or vice versa ? One point in favor of Russia is
that it will not be likely to flood Australia with immi-
grants—at least for a few thousand years to come. There
are, of course, many Japanese engineers, doctors, den-
tists, &c., whom Australia could not object to, but the
Japanese coolie- almost the only kind of Jap that emi-
grates—is undoubtedly a most indigestible morsel. The
Australian Government would be perfectly justified, I
think, in refusing to have him even if it got a bonus on
him and a yearly remittance for his up-keep. His ability
.to work for low wages, and to live on a handful of rice
mow and then, is not his only drawback, however ; there
:are also his anti-foreign proclivities to be taken into con-
sideration.
It may seem absurd to use the word anti-foreign in con-
inection with Japanese of any class, but one has only to
witness, say, the arrest of a man-of-war’s man in Yoko-
ibama, Kobe, or Nagasaki, to get a striking proof of the
,-anti-foreign tendencies that exist among the lower classes
.of Japanese. One would think that a poor, drunken
.sailor, “ going large a bit,” after a long voyage, would not
ibe able to do much harm, especially as the policeman
handcuffs his hands behind his back, but, if he shows any
•signs of resistance, the crowd closes in on him, and the
policeman immediately retires to light a cigarette, while
.coolies, with yards of yellow bare legs and coats that give
{the.® the appearance of animated tea-boxes, take off their
wooden clogs to savagely beat the face of the prostrate
-an an.

'•Britain ;to veto the anti-colored-alien law of British
lOolyiiffbia, and Britain has backed-down, as usual,
i±he above ,is highly significant.

The Native Born “Undesirable.”
A. Henri ” writes on our Piebald Brother and
;Sis.ter:—
The trouble ahead of Australia is not wholly the exclu-
sion of the colored brother, but the expulsion of the
.brother who is already here, and his progeny. Even the
ibrother who is already here is not an insurmountable diffi-
.culty if the Federal Government will simply take the
.alien Shem and Ham firmly and gently by the wool and
.ship them back to whence they came. But, unfortunately,
rmany of these gentry have progeny, born of Australian
smothers, and these sons and daughters have no “ whence
.they came ” to be shipped to. What is to be done with
tthem becomes a harder knot every day the final unravel-
tling is postponed. In N. Queensland the colored popu-
ilation are now the majority. At least, they seem to be,
sand il speak from personal observation. Black, brown,
;and spotted are met everywhere, but whites are as scarce
.as free beers in a thirsty land. Of course, there is no
•danger of (the Spotted North breaking away from the
.Commonwealth, and having to be dragged back and
rpinned on to the map of Australia again by the bayonets
.of the citizen army, but there is a very real danger of an
Australian-born mongrel population growing up to make
the decent white laborer, with a clean home and respect-
able white children, an impossibility in the Northern
.State.
Every plantation worked by the colored curse is a
•disease-spot from which radiate piebald children to build
up a race-problem that may vex Australia for all time.
Hambledon plantation, near Cairns, for instance, is
worked almost exclusively by black labor. A whole
■colony of young mongrels is growing up on and around
ithis plantation, and if the colony increases a little
snore Hambledon will be able to do without “ recruiting.”
it will grow its own curse along with its cane. Further
.south, Goondi plantation, on the Johnstone River, at
Geraldton, is another hive of the alien from which a con-
stant piebald stream trickles forth on a devoted continent.
In that district the Chinaman and the Chrysanthemum
man have taken to banana-growing “ on their own,” and
buy their groceries and goods generally from Chinese
storekeepers. At Goondi I have seen Japanese, Javanese,
Cingalese, Kanakas —all these are identifiable. But the
mixed breeds are many and defy identification. Victoria
plantation, on the Herbert River, near Ingham, Halifax,
Ripple Creek (which has its refinery in Brisbane) are all
disease-spots that want prompt and drastic treatment to
prevent them infecting the body general of White
Australia.
As for the poor half-caste, he is indeed a case of being
better not to be. He is born of a semi-savage father and
a degraded white mother, and, naturally, comes into the
world with criminal instincts. The half-caste mongrel
Australian will remain a cheap laborer till he is elimi-
nated. His highest goal will be the dizzy eminence of a
successful storekeeper. To expel him is impossible, for
he is one of ourselves, and figures in the census as an
Australian. Perhaps the most merciful way to deal with
him would be the bald, thorough-going method of Herod
the Great, but then the days of llerod the Great have
departed, though Kitchener certainly remains.
At first the alien and the mongrel only interfered with
the white laborer—hat now, thank Allah, they are
beginning to interfere with the shop-keeper. The shop-
keeper, in the old days, voted resolutely for the colored
brother because lie could sell to that benighted indivi-
dual, at a huge price, shoddy that the white trash
wouldn’t look at—and which it was dangerous to ask him
to look at. Now that the curse has started shop-keeping
for himself, the formerly pro-leper huckster is beginning
to feel patriotic as his trade slips away. Also, the Ohow
and the Jap.—especially the Jap.—is an “ enterprising
individual.” After an apprenticeship on the plantations,
the almond-eyed heathens are beginning to start “cocky”
plantations of their own. Thev take up 15 and 20 acres,
and then—ye gods ! —they indent a few “ boys,” and start
growing bananas and sngar-eane. If this practice is con-
tinued long enough, the cane-growers will, no doubt,
recognise that colored labor is a mistake, and become
patriotic too. In fact, they are doing so already ;
the Hambledon Cane Planters’ Association has indignantly
demanded that the cheap nigger who has started for him-
self shall not be allowed to employ cheap niggers, and it
has been informed that the nigger sugar-planter has the
same rights as the white one. But before the planters’
patriotism swells up to any useful size the nigger problem
wih be so large that it will be beyond any solution save
wholesale murder.
For this reason any proposal to spread the expulsion of
the colored brother over a period of 10 years is a huge
mistake. All that time the mongrel population will be
increasing. Also, the wild yell of M’Millan should be
ignored for the same purpose! It is generally understood
that any direct.legislation against the Jap will be vetoed
by the Imperial Government, and the Jap is the most
aggressive and virile of all the aliens ; and while the
Imperial Government is making up its mind on the sub-
ject, and the M'Million is screwing up his shoddy courage
to the cutting-the-painter point, the “white Marys”
will be adding to the piebald population. In 10 years’
time the youthful alien who now trots about Cairns and
the plantations with lanky, bare leg's will have got a white
Mary of his own, or, perhaps, he will fancy the Australian
“ yaller-girl ” ; but, anyhow, a second generation of Aus-
tralian-born mongrels will be on the spot, and the whole
problem fearfully and wonderfully complicated. The
educational test must be grimly applied to the Undesirable
Immigrant, and the benevolent Federal Government had
better set aside a sum of money, and charter a few steamers
to carry back to the greasy, golden, pagoda-decked East
the Undesirable already here who cannot pass an educa-
tional test entitling him to trash cane in Australia. Such
benevolence would be a beautiful and touching example
to the wicked Boer not to ill-use his brother nigger in
the future.

More Freetrade “ Facts ” by G. K,eid.
The Federal Parliament, in the course of gather-
ing information on which to base its social and
fiscal legislation, has had a return prepared deal-
ing with the employment of children in the fac-
tories of the various States, so far as the informa-
tion is procurable. The two States wherein the
information is most complete are, as might be
expected, the two with the largest population—
N.S.W. and Victoria. And, incidentally, the
facts supplied furnish another commentary on the
utter unreliability of that bounding political
mountebank, George Reid. Some time ago,
when the Federal elections were in progress, the
strange, unscrupulous Foreign-trade howler went
round on what he called his Freetrade campaign.
In the course of his progress he visited Victoria,
and came back with a broken heart and greasy
tears dribbling down his waistcoat to tell the
people of New South Wales this story :
I pity the workers of Victoria when I go through the
factories and see in them, not the workers of Victoria,
but their children. I pity the industries of Victoria when
I see man after man thrown out into the street because
they have grown too big for them, and young children
brought in to take their place, AND WHEN I KNOW
THAT FOR EVERY MAN YOU FIND IN THESE
VICTORIAN FACTORIES YOU FIND HALF-A-DOZEN
CHILDREN.
And now it appears that the poor old inventor
might have spared himself his grief, and his
broken heart, and those oily tears, for he never
saw all those children toiling in Victoria’s pro-
tected factories at all. These are the actual
results at the end of 1899 (it appears that complete
returns up to end of 1900 are not yet available) as
published by the Federal Parliament: —
Employees in N.S.W. Factories.
Adults Children
Children in every in every Total
Adults, under 15. 10,000 10,000 employees,
employees, employees.
1898 .. 61,961 498 9,905 95 52,459
1899 .. 54,176 595 9,890 110 54,771
Employees in Victorian Factories.
1898 .. 54,851 951 9,830 170 55,802
1899.. 59,921 1,142 9,813 187 61,063
Of the adults in Victorian factories about the time
George Reid made his famous visitation, 43,899
were men —43,899 men to 1142 children of both
sexes, or nearly 39 men for every one child. And
during the preceding 12 months the men em-
ployed in Victorian factories had increased by
3291, and the children of both sexes had
increased by only 191. And a “child” in a
Victorian factory means someone over 13—
not, as in Freetrade England, someone over 10.
And George Reid came back weeping to tell how
there were six children for one man in the Vic-
torian factories, and how he was all broken up
under the middle-button of his waistcoat to see
man after man thrown out into the street, and
children put on in their places. Yet, when the
tariff discussion comes on, George Reid, the man
whose alleged facts always prove to be some such
invention as this, will doubtless pose as an indi-
vidual whose statements are at least moderately
reliable, and whose word is worth as much as that
of the average Chinese witness.

The Flag 1 .
The Flag is still a nation’s necessary emblem.
Descendants of sense-stimulated animals, most of
us still need a sense-impression to draw its keenest
emotion from a sentiment. To move the average
man, abstract must always be translated into
concrete. He is a doubting Thomas, never sure
till he has thrust his hand into the wounded
side; a Capuchin’s devotee, who understands
spiritual marvels only when he has handled a lock
of the saint’s hair, or gaped at a drop of his blood
in a bottle. To the highly-civilised man, nothing
that can be perceived by the senses is one-tenth so
inspiring as Space, in Silence. His imagination
can people vacancy with shapes, with sounds, far
transcending any product of mundane artifice.
But the average man is plain savage ; so we give
him flags to stimulate his vision of the Fatherland,
drums and trumpets to heat his blood to the pitch
of martial onset. Naturally, in the present
condition of things the flag proposed as
the future emblem of the Commonwealth is
vulgar and ill-fitting,—a staled rechauffe of the
British flag, with no artistic virtue, no national
significance. We have to understand and remem-
ber that the Commonwealth Constitution is still
for the most part a framework, a formula : that to
the height of its spirit we have not yet risen. It
represents an aspiration, a tendency, rather than
a fact. One could not expect otherwise. Minds
move slowly ; and Australia is still Britain’s little
boy. What more natural than that he should
accept his father’s cut-down garments,—lacking
the power tb protest, and only dimly realising his
will. That bastard flag is a true symbol of the
bastard state of Australian opinion, still in large
part biassed by British traditions, British customs,
still lacking many years to the sufficiency of man-
hood which will determine a path of its own.
The natural feeling of resentment at the indig-
nity is not shared by the multitude. Probably
seven in ten of Australians or British-Australians
are conscious of no offence in the monstrosity that
has been foisted upon them for a symbol. They
have wit enough to follow when led along
a higher path ; but they cannot see for them-
selves, and they have no leaders. The flag
represents the old generation, the old leaven.
But the new generations are growing; the new
leaven is working. The New Spirit is beginning
to be felt in Literature and in Art; even in
Politics. With the New Leaders will come a New
Flag.

Louis Eotlia, Alfred the Great, and the
Sydney “Daily Telegraph.”
Two leading articles which appeared side by side
in Sydney Telegraph of 21st Sept, embodied
whole volumes of that stereotyped subserviency
which passes with.a certain class as “patriotism.”
One was a bitter denunciation of the Boer com-
mander-in-chief, Louis Botha, for his criminal
conduct in maintaining the struggle for indepen-
dence against incredible odds, when his main
army had been defeated, and his capital was in
the hands of the enemy, and he didn’t seem to
have left any prospect of ultimate victory. The
other was a shout of joy and exultation over the
thousandth anniversary of the death of Alfred
the Great, because he kept up the struggle for
independence against enormous odds, even
when his capital was in the hands of the
enemy, and his army was defeated, and he
didn’t seem to have any semblance of a
chance left. One article demanded that stern
and brutal measures of terrorism should be
brought to bear on the man who blocked the
course of British empire-building; the other was
a cry of admiration about the man who blocked
the course of Danish empire-building—about
Alfred the Empire-wrecker of 1000 years ago.
One demanded that the Boer leader should no
longer be regarded as a belligerent, but as a mere
thief and brigand, because Britain has declared
his native land annexed, and because, having only
a thousand men left, he isn’t now so much a
general as the head of a band of outlaws. The
other was a paean on the squalid fugitive, who, ac-
cording to tradition, got down so low that he hadn’t
one follower left, and who is said to have hired
himself out to do menial offices for a Saxon “cocky”
ten centuries back, and whose country the Danes
had declared to be annexed, and whom the Danes
had made a rebel and an outlaw. True, Louis
Botha represents a race which seized the Trans-
vaal from its aboriginal owners ; but then Alfred
the Great represented a race of ruthless invaders,
who had stolen England from its previous inhabi-
tants and drenched it with blood in the stealing.
Botha is Alfred the Great over again, but
fighting against far heavier odds —the same strong,
patient man holding up what looks like a lost
cause—but he has never been charged, as Alfred
was (it is regarded as one of the most glorious
stories in the great Saxon’s history) with acting
the spy in the enemy’s camp, contrary to the
“ recognised rules of warfare.” Each was opposed
by an enemy who followed the policy of making
the Country a desert. Each professed a faith in
God which was almost pathetic under the circum-
stances. And beyond the fact that Alfred was
our ancestor and Louis Botha is somebody else’s
ancestor, it is difficult to see where the difference
comes in.

The Federated Kanaka.
Last Monday there was published an apparently
official forecast of the provisions of the
Federal Kanaka Restriction Bill. Its main features
are that the introduction of Kanaka labor is to be
permitted for three years longer, but every man
introducing Kanakas during that period must
return an equivalent number to their islands
before the three years expire. Therefore, at the
end of the three years there will be less full-
blooded islanders in Queensland than there were
at the beginning by reason of the deaths during
the interim. And after the three years the im-
portation is to cease, but the returning process is
to go on till the full-blooded Kanaka becomes
extinct, or nearly so. He will not become quite
extinct; a sediment of long-settled islanders will
remain ; but they will probably be too few to
amount to much. But all this time that the
Federal Government is temporising the half-caste
Kanaka—the Australian-born Polynesian who
can’t be sent back to his island because this is his
island —the product of the Polynesian savage
and the degraded white woman to whom he
isn’t married—will be increasing. And the
proof that the Kanaka is not indispensable
to the sugar industry will be postponed for
three years more. There will be three years more
for dismal prophecies about the impending ruin
of the sugar industry, and for underground
engineering for the repeal of the new law.
During that period, if the industry goes up
there will be the dismal cry everywhere :
“Look at the vastness and importance of the
great national undertaking that you are about
to destroy ”; and if it goes down there will be
another cry : “ Look how a great national
industry is already being destroyed by the mere
prospective loss of the labor on which it depends.”
Probably it would do the industry a vast amount
more good if the abolition of its Kanaka commenced
immediately ; if importation ceased at once, and
the return of the existing islander began now
instead of three years hence, the industry being
afforded sufficient Protection to enable it to com-
pete fairly, on a white labor basis, against
foreign rivals. Of late years, despite an abun-
dant supply of nigger-labor, it has been a fast-
decaying business. It seems to be dying even faster
than its nigger does. For the year ended June,
1899, Queensland manufactured 163,734 tons of
sugar; in 1900, 123,289 tons ; in 1901, 92,554 tons.
The export dropped from 129,233 tons in 1899 to
54,705 tons in 1901. The Queensland planter
is degenerating, it would seem, into a mere
nigger-driver. He has learned to rely on
his cheap nigger, and has never paid
much attention to good agricultural methods,
and up-to-date machinery. The white man always
tends to mental decay when he becomes a mere
driver of cheap, black men, and the Queensland
planter, sitting in lamentation among the accumu-
lating ruins of his nigger-labor industry, appears
to be a case in point. The business is already
falling to pieces with its nigger; and it is time to
try a new system, if only to see whether that will
not galvanise it into new life.

The Push-Loving Beak.
When push outrages became so numerous that
one or two Js.P. have been victims, and, in ac-
cordance with the law of averages, it is almost a
bishop’s turn next, the inept Fat person of the
community raises a shriek that authority shall be
given to use the lash. His reason always is that
“ existing laws have proved insufficient to stamp
out larrikin brutality,” If someone would only
try the experiment of enforcing the existing laws,
instead of allowing them to be a dead letter, then
Australia might be better able to decide as to their
sufficiency or insufficiency. Unpaid justices and
stipendiary magistrates and even salaried judges
are the persons chiefly responsible for the growth
of homicidal larrikinism. It is probably not true
that magistrates are systematically bribed or ter-
rorised by the organised pushes ; but it is difficult
to find another reasonable cause for the fond-
mother kind of attitude which they adopt towards
the misguided youth who mashes a citizen’s
head to pulp. For instance, at a Melbourne
court, the other day, a pushite was brought up to
be dealt with for having (in company) broken into
a Chinese laundry, brutally assaulted a Chinaman,
used obscene language, and resisted the police.
The night’s fun cost the fellow just £3 —most of it
for law costs and partial repairs to the laundry.
If he had broken into the place, and hadn’t nearly
murdered the inmate, it would have probably
been decided that he intended burglary,
and he might easily have got a sentence
of three years. But the fact that he simply
wanted to wreck things and nearly kill somebody
stamped him as a mere honest, commendable
pushite, and condoned his burglarious feat. So
long as violent assaults are regarded by
the law as not only lacking criminality, but
actually meritorious circumstances, to be
taken into account, like tho youth of the
prisoner and previous good character, in mitiga-
tion of punishment, push outrages will increase.
A few law-administrators like Judge Habt, who
has lately been pacifying a rowdy district of Cali-
fornia by sentences of 30 years gaol for murderous
and unprovoked assault would make a wondrous
addition to the security of our city streets. It
may not be necessary to go so far as that, but a
law fixing the minimum punishment for brutal
and unprovoked assault at two years would make
a great change in the aspect of things. At
present the offender may be sentenced to not
more than so much gaol, but he may get any
amount less. And, for some reason of his own,
the magistrate, in the exercise of his own indiscre-
tion, gives him so much less that the tariff for
something approaching murder has got down to
about 10s.

The Japanese Consul in Sydney was interviewed
last week on behalf of the Telegraph. Sample
of the brown gentleman’s insinuating way of
putting things follows :
The Japanese Government is willing to enter into any
suitable arrangement with the Commonwealth Govern-
ment for further limitation of Japanese immigration if
desired, but does not see any necessity for an Act which
would place the Japanese on the same footing as
Kanakas, Polynesians, and negroes. The Japanese think
that, by reason of their importance as a nation, they
should be placed on an equality with European nations.
The wily Jap. here sticks out conspicuously in
two directions. His Government is willing to
enter into any suitable arrangement—in other
words, it asserts its right to be consulted before its
subjects are excluded, and to give or withhold its
consent to their exclusion. But every State has
the right to exclude the subjects of other States
without consulting them unless there is some
agreement to the contrary, and Australia
has no such agreement with Japan. There-
fore Australia has every right to exclude the
Jap. without asking any Japanese authority to
enter into any suitable or unsuitable or partially
suitable arrangement on the subject. Further,
the Jap. gentleman wants his people placed on the
same level with Europeans ; in other words, he
doesn’t mind the Jap. being excluded if the
German, Dane and Scandinavian are excluded
also. As he knows perfectly well that these are
not going to be excluded, the artless Jap. gentle-
man offers an impossible way out of the difficulty,
and does it with an aspect of fairness which is
very beautiful to behold. His assurance that hi 3
Government doesn’t in the least mind letting us
do what we have a perfect right to do without
consulting it, provided we do it in a way that will
please his Government, and also do it under
certain terms and conditions that he knows are
wholly out of the question, is most interesting.
.
A proposal by O’Neill, M.L.A., to license com-
mission-agents of all sorts and sizes was igno-
miniously thrown out of Victoria’s Leg. Assembly.
O’Neill’s idea was a capital one, but he could
hardly expect it would catch-on in a House where
nearly 25 per cent, of the members hang out the
“ general commission agent ” plate. The time is
rotten-ripe for action in the direction of O’Neill’s
Bill. Nine-tenths of the men who carry about
with them a 3ft. by 2ft. brass shutter with all
kinds of agents scrawled over it are mere con-
fidence-tricksters in disguise. Hundreds of these
alleged men are making a good living out of un-
suspecting widows and old unmarried women, who
have no strong male relatives to batter the vampire.
One of the worst of the species is the financial
agent. This creature advertises in the news-
papers that he has money to lend. When applica-
tions are made, he asks for, and gets, a valuation
fee. The valuation may or may not be made, but,
in any case, no money is ever lent. Another Bill
which O’Neill has on the stocks is likely to go the
same way. Its purpose is to register and license
stock-and-share brokers. Already a howl is being
raised against the measure in certain quarters.
Useless parasites who creep about the. by ways
and gutter-ways of stock-exchanges, and who
think, because they humbug people by means of
scraps of paper called scrip, they are engaged in
mining development, are beginning to talk largely
about the injury which the Bill will do the great
mining industry. Some day, the people who earn
an honest living by doing some useful and honest
work will realise what a horde of financial vermin
they are carrying about, and then someone will
invent a new kind of guillotine for beheading six
agents at once.

Vic. Full Court sat heavily on the attempt of the
bigger employers in the fellmongering trade to
upset the Minimum-Wage Board’s decision. When
the first-appointed Board started, in March last, to
fix wages on a 48-hour basis, the “ masters,” with-
out waiting to find out what the wage would be,
unanimously quitted the Board, alleging an eight-
hour day would ruin them. As the employers
declined to nominate other representatives in their
places, the Governor-in-Council appointed five
persons, not then in the trade, but all experienced
fellmongers, and these, with the employees’ dele-
gates, fixed the wages. The appellants relied
chiefly on the objection that the Governor had no
power to appoint, as employers’ representatives,
persons not then in the trade, and, as the em-
ployers in the trade had decided not to act, they
thought they had thus found a way of closing up
the Wages Boards altogether. The Full Court
unanimously upset this impudent bluff, holding
that any person so appointed—even a Supreme
Court Judge—was legally a representative, and—
in this particular case—if such persons were not
capable of being fairly representative, that was the
employers’ own fault. Here endeth the first
lesson.

next war will be with Germany—over her aggres-
sive intentions towards South America.
