Opera House Rickards’ Variety Co.
Yerdi—and again Verdi. Another of this
extraordinary genius’s works was given for the
first time by the Williamson Opera Co. on Satur-
day night. “La Traviata’s ” skeleton is jointed
from Dumas’ book, “La Dame aux Camelias,”
and Verdi has embodied it in music and clothed
it in song. If every school-boy does not know
Dumas’ story, pretty well every adult does.
Camille, the heroine (Violetta she becomes in the
opera), belongs to a very ancient profession, and,
judging by the Arabian Nights splendor of her
home, finds it highly remunerative. From many
lovers she selects one, Alfred Germont (Dani) and
loves him with a great love, even to the verge of
selling her horses and carriages in order that
he may not have to contribute to the house-
keeping fund. Whilst Alfred is away try-
ing to stop the sacrifice, Germont senior
calls on the lady who wears camellias in her
hands and hair, and entreats her to set his
son free, as Mdlle. Germont’s matrimonial pro-
spects are being injured by the lad’s liaison.
Violetta abandons the lad for his good, and pro-
ceeds to die of consumption, accelerated by grief
and a new protector. Baron Dauphol (Signor
Salvarani) is the new lover. He and Alfred meet
at the house of a lady (Miss Boanas) who has the
same social status as Violetta, and later the pair
fight a duel. By-and-by the lovers are reconciled,
and Violetta dies in Alfred’s arms, surrounded by
friends and a doctor (Lombardi), who is (on Her
Majesty’s stage) a replica of King Charles, who
was beheaded.
* * * •
Madame Sapio, as Violetta, sang with soul
and enthusiasm, and made the heroine a graoeful,
winning frisker. Consumption, on the stage,
in a lace wrapper and a pretty bed, is a pic-
turesque disease, most valuable to novelists
and opera-builders, and Madame managed her
death as if she had invented the microbe that
Koch is trying to kill. Her “Won Swpete”
and sad “Mora la mia” in the scene with
old Germont were gems of dolorous music.
Signor Dani, minus his perky little moustache,
and with curls around his face, looked a fat,
spoilt boy of nineteen ; but he made an
adorable lover, and gave the drinking-
song with all the magnificence that good
drink deserves. In the last scene of all,
where his love is as a faded rose in a
dying hand, the tenor proved himself
tender, sympathetic, musically broken-
voiced. Miss Boanas made a beautiful
“other bad girl.” Bozzoli, as Germont
senior, gave the “Die Provenza” in
his finest baritone. Signorina Marchi
had little to do but charm the audience
with her magpie clothes and nun’s face.
_** * *
“Sweet and Twenty,” a light
comedy by Basil Hood, replaces “ Lady
Huntworth’s Experiment ” at Sydney
Royal to-day (Saturday). Brough has
learned by experience that the strong play
of Pinero or Jones, with a flavor of genius
in its story and of last night’s carouse in
its treatment, doesn’t pay as well as the
little trifle with a comic butler, and a
clergyman in the cupboard, and plenty
of cooking talk. So comedy will pro-
bably continue to verge on the farcical for
the jest of the season.
** * *
after the departure of Maggie
Moore, the usual dramatic company
flocked back to Sydney Lyceum and pro-
duced the “Orphan Heiress,” a drama
which ha 3 one bright touch of originality,
inasmuch as the adventurer and his
sister don’t turn out to be husband and
wife, or something in that line, in the
last act. When the plot begins with a
maundering old gentleman who hasn’t
changed his will, and whose child is lost, the
experienced playgoer knows that that old gentle-
man will have to die of heart-disease, or of suddenly
administered poison, or of gunshot wound in a
drawing-room chair, so as to let the will get a
chance to throw itself headlong into the drama
like an armed devil running amok. This time
the playwright chooses gunshot wound. Before
death occurs, the wife has come back from some
place unknown, and they discover that the child,
which each of them thought the other had,
has got lost between them. The wife left
her husband’s home merely because the
adventurer and his sister, having got into
the household in an indefinite sort of way,
made her life miserable by scoffing at her
before the servants.* She explains this to
the old gentleman on her return, and
he seems so reasonable about it that it is
extraordinary that she didn’t say it all before she
went, and thus save herself anguish and travelling
expenses. The child —a grown-up one—is dis-
covered by the usual vague means in the next act,
when the mother comes in to her rustic home to
die on the lawn ; after which incident the orphan
heiress is transferred to the keeping of the adven-
turer and his sister, who seem to have been left
guardians for no reason whatever. The adven-
turess pursues her in a glittering bodice and some-
times in a red and black costume, and the shrieks
of the heroine as she is chased about her own
house are loud. What the adventuress does to her
behind the scenes is not very ole. ?, but it
seems to be physical violenoe to a pronounced
degree. It xs understood that the terms of
the will and the English law on the subject
of wards make this unavoidable. About
this time her lover is beguiled into the strong
room of a bank, for reasons that are shrouded-in
mystery, but as a great many people rush into this
aot and fall into boxes, or rooms, or inside the
flooring, the matter is quite satisfactory from a
gallery point of view. What is supposed to be
the most thrilling episode occurs when the heroine
is inserted into the den of a Parisian circus tiger
that is mostly human being. The vision of the
lady who died on the rustic lawn appears at the
back of this beast, but he doesn’t take the slightest
notice. The orphan is finally saved by the funny
man, who has disguised himself as a bear in order
to rescue her in a comic and amusing manner.
The funny man, admirably played by Bert
Bailey, does a number of kind actions, dis-
guising himself as a policeman, or an animal,
or anything else that seems likely to disarm
suspicion, in order to get the heiress out of her
various troubles. The surprising things that a
stage-funny man will do for the best girl of a
casual acquaintance are noticeable in almost every
melodrama.
* * • *
Miss Ivy Gorrick is a nice-looking girl, and
though not particularly striking so far, does well
enough as a simple little heroine in a simple little
white dress. Miss Kathleen Duggan, as Lady
Ainsley, has no time to sing, and her acting is
much better than her melody. Bert Bailey has
one of those lightning-change parts that suit him.
Paris by night and the heroine’s rustic retreat
with a blue river near the front door, are both
pretty scenes, but the Cage of Death, with most
of the wild beasts'merely painted on canvas, is a
distinctly tame affair. The tiger that isn’t painted
does not give the long, lithe spring that he springs
with on the bills ; he merely hunches himself up
in a reticent manner and looks as if he would
prefer to hand in his resignation as a wild animal
and go out of the business altogether.
** * *
Mr. Henry Lee at Sydney Palace suggests
a very little story sent in to the editor with a very
long letter of explanation. Most of his imi-
tations are good, but they are prefaced
with an announcement by the imitator which
makes the imitation appear like a postage stamp
set in a two-ton frame of carved oak. With the
omission of his vainglorious speech Mr. Lee would
do better. Otherwise the very fine variety show
at the Palace is not open to any serious criticism
this week.

Capt. Edwards has. brought “ Our Navy ”
back to Sydney, and an orchestra, garbed in white,
like waiters in a restaurant, help on the represen-
tation of Jack’s career at Queen’s Hall this week.
As usual, the cloven hoof of the Royal Family
protrudes itself into the programme, as also a
MLLE CELINSKI.
Photo, by the Grouzelle Studios, Sydney,
certain amount of funeral. The performance,
more than on previous occasions, has a rather
Sunday-school entertainment flavor, and one
almost expects to have tea and bun handed
round in the interval. The great white,
whirling seas, however, are as capable
as ever, and when a cinernatographed Jack
climbs up somewhere into the drifting mist to furl
the foretopsail, and the wind whistles through his
hair per medium of the industrious man behind
the scenes, one forgets the Royal personages, and
also the funeral, and enjoys a few brief minutes of
peace.
** * *
At Sydney Tivoli, the most interesting per-
former is Professor De Wynne, juggler and
shadowgraphist, whose coin-spinning tricks are
clever, though the hard ball he flings in the air
shows a tendency to come down on the performer
with disastrous results. Afterwards De Wynne
waves the shadow of his hands over a white
cloth, and forthwith these members wreathe them-
selves into rabbits, alligators, a clergyman wagging
his head in a tall pulpit, and a jockey attempting
to win on a Randwick racecourse. Cecilia
Dubois sings a graceful French song or two,
and as she warbles, the kaleidoscope gives her]
every two lines or so, a magnificent new costume
without her taking the slightest trouble in the
matter. Sometimes her garb is a quaint design in
blue and rose oolour, sometimes her white dress
turns to yellow silk with trails of greenery for
trimming, and great red roses nestling at shoulder
and waist. There is no ocoasion, however, for
the kaleidoscope to send a large sunflower sprawl-
ing over her face. Zobedie still climbs stairs on
his hands, and Ted Box whistles well, though the
rest of his comedy business is quite ordinary, and
gives the audience the impression that it could be
the same kind of happy ox itself if it took the
trouble. It is, after all, a simple matter to put on
one’s hat while lying on the floor, should it seem
advisable to complete one’s toilet in this manner.
** * *
“ Cyprienne,” translated and adapted
from the French play “ Divorcons,” was pro-
duced at Sydney Criterion in aid of the Sydney
Hospital and German Benevolent Society, on.
Wednesday and Friday last week. It is an
interesting, up-to-date drama, wherein all the
ladies at Rheims are depicted as extremely
anxious for divorce, and waiting eagerly for a law
to be passed that will enable them to leave off
being honest women whenever they choose. The
clamor for divorce is very great in the drawing-
rooms of the aristocracy, and occasionally
the populace clamor outside as well. One
woman wants general divorce because it will
promote a circulation of husbands, and Frau
Buhrow, as Cyprienne, wants it because then she
can have her husband as a lover, and go to dubious
restaurants, and have the waiter believe that they
are not really married to each other—which, by
that time, will be the case. Certainly, the woman
who can find soul-excitement in dining out with
her own husband, even though he is disguised
in some other character, must be hard -up
for amusement. But when Cyprienne finds,
later on, that the circulation of husbands
means that her own old man will revolve round
with the others, and be liable to be annexed
by all the enterprising widows in Rheims,
she grows dubious about divorce. And finally,
when she recollects that the other lady
will have a right to drive in her carriage, and
sit in her boudoir, and alter the arrangement
thereof, she is quite broken up, and the
desire for divorce fades utterly. Frau Elsa
Buhrow, in a magnificent yellow robe and a tak-
ing broken accent, made many strong points, and
amongst a long cast of people of little importance,
Mr. A. B. Davies did well as the calm methodical
husband.
** * *
That the Empire and two other music halls
should do well in Sydney shows that the citizens
have an enduring affection for the serio-comic, the
artificial nigger, and the impossibly drunk person.
With much the same company as before, the
Empire continues to do good business.
*# * *
Mademoiselle Celinski will shortly intro-
duce “The Arcadian Nights” entertainment to
Sydney for the purpose of producing a novelty in
the world of art. The idea is to present the
masterpieces of great composers simultaneously
with the actual scenes which prompted the com-
positions. The scheme is daring and original,
and will no doubt excite much attention.
** * *
Up to Friday night, Wilson Barrett will
continue to bask in the limelight of his latest
character, at Her Majesty’s (Melb.). “The
Christian King” is neither a fine commercial
speculation, nor a dismal failure. The first two
acts promise a fairly good show, after which it
tails off into an exhibition of Barrett’s voracious
appetite for “fat.” If that massive, heavy-toned
veteran were a slim young ruler—the original
Alfred, by the way, was 22 years of age when he
started kinging the situations he dominates
would appear less unreal, but a perpetual Wilson
Barrett shining as a central star emphasises the
dramatic hollowness of his five-act undertaking.
The little bit of plot has no love interest
apart from Barrett. There are two girls,
Miss M'Carthy and Miss Latimer, and they both
of them adore the gentleman who plays the name-
part. They fear no other foe in shining armour
(besides, Barrett monopolises all the shining
armour in Engle-land, anyhow), but when He
comes along they surrender on the spot, and as
his intense Christianity confines him to one girl
(Miss Latimer), poor Lillah M‘Carthy is left to
gnash her teeth and rant hopelessly of her con-
suming fires. One brief squeeze from Barrett in
the first act is all she ever gets to make her
happy. Her attempt to poison the other girl
is a failure, and subsequently she retires to the
Danish camp to wait for her Saxon god with the
Roman nose. He comes along in due course and
occupies the middle of the stage whilst gammon-
ing to twang a dumb harp. Then the Danish
potentate, naturally objecting to the intrusion,
ties Barrett to a tree in the same old stage centre,
so that love-sick Lillah may heap adoration upon
him, and kiss his Apollonian brow. She is about to
die for Barrett, who all this time has been absorb-
ing limelight. The royal Dane bargains with the
lady to set her Alfred Barrett free in exchange
for her “sweet body.” Alfred rushes off to save
Engle-land, and his tempestuous girl take 3 the
precaution to commit suicide before resigning her
sweet body to its fate.
m • * *
Two or three improvements happened to
the drama during last week. The 20th century
shop-walker’s moustache which Barrett had origi-
nally worn in the Danish camp was laid aside as a
superfluous attraction. Also, the vision from on
High took a comparatively back seat, instead of
putting Barrett’s light out pro. tem. Never-
theless, there is room for an amount of recon-
struction in “ The Christian King,” and somebody
who possesses a livelier fancy and a more ancient
vocabulary than W.B. ought to undertake the
job. The last five nights of the season, com-
mencing on Saturday, will he given to four
revivals of tragedies that figured on Mr. Barrett’s
list when he brought his first co. to Melbourne.
On Saturday (28th) he plays Yirginius, a parental
Roman part which suits him very well. Then
Hamlet and Othello for one consecutive night
apiece. Finally, Claudian for two nights and a
matinee.

At the Bijou, “A Message from Mars”
will be delivered to Parker for the last time on
Friday. The pretty little comedy has paid its
way for five weeks, in spite of numerous weak
spots in the performance. Melbourne speaks of it
affectionately. But a stronger company might
have made the story more human and interest-
ing. Ross —who presents a cheerful, unaffected
Parker —is somewhat too small for the character in
every respeot. It shrinks to his size, just as
naturally as it would fit a 13st. person whose
selfishness and reformation looked fat and impor-
tant. And yet Ross, as Parker, gets nearer to the
truth of his stage identity than any other member
of the co. On Saturday they set up “Tom, Dick
and Harry,” with “A Highland Legacy” for
curtain-raiser.

“Riding to Win,” the remarkable Bland
Holt drama of wheel and woe, proceeds as usual
at the Royal. Some fine Saturday night it will
retire in favor of “ Hearts are Trumps,” but for
the present bikes are trumps, and the dawg in the
tank is an amusing joker.

the land.

COLUMN
Queensland’s railways, for the year ended June
30, according to the report of the Queensland Com-
missioner for Railways, yielded £258,955 over and above
working expenses. This means that they paid only
£1 6s. 3d. per cent, interest on the money borrowed for
their construction. The money costs about £4 per cent.,
so the Queensland railway deficit is about £552,135. This
is the biggest railway shortage in Australasia Yet the
good old drought can’t be held responsible for the fall.
The earnings for the year just ended were actually greater
than in 1898, and nearly as great as in 1899. Here are
some curious facts :
Representing
Gross Working Net on capital
Earnings. Expenses. Earnings. invested
in the lines.
£ £ £ £s. d.
June, 1896.. 1,085,494 644,362 441,132 212 7 p.c.
„ 1897.. 1,179,273 684,146 495,127 217 5 „
„ 1898.. 1,215,811 686,066 529,745 218 5 „
„ 1899.. 1,373,475 784,811 588,664 3 3 0 „
„ 1900.. 1,464,399 948,691 515,708 213 5 „
„ 1901.-1,316,936 1,057,981 258,955 1 6 3 „
There has been no increase in the mileage this year to
account for all these extra expenses—not one single mile.
And since 1896 the increase has only been from 2400 to
2801 miles—that is to say, less than 17 per cent, increase
in the amount of railway to be worked, and 64 per cent,
increase in the working expenses.
If the increased expenses ari:,e from paying better
wages or paying for repairs and improvements out of
revenue, instead of out of loans, they are not onlj' justi-
fiable, but commendable. But they probably arise in
great part from sheer bad management. At all events, it is
no use blaming the disaster on the drought, and assum-
ing that things must improve when the rain comes. The
Queensland lines, in the bad year just closed, have yielded
more per mile than they did in 1896, when the profits
were twice as large. Compared with the best year (1899),
the earnings have only dropped about £55,000, but ex-
penses have increased about £273,000. That expense
item is the whole cause of the trouble.

Westralia’s loan, it now appears, f§ ' for
£l,soo,ooo—not £1,000,000, as stated in the early cable-
grams. The only difference is that this makes nearly
£6,300,000 added to the public debt of the Commonwealth
inside a few weeks (N.S.W., £4,000,000 ; Westralia,
£1,500,000; Tasmania, £200,000; and Queensland £528,000
of deficit coverted into permanent debt), instead of
£5,800,000, or nearly £ll3s. of extra debt for each inhabi-
tant. Things in this country are at times even more
sudden than they look.
• * * *
Camperdown, one of the 43 pockethandkerchief-
municipalities of Sydney, was recently haled before the
law courts by its unpaid debenture-holders, who applied
for the appointment of a receiver. Then things took an
unusual turn. The law specifies that every municipal
council which intends to borrow must give 14 days’ notice
of its intention, and also publish, for the information of
the ratepayers, particulars of the uses to which the money
is to be put. In this case it was merely specified that the
money was for “ permanent improvements,” and no par-
ticulars were given, so the Court held that the debentures
were bad. This is rough on the lenders of the money, and
the slovenly ways of the ridiculous penny-alderman are
calculated to bring municipal securities into ill-odour.
Further, it is regrettable to see that the habit of going
broke is spreading among the Sydney municipalities.
If Sydney was all one municipality it would be large
enough and sufficiently dignified to make some serious
effort to avoid disasters like this, but when the city is
cut up into 43 municipalities they are mean little con-
cerns, and the breakage of one of them makes hardly more
excitement than that of a suburban ham-and-beef shop
or the suspension of an apple-stall. Camperdown,
apparently, drifted into trouble from the usual cause.
The rates were Is. 4d. in the £and the N.S.W. Statistical
Register shows this sort of progress in recent years :
Revenue. Expenditure. Deficit.
1897 £2868 £3431 £563
1898 3151 3461 310
1899 3048 3656 608
The weary aldermen never made any apparent attempt to
put things straight, but kept on with the old rate of Is.
4d. in the £, and let things slide. At February, ’99 (the
latest date covered by Coghlan’s figures) the liabilities
were £24,007, of which £17,000 was borrowed on deben-
tures at about 6 or 6 j per cent., and £6OOO was an unse-
cured overdraft at 8 per cent. There is something very
miserable and helpless about this kind of drifting process,
but most of the Sydney municipalities plod on after much
the same indefinite fashion. It wasn’t the aldermen, by-
the-way, who entered the defence that the debentures
were bad. They told the court to fix things how it
pleased, but the bank, which reckoned if the debenture-
holders were paid its overdraft would be badly left, did
all the fighting.

The most notable feature is the serious increase

Including the dividend, £6O 3s. 6d. per cent, of the total
income went, during the year to 31st August, 1901, for
purposes other than assurance, which is a very heavy
rate, and the very worst on the co’s record. By
reason of this, and the amount written off goodwill,
the assurance fund has decreased considerably. Tne
People’s Prudential has just held its first quinquennial
investigation. It is a concern which does many small
and complicated varieties of assurance, but the actuary
cheerfully cut the knot by assuming that it wasn t
necessary to make any reserves whatever against
the liability for accident insurance, compensation for
temporary disablement, the medical attendance benefits,
and the temporary life assurance afforded to members
children. Under these circumstances, it_was decided that
the present value of all policy-liabilities is £8266, so there
is an apparent surplus of £349.
There are no separate accounts for ordinary
assurance, industrial twopence a-week assurance, and the
promiscuous accident and medical benefit and midwifery
business. The probabilities are, however, that the
latter, which seems to be only another branch Of the
great art of sweating the medical profession and of
hiring a doctor cheaply and sub-letting him at a large
profit, does more than anything else to keep the concern
going. There are so many small things mixed up with
life assurance in the People’s Prudential that it wouldn t
make much difference if an undertaker’s shop and a
ham-and-beef emporium were added, to round matters
off.

The Cascade Brewery 00. had, until lately, a nominal
reserve fund -of £16,600, but as this was more than bal-
anced by a huge paper item, “ Goodwill, £25,000," there
was really a large deficit. Six months ago the co. wisely
applied the whole reserve fund to reduce goodwill to
£B5OO, and decided to write off the balance by half-yearly
instalments. This paper item now stands at £7600, less
£5B undivided profit, reducing the deficit in the assets to
£7442. If the present increase in the profits can be
maintained it should be possible to completely shift this
burden in about years.

Marshall’s Paddington Brewery (Limited)
Sydney, makes a reasonably good showing, yet by reason
of the movement tor the abolition of “ tied ’’-hotels, this
is a parlous time for breweries. When that abolition
comes off, then the brewery whose beer pleases the public
best will have better times, and the other brewery will
have worse times, and as no one knows which brewery
his brewery may be, the public taste being unaccount-
able, it is a dangerous time to buy beer-shares. Recent
accounts of Marshall’s co. have been as follows :
Net Nominal
Profits. Dividends. Reserves.
July, 1896 £6,024
„ 1897 £3533 6 per cent. =£l7Bl 7,776
„ 1898 3652 6 „ „ 1781 9,647
„ 1899 5147 6 „ „ 1781 13,013
„ 1900 5958 6 „ „ 1789 17,182
„ 1901 5970 6 „ „ 1789 21,369
The situation looks promising, barring the dark suspicion
that hangs over the prospects of all breweries just now,
when a practice which has brought much grist to the mill
is in serious danger of abolition. As with many other in-
stitutions, by the way, the largest item in the accounts
of Marshall’s Brewery is “ Buildings, Land, Plant, Good-
will, and Furniture, £56,589.” How much the genuine
reserves would be after the wholly paper-item of goodwill
was deducted it is impossible to say, for the amount of
that item doesn’t appear. This very common system of
including in the assets an imaginary item of unknown
size makes a balance-sheet a humorous conundrum rather
than anything else, and the reserves shown are purely
nominal, inasmuch as they are subject to the deduction
of an unknown amount, which will leave another un-
known amount.

It is difficult to say exactly whether even this very

£31,523.” How much of this is goodwill doesn’t appear,
but if the amount is (say) £2OOO, then there is no real

sales and advertisements appear to be decreasing, but by
steadily cutting down the expenses, or the allowance
made for depreciation, or both, the net profits show an
improvement.

The co. is making profits in a small way, but at the same

against them, seeing that they were acquired in the boom

The business is growing, but apparently any extra returns

earnings wouldn’t stop the increase, for the Mutual Store
made only £4510 in the last year, and the liability grew
by over £BOO0 —it looks as if something serious would
happen. The whole trouble appears to lie in the fact that
it doesn’t pay to have £llO,OOO worth of buildings in
which to hold £79,000 worth of goods, almost all bought
with borrowed money.

J. Robertson: It isn’t the best co., but it is

earthly enterprise—war, revolution, national bankruptcy,
general resurrection, and the like.

As here anticipated, the specifications show
that the “new Broken Hill sulphide process ” leaves the
biggest loss in sulphide treatment untouched. This
biggest loss is in concentration—a loss of much over 50
per cent, of the metals. To make the concentrates more
suitable for smelting it is usual to roast the ores and then
subject them to a current of air to complete the oxida-
tion ; in this new process, by mixing the ore or concen-
trates with gypsum or calcium sulphate, it is claimed
that the complete desulphurisation of the sulphides and
the cineration of the ore (things which are necessary
before smelting) are completed without any prelimi-
nary roasting. The independent laboratory tests
made by the B.H. Prop, having been satisfactory,
an experimental plant will be erected on the mine.
If it succeeds the process will be worked on a
royalty. Opposition to the granting of the patent on the
part of the patentees of another roast reaction process is,
however, mentioned. And Greenway, general manager of
Block'll, has filed application for a patent for a somewhat
similar process.
» • * •
The British, which had to shut down more for
want of ore than through the lead slump, has picked up
the central ore-body at 500 ft. —excellent stuff, so far as
tested. Now we shall see whether the co. will make its
common mistake of rushing at an insufficiently developed
ore vein, or whether, wiser from experience, it will
thoroughly open out before re-starting the mill.
* • * *
Partly on an improvement in the lead market,
but more upon certain improvements in the processes for
treating the sulphides about which there has been a great
deal of mystery and humbug, silver shares have had a
little recovery. The metal-market reaction was bound to
come, though perhaps not to stay ; the other, for present
purposes, is the more important. But it is, for all that,
too much to call it a new sulphide process. A new sul-
phide process ought to be a process for treating the sul-
phides ; this is only a process for getting sulphide
concentrates ready for smelting. That would not be so
important a distinction if the concentrates contained a
fair percentage of the metals. But they don’t; they
usually contain 70 per cent, of the lead, 45 per cent, of
the silver, and 10 per cent, of the zinc—and the zinc is of
less than no value at all there. This new process will not
improve this extraction; the loss of a-third of the
lead and from a-half to two-thirds of the silver
and all the zinc will go on as hitherto. What it
will do, if it does on a big scale what it has done on a
small, is to allow more of the metal in the concentrates
to be smelted out and at a much reduced cost —this
reduction (with the proviso just mentioned kept in view)
would probably mean from £85,000 to £lOO,OOO a year to
the B.H. Prop, alone. Broken Hill silver-lead ores are,
for the most part, of two classes —oxidised and sulphide.
Nearly all the former can be smelted in the common
water-jacket furnace ; that is the sort of stuff the co’s.
made their fat divs. from even when they knew hardly
anything of mining, &c., and when costs were prepos-
terously high. Generally the ore was not very rich;
but it was easily mined and cheaply reduced—re-
quired no crushing, no concentrating, no prelimi-
nary roasting ; ran through the furnaces rapidly,
and so cost little for coke, coal, and attendance,
items which now mop up most of the old profits. On the
other hand the sulphides (which are all the most of the
mines have left) cannot be smelted ; their zinc will not
permit it. They might be roasted before smelting ; but
that is very costly and wasteful, owing to their silicates.
The present proposal is to roast the concentrates (and
Erobably also a portion of the slimes, which are now little
etter than a waste product of concentration), employing—
this is the new part—gypsum as a flux, and subsequently
smelting the roasted mass. Sulphuric gas is driven off in
the roasting, and the lead, hitherto in the form of sulphide,
becomes mostly oxide and sulphate, which are good
smelting ores. Though at the time of writing not dis-
closed, this is understood to be the process. A small
experimental plant will probably first be erected
at the B.H. Prop, mine; but as there is much gypsum
near the S. Australian coast the operation, if it should
succeed, will soon be transferred to Pt. Pirie. The pro-
cess is the discovery of Carmichael, B.H. Prop, experi-
mental chemist, and Bradford, of the same co.’s metal-
lurgical staff—the former with a Scotch, and the latter
with an Australian training.
» * * •
J. H. Curie, the mining commissioner to Lon-
don Economist, sums up the leading Westralian mines.
Investment in them he does not recommend, because
intrigue rules the market. Those of Kalgoorlie are not
improving in depth, as is shown by developments in the
Great Boulder and Boulder Perseverance, the latter’s
700 ft. level being admittedly the poorest, although it has
opened a lot of payable ore above it. The mines looking
much worse than when Curie was there some years ago
are the Associated, Lake View, Brownhill, and Kalgurli.
He previously described the Associated as “ the greatest
mine in Western Australia, if not in the world.” Evi-
dently he was grossly deceived, and this statement has
led writer to think that Curie, who is a very
able mining scribe, is a better examiner of men
than he is of mines, and that he arrives at his
conclusions from a consensus of opinion, and is
not solely guided by his own judgment. If he
had thoroughly examined the Associated on his
first visit some years ago, and was capable of arriving at
a correct estimate of its value, he would have known that
the prospects then were infinitely less than they are to-
day. Curie made probably the biggest mistake of his
life in setting up the Associated as Westralia’s biggest
mine ; and this scribe is almost satisfied that he has
made another, though smaller, mistake in his last article.
At the time he called it a great mine it was being robbed of
its patchy richness, the eyes picked out of it, its develop-
ment shamefully neglected. Its plant, that cost nearly
half-a-million, was an abortion ; in fact, the mine was run
to earth. Eighteen months ago its management was
handed over to Hewitson, and it has taken him all that
time to get the show in order ; it will take another three
or four months to put the plant in shape. An enormous
amount of development has been done. Big ore-bodies
have been disclosed, and it is almost certain that when
the plant has been completely re-modelled the Associated
will turn out for a long time to come from 6000 to 8000 oz.
a month. The profit will be considerable, although it will
not be enough to warrant the present price of the shares,
but it will in all human probability be greater, and the
mine will be more permanent, than Curie’s latest article
indicates.
Mr. Curie is absolutely correct in re the Brownhill,
but it was always suspected that that mine was working
a flat run of gold which would pass out at a comparatively
small depth. Its best ore is mined near the N. Associated
boundary, and it would not have had these reserves had
it not acquired the Cygnet lease. The purchase of that
lease was another proof that the ore was passing out of
the ground. Other leases have been acquired, but as they
were side-on to the original property, nothing has been
found in them, although a statement was lately made
that a new ore-body had been picked up in one of them.
So many little pipes and pockets of ore have been
found in different parts of this mine that the
chances of hitting any permanent ore-body outside
of the original are very remote. The Kalgurli looked
remarkably well in the oxidised zone, and the tellurides
came in at a comparatively shallow depth. At one time
a part of the mine glistened with tellurides like a
jeweller’s shop with diamonds. It was not long, how-
ever, before it was established beyond doubt that the ore
body was limited in extent, that while it was of great
width in places it had no length anywhere. With depth
the width has become less and no greater length has been
proved. It has been crushing for 18 months, producing
an average of about2oooozs. permonth.andhaspaidnodiv.
It is increasing its plant, and no doubt for a time will
increase its gold production. It is not likely to ever
become a steady div. payer, and if it were owned in Aus-
tralia instead of in London it would not have half its
market valuation.
Of the Lake View, manager M'Kinnon reported
over a year ago that then there were only 70,000 tons of
ore in sight. Fully that quantity has been milled since,
so it must be now very short of ore, or a great quantity
has been developed since. Of the latter there is not the
slightest doubt, but it is impossible even to imagine that
the developments have been such as to warrant the pre-
parations being made to deal with 12,000 tons a month.
Remarkable developments of new ore bodies will have
to take place to prevent another collapse in Lake
Views. The mine is drifting to that end. The
new chairman, O. D. Rose, at last annual meeting, in
speaking of quarterly divs. of ss. and a bonus distribu-
tion of the balance at the end of each year, said, “ Unless
something unforeseen occurs.” He possibly had the in-
evitable collapse in his mind’s eye, in making that reser-
vation.
Curie incidentally refers to the number of
mines working on the Kalgoorlie field having no prospects
whatever. He is also right there. The most appalling
feature of Kalgoorlie field is the great number of mines
working without hope of reward. Water is their only
excuse for working at all; to work them as gold-mines is
to waste money by a false pretence. Curie says of
the Great Fingall that it is likely to become
one of the great gold-mines of the world; that
shares, which were then nearly £lO, would probably see
£l5, and would be a fair investment at that price. Of
the market generally, he thought it had reached its lowest
ebb, and, as he wrote some seven weeks ago, he was
right, for at that time Westralians, almost without excep-
tion, were lower than they are to-day. He anticipated
the early improvement which has taken place, but de-
clared that it would be only temporary, and strongly
advised people to take advantage of the opportunity to
clear out of stocks where the prices are not warranted by
dividends or future prospects.
Present writer thinks it not unlikely that the
present good prices will be kept up for the next two
months, and it is probable a further improvement will
occur during that time. There is no possible chance of
their ever being carried to the figures they once reached
—Lake Views over £2O, Boulders 405., Perseverance £l7,
Kalgurlis £l2, and others in proportion ; but it may be
regarded as a certainty that, in the next slump, which
will come as surely as night follows day, they will be car-
ried to prices lower than they have ever reached, and
this may have the effect of placing them on a fair mining
dividend-paying basis, where, up to date, they have
never been.
« » • *
Chillagoe Railway and Mines: “Main Shaft”
obviously has no knowledge of mining, or he would not
have made so many absurd statements ; consequently,
others that cannot be contradicted are largely discounted
and carry no conviction. “I am in a position to state
that there is no such thing as an immediate scarcity of
ore to be considered.” The co. has been working these
mines for three years, and it was alleged almost at the
start that there were half-a-million tons of ore available.
Now we have the consoling fact that there is no “ imme-
diate scarcity of ore.” Writer wonders whether there
will be any scarcity of ore before the furnaces have been
working three years ! “ Stewart’s estimates of tonnage
are not attacked—his values are.” This is a distinction
without a difference, if you like. How can one attack
values without affecting tonnage? “The railway is a
fine piece of work ; should pay profits on public traffic
alone, excluding the proprietors’ traffic. The Ohillagoe
line as a profit-maker will be a bigger concern, and will
show greater profits per mile, than the Silverton Tramway
Oo.”
The equivalent to the first part of this state-
ment would be that the Mt. Lyell railway would pay’ if
the mine were shut down. To say that a railway
that is just finished will be a bigger profit-maker
than one which has been running, and making
profits, and declaring huge divs. for many years,
is taking on a big contract. In fact, it is all
prophet. “Coke and mine supplies will have only
a third of the haulage Broken Hill had to pay.” How
silent re the fact that one railway has a route as flat as a
billiard-table almost from starting point to terminus ;
while the Govt, section of the other was the most ex-
pensive railway ever built in Australasia, and haulage
is the most expensive per mile of any railway in
the Commonwealth. Of course the mining developments
are wrong. They always are when ore becomes scarce.
“ The present management sank big shafts off the lodes,
and generally behind the lodes, when, for all purposes of
prospecting, the obvious course was to get under the ore
already exposed.” Big, permanent shafts are invariably
sunk off the lodes. To sink a main-shaft in the lode
means either to sacrifice a large quantity of ore, or the
shaft itself sooner or later. “ For all purposes of pros-
pecting.” That conveys the impression that these groups
of mines—which were formed into a co. of a million, and
have spent about half-a-million in the building of a rail-
way—should have done their prospecting before they
started on the other bigger and more expensive works.
Of the Mungana 00. “Mainshaft” details
developments, and, if his measurements and figures in
this instance are reliable, they go to prove that the best
group of the lot has been parted with. It was given out
last week that Danvers Power’s report would fully con-
firm Stewart’s estimate of tonnage and value —and if this
proved correct some resignations would follow. Also
rumored that Mr. Weinberg, the general manager, had re-
signed, and shares took a 3s. 6d. jump. It is now officially
stated that it will be another month before Danvers Power’s
report is ready, and that Weinberg is only leaving for a
short holiday. The smelting has started ; the first 500 tons
gave an average of 8 per cent, copper, and the manager
says that this average will be approximately maintained.
Writer is informed by a much greater mining authority
than “ Mainshaft ” that the six furnaces could be put in
blast almost immediately if it were deemed advisable ;
that the discrepancy between promise and performance
of Chillagoe is most glaring ; and that, while (after three
years’ working) there is plenty of ore at grass to start the
she furnaces, it would not be long before they would have
to stop for the want of it.
The market too clearly indicates that that is the
real position, or why is it that this co., which had a
market-valuation of nearly two-and-a-half millions long
ere either the railway or the smelters were finished,
should now have a valuation of little more than half a
million when both are running? There is but one pos-
sible answer —that the mines are not what the public
were once led to believe. When the newspapers were all
barracking for this inflated concern, the writer, at any
rate, sounded many a note of warning. The public were
carried away by the Mt. Lyell, Broken Hill, and other
mining magnates, who took up this gigantic scheme, and
could not believe it possible that a £IO,OOO a-year manager
could make a mistake. That he has made one is now
only too clear, and to-day the position is even more
appalling than the public have any idea of; the
ravings of “ Mainshaft ” will not alter it. This is how
things stand : The co. has a tremendous debenture-debt,
secured on the whole of its property as well as on reserved
shares. There is no possibility of the latter being
accepted when they are far below par, or when the whole
property can be collared for the debt. In addition to
this, it recently borrowed £50,000, and has no means by
way of shares to repay it. It will probably soon need to
borrow another £50,000, and where _ it is coming
from is hard to foresee. It is wasting money and
time by paying fat fees for examinations that can
do no possible good. It has a smelting plant, the best in
Australia, in which all the improvements of Mt. Lyell
have been copied, and all the defects remedied, with
which to prove values in a practical way instead of assay-
ing in a laboratory, which is always surrounded with
doubt.
One of two endings is inevitable —reconstruction
or liquidation. The latter would mean that this gigantic
concern, in which so much hope was centred and money
sunk, would pass away to the debenture-holders. No
doubt reconstruction will be determined on, and that
will mean shareholders taking upon themselves a further
liability. Big men with big money may make mines big,
but they cannot create them. At the very inception of
this scheme these big men were advised, by a
copper-mining man of world-wide experience, to spend
£50,000 to £IOO,OOO in locating and proving the extent
and value of the ore-bodies. No attempt had ever been
made to establish the depth of the ore, and the precarious
and treacherous character of the country in which these
lateral surface ore-bodies and veins exist was often re-
ferred to. But the Chillagoe authorities rushed headlong
into an expenditure that has never been equalled in Aus-
tralia, and when the time has at last arrived to prove
what the undertaking is really worth, the market,
which seldom makes a mistake, says “ something only
equal to the cost of the line,” and the barracker, “ Main-
shaft,” says there is no immediate scarcity of ore.

The Hecla mine, Lake Superior, U.S.A., have
recently given an order for 80 Wilfley concentrators in
one lot. They have decided, after trying every known
concentrating machine, that the Wilfley table is by far
the best. The Wilfley' concentrating machines continue
to gain in favor in these States. The sole manufacturers,
The Austral Otis Co., South Melbourne, inform us they
are kept fully employed in turning out these machines,
and have sold a very large number. *

If further proof is needed that Luhrig Vanners
are the best and most economical machines for dressing
fines and slimes, it will be found in the fact that the Tas-
mania gold mine, Beaconsfield, Tasmania, has decided to
increase the number of these machines in the extension
of the present mill and reduction works. When com-
pleted it will have over 100 head of stampers at work, and
60 Luhrig Vanners, including the additional 24 of the
large type. Before finally adopting plans the Tasmania
board sent its manager to inspect the chief reduction
works in other States. Messrs. W. and J. Lempriere, of
Melbourne, are the Sole Agents for the Luhrig appli-
ances.*

LEROY
LIQUEUR
BRANDY
It is a rather regrettable fact that Bishop
Barlow attended the last, instead of the first, per-
formance of “ A Message from Mars,” when that
cheery little comedy was played in Townsville,
last April. For, as the advertisement “dodger”
stateth betwixt the leaves of the “Mars” pro-
gramme, the Bishop said to himself next morning,
said he—“lf I had seen the play on Saturday
night I would not have hesitated to introduce a
commendation —a strong commendation—of it
into a sermon. I was very pleased, indeed, with
it all, and very glad to have been able to go.” He
would not have hesitated —mark the word, dear
friends! —to commend the entertainment in his
sermon. Brave, “broad-minded” Bishop! He
must be the original Rev. Barlow who was pleased
to approve of the stage-ladies in “ The Vicar of
Bray.”
The Bishop’s views re “ The Message ” remind
present scribe of an incident that befell him on
the second night of the original ‘‘Sign of the
Cross” production at Melb. Princess’s. He
sat behind a godly theatre-party in the d.c.—a
party numbering 16 believers in all—ls of them
young men, with incipient Nonconformist beards,
and the 16th a patriarchal person, to whom they
seemed to sit in the capacity of body-guard. At
the end of the first act, when they had finished
applauding with all their hearts, and hands, and
souls, .and feet, the aged dissipationist leaned for-
ward and caught the eye of the sandy-haired cap-
tain of the guard, who occupied the end seat.
And, saith the shining old light to that early
Christian —“I am glad you brought me, Emanuel,
lam glad you brought me.” To which his young
admirer replied—“ I told you I had no hesitation
in asking you,to join our little party.”
* * * ♦
“ Puyyaka ” joins in the controversy :
“A Father in Israel” (8., 14/9/’01) may be right in his
strictures upon the modern Cissie-nurse, but he has
gott.n a curious notion of the luxuriance of medical mid-
wifery-fees. In Israel they may run to £lO 10s., as stated,
but the Australian medico is lucky if he gets £3 3s. for a
difficult case, with subsequent daily visits for nine days
included. Present writer, indeed, knows of many cases
where a guinea has been taken, and of one where lodge-
pressure has sent fees down to 17s. 6d. Of course, the
medico in question was “foolish,” but he, too, was an
extensive father, and six hungry boys mean much. One
may fix the average medical cost of youngest Australia at
£2 25., and the Semitic Parent’s estimate of £2l for
clothes, drugs, and appliances is also too high, except
for No. 1, because the things needful will serve for a good
many succeeding numerals, and £3 would well cover
renewals in each instance. Thus, allowing nurse’s fees—
of which more anon—at the “ Father’s ” £lO 10s. esti-
mate, and incidental convalescence expenses at £2 25.,
we have as the cost of the incipient citizen’s advent (if
he is the second or third incipient citizen in the family) a
total of £l7 145., instead of £50 —and in the present state
of the birth-rate he’s extremely cheap at the price. But
the great bulk of middle-class families do not
engage a doctor at all. They depend upon a
local “wise-woman” of the variety considered by
“ F- in I.” to be of necessity skilled by virtue of personal
maternity. The victims of accidents are not looked upon
after recovery as being of necessity expert surgeons, yet
this “motherly” fallacy always holds. If all is well, the
“experienced matron ” gets the credit. If it isn’t, she
hangs on to the last in the hope of “ things coming right,”
and only sends for the doctor when the victim’s life is in
serious danger, thus shifting to his shoulders the respon-
sibilities incurred by her own ignorance. Should he
succeed in averting serious consequences, the “motherly ”
one again takes charge and follows such of his directions
as she thinks fit. Present scribe has no liking for Nurse
Oissie and her kind, but she has a great superiority over
the irresponsible Gamp-party with the grubby nails, in
that she generally knows enough to send for medical
assistance in time, and has a thorough regard for cleanli-
ness. Be she ever so taken up with the set of her cap,
she knows something about antiseptics. The “ experi-
enced ” fraud may be “ able to manage a baby ” (generally
by filling it up with narcotics), and even to cook, but
neither is of much use as a prophylactic against
septicaemia. If the allegedly “trained nurse” would
stick to her plain duty she would be useful, but, although
the thirst for domestic admiration—mostly male—renders
her dangerous, she is never quite so dangerous as the
average comparatively unclean person with the “ personal
experience.”
Now, as to fees. The ordinary nurse’s charge is certainly
too high. A guinea per week and help is ample, unless
the nurse possesses special qualifications as to invalid-
cookery, &c. The real nurse is free from airs, and will, if
need be, cook and sew. She is priceless—but very rare !
Having said which, present writer will seize his bag and
go off to perform an intricate and risky obstetric opera-
tion under chloroform for the usual fee of £3 3s. Would
that he were in glorious Israel 1

If “ A Father in Israel ” shifts his camp to our district

with a shilling or two knocked off for cab-fare when the
happy father is too absent-minded to defray midnight
travelling expenses. So the new baby’s first month in an
ordinary middle-class suburb doesn’t cost its Scotch
parent more than £lO, all told ; and if an honest middle-
class man isn’t prepared to squander that amount on his

The brewer who owns “tied” hotels
always insists that his enslaved publican shall buy
not only beer from him, but also wines and spirits,
and often cigars and aerated waters as well. As
regards the wineS and spirits, a merchant in that
way of business gave this significant item of evi-
dence before the N.S.W. “Tied’’-House Com-
mittee :
Several brewers bought quantities of low-grade liquors
from his firm. They only imported that quality to supply
brewers. So far as his experience went, the brewers
bought only the low grades of wines and spirits.
Pity that some tobacconists were not also called
to report as to whether they had to import a
specially infernal variety of penny cigar to supply
tied houses with. Anybody who has wasted his
substance on a cigar in the ordinary second-class
Sydney pub. would naturally assume that it was a
special horror, made for a special and nefarious
purpose that wouldn’t bear daylight inspection.
And these low-grade spirits, specially imported
for the “tied’’-hotel trade, are charged to the
luckless publican at quite the ordinary price,
or more than the ordinary price of good
spirits. No wonder it is difficult in the
Sydney suburbs to get a drink that doesn’t taste
as if doormats and dead men’s bones had been
soaked in it, or a cigar that doesn’t burn sideways
with a black knob like a lump of coal in the
centre, and go to rags before it is half-consumed.
Also some more “ tied ’’-hotel evidence as
to where the adulterated beer comes from :
Dennis Jones, licensee of the Prince Alfred Hotel,
William-street, said he entered his house at a price of
£5OO, and paid £l5O. His was a “ tied’’-house, and he
had been supplied with bad beer. In one week he had to
send back to the brewery four barrels. His trade had
suffered, especially since his prosecution for selling beer
containing salicylic acid.
The Chairman : Do you swear that you never put the
salicylic acid in the beer ?—I swear it on my oath.
You sold it just as it came from the brewery ?—Yes.
When the Customs officer was in my bar I challenged him
to come down into the cellar and test several barrels of
beer there that were unbroken, to see whether they also
had any salicylic acid in them. The Customs officer re-
fused to do that.
Witness said he went to the brewery and complained of
the quality of the beer, declaring that if he did not get a
better quality, he would have to buy other beer to save
his custom. The manager threatened that if witness did
that he would be dealt with. Witness then offered to
pay off his debt to the firm in 24 minutes if he was freed
from the bond. The manager was willing to take the
money, but refused to release him from the bond.

Rumored that the shell of the Red Rover,
wrecked nearly 60 years ago, has been uncovered
by easterly gales on the Moruya coast. The news
should bring a thrill to Sydney hearts, for the Red
Rover carried to Port Jackson many a bouncing
convict lass who was fated to assist in the foun-
dation of a First Family—or eke a second or third
one.
* » • •
Deadly outrages committed by “pushes”
in Melbourne owe their origin to the in-
sanely-lenient view taken by magistrates towards
against the person, as distinct fiom
offences against property. North Melb. bench
recently fined one of the Crutchy Push 2s. Cd. for
hitting a passer-by in the mouth with a crutch,
and then a vagrant was fined 20s. or 14 days for
being found sleeping in an unoccupied house !
North Melb. bench is not open to the charge of
corruption as others are, but it is one of the very
worst for the administration of justice in Victoria.
“ Pushes ” will never be suppressed by an honorary
magistracy.
South Australian population figures for
1900, lately got together, are most gloomy, girths,
with one exception, are the fewest since ’77 ; the
birth-rate per 1000 the lowest, with one exception,
for 40 years. The “ illegitimate " birth-rate was
far the highest on record, more than doubling the
mean of a few years back. By move-
ments of population the State
lost nearly 500. The marriage
rate per 1000 has gone down
from 8-87 in 1882 to 6’37, which
is lower than that of any
other State or first class
European country; and,
except for Maori land

est. Last year’s Wes-

must be almost
a record, though

The evil-minded female is not confined to
Australia. A girl, the other day, appeared at a
M.L. magistrate’s court with the old tale of out-
raged virtue. She was a domestic, and the burden
of her tale was that she was never previously inti-
mate with the man, but on the occasion complained
of he asked her to go into the orchard with him.
She did not do this, but he went first and she
followed, and “ was ruined.” The remarkable
feature of the girl’s statement was that “she was
sure it was him because she marked the date on
the almanac!” Case dismissed. But a young
woman of such business-like habits is quite lost as
a domestic servant. She should be a book-
canvasser or an egg-fancier, or something calcu-
lated to enlarge the mind.
* * * *
A trooper with a fancy name, hailing from
Mudgee (N.S.W.), writes from South Africa to
the newspaper of his native town :
We took five prisoners not long ago, and one of them
had a bandolier full of explosives. One of the chaps put
one in his rifle and blew the top of his head off with it,
and would have served the other four alike if he had not
been stopped. The proper thing, too. I reckon they
ought to shoot everyone they get hold of, and not take
them prisoners at all. We laid a trap for them in a
deserted town we came through. We shot four dead,
put three bullets through another, and left them there.
. . . By Jove ! the Boers are a dirty lot. You can always
tell when you are close to a house on account of all the
filth and stuff about. We are burning every house we
get near, and shoot all the ponies. My squad was out
running ponies in one day. We brought in 750 head, shot
every one of them in a yard behind a kaffir’s hut, and
left them there. I guess they would have a splendid
odor in a few days. . . . The only trouble out here is
scarcity of wood. If we don’t happen to strike a fence or
a house near camp we are “ goosed ” for a fire, and that
means no tea.
After a few months’ rioting among the “dirty
Boers ” in this way, what a charming citizen will
that cold-blooded ruffian and murder-apologist be
for Australia ! But as to the dirt of the Boers,
the same paper (same issue) prints this from
another trooper
I was very much surprised with the Boer farms, for,
from newspaper accounts I had read of the people, I
thought they were an ignorant, dirty race, but the sight
of their homes totally dispelled that idea, for, almost
without exception, they were scrupulously clean and
neat, and in many houses there was, either a piano or
organ, and books were to be found in every place.

John Tolley, 67, old age pensioner, locked
up and fined at Armidale (N.S.W.) for drunken-
ness, was subsequently found to be suffering from
pneumonia, not drink, when arrested. He died ;
and the authorities express genteel regret at the
“ distressing blunder.” But that sort of blunder
never happens in regard to the £lOOO-a-vear
pensioner. His drink-torpor may be charitably
ascribed to illness, but never vice versa.

Yet another muscular limb of the law in
Melbourne has been ordered to pay for the keep
of his casual infant. Young constables are not
encouraged to marry ; they have to be of a fine
stamp physically ; and the life has unreasonably
good opportunities of temptation. So, all things
considered, Bobby must have lots of luck, or he
would be publicly asked for maintenance much
more frequently.

HOME CIGARETTES


Some Brisbaneites, parsons, Boodle-
wraithians, and others, evidently with an eye on
John Norton, ask that the sale of Sunday papers
(theoretically illegal there, as in Sydney) be
stopped. Melbourne took its John by the fore-
lock, and long ago specially legislated against
Sunday newspapers. It is said that Norton had
an idea of circumventing the law by establishing a
dairy and distributing Bpp. of objurgation,
nominally free, with the Sunday’s milk. The
cart would thus come round flowing with milk and
honey.
* * * *
If the Sunday sale of papers were for-
bidden, it would beahard knock to oneSydneyß.C.
journal which is regularly sold in the churches on
Sunday mornings.

From “ a Man who is Afraid ” :
When Lyne was N.S.W. Premier he, “in the interests
of safety,” ordered the removal of the Norddeutscher-
Lloyd line from its berth at the Circular Quay, Sydney.
The week after the huge Liverpool White Star liners took
the vacant berth, and have stayed there ever since ! On
a recent, Saturday, about the time everyone was going
home for lunch, the North Sydney ferry-boats were
blocked for some time while a steam collier tried to get
alongside the Medic lying at the wharf. A Mosman boat
laden with passengers attempted to get round, when a
tug-boat towing a punt, laden with locomotive machinery
from the big steamer, suddenly shot out under the stern
of the collier. The ferry-boat had a miraculous escape.
As a remedy for this state of affairs it is proposed that
—all the Parramatta River and Lane Oove ferries shall
add their quota to the confusion of Circular Quay 1 If
the authorities are looking out for excitement, why not
also berth the warships in Sydney Cove ?
NASTY !
MRS. CLUCK: “My work is never done. My girls
grow out of their dresses so quickly.”
MISS RASP : “ Hereditary tendency , no doubt.”
A railway collision evidently strikes par-
ticipants differently. Some of those in the recent
Melb. shock lost their wits entirely and were so
unnerved as to utterly decline to go on or back in
the train, or even to ever use such a vehicle again.
Others had their wits so sharpened as to annex
any portable property they could lay their hands
on. Quite a number of passengers said they had
money, watches, eye-glasses, etc., jerked from
their persons by the sudden stoppage, but not a
solitary lost article was found. A well-known
dog-training “ professor” was tossed violently into
the stomach of a respectable person opposite, and
when he found equilibrium he was minus his
beautiful set of false teeth and a purse
containing £7 odd—and not a sign of either
article was to be found anywhere afterwards,
not even a solitary tooth. Of course it is
safe to assume that not nearly all the missing
properties were stolen. In almost every accident
there is to be found the prompt and business-like
female, who fills the air with screams for the
restoration of her diamond necklace or her Koh-i-
-noor ; and the seedy man, who is calling out
dismally for his roll of notes and his gold watch
with his monogram in diamonds. They do this in
the hope that the Department will pay for the
alleged lost property. In such cases the person
who yells first has the best chance. The person
who calls out two weeks after the accident is
seldom believed, but the one who calls imme-
diately inspires a certain amount of confidence.
* * * *
There is a growing tendency on the part
of people suffering from mental strain and worry
to shed their clothes. Nowadays when the active
and underpaid policeman finds himself compelled
to escort a lady to the watch-house he has to con-
stantly guard against sudden attempts, not to
escape but to undress. A gentle and inexperienced
constable was assisting a lady across a Sydney
park the other day to the nearest haven of rest,
when she suddenly wrenched herself free and ran,
and before he could recapture her the rounded
outlines of Venus di Woolloomooloo had nothing
on but one boot. In Dunedin (M.L.) a respect-
able woman, whose sons had all got into gaol,
became hysterical lately, rushed on to the railway-
line, and tearfully began to strip in front of an
approaching train. In Melbourne, last week, a
woman, whose husband had disparaged her cook-
iug, burst into tears, and before the startled
complainant could interfere, had rushed out and
deposited her draperies on the pavement. “A
man what had a wife what had a figger like that
ought to be grateful if he got no dinner at all,”
said one onlooker.
The returned Victorian cohentingenters are
still loudly complaining of the apathy of the
Government in not finding them billets. Let
Peacock treat them as Britain treated its
war-worn veterans a couple of centuries ago.
These wrecks were graciously permitted to beg at
street-corners without danger of being run in by
the police of those days. The kookaburra should
abolish the Vag. Act on behalf of the Yic. fire-
eaters, and allow them a similar privilege. The
warriors will then be enabled to see at what rate
Australians value their “services.” If this privi-
lege was good enough for the battered heroes of
Blenheim and Malplaquet, it should be good
enough for the five-bob swaddies who went to
S’Africa and were asked to help six soldiers to
jump on one farmer and his wife and children.
* * * *
A Gatton (Q.) deputation, mostly of O.’s
and Fitz.’s, has protested to Home Secretary
Foxton that the removal of a police-officer from
that grisly township was due to his having arrested
an influential Mason for alleged cattle-stealing.
The matter is to be further investigated, and
Gatton police will be once again under the lime-
light.

A policeman with a delightful sense
of humor has come to light at S. Melb.
When an indignant resident growled that a
Eack of street-corner youths were in the
abit of interfering with his domestic peace,
the resourceful “ trap ” promptly went along
and arrested the resident’s own son for a start.
■* * * *
Re the discarded and discontented returned
cohentingenters, sundry Maoriland papers have
struck the idea of granting workless warriors
free space wherein to advertise for employ-
ment. The Bulletin commends this fact to the
notice of the Sydney and Melbourne dailies, but
hardly expects that their fervid “patriotism ” of
a couple of years back will now have enough life
left in it to follow the example. There’s no money
in the backwash of the war !

The Methodist Conference, it is cabled,
informs the Anglican Bishop of London that
‘ there are great difficulties in the way of union
with the Anglican Church.” Perhaps the chief
difficulty is that no Wesleyan parson could ever
satisfactorily fill an Anglican pulpit; it takes
generations to attain the propah trim of whiskahs
—the well brought-up-old-maid air, which is the
big bulwark of the—haw—G. of E. priesthood.
The Melbourne University frauds get
bigger every day, and now reach something like
£20,000, all of which has been taken right under
the nose of official auditors, who seemingly
accepted the'word of the embezzler that all was
correct. The so-called University finance com-
mittee, composed of Supreme Court judges and
professors, seems to about reach the high-water-
mark of financial incompetence. It owns up
cheerfully and unreservedly that it knew nothing
whatever about finance generally, and University
finance in particular. For years it had simply
done what the embezzler told it to do with the
docility of a trained poodle. What is to be the
upshot of the frauds nobody can tell. Probably,
by the time all the revelations are out, ’Varsity
will have to close its doors as a seat of learning,
while the building is turned into a butter-factory!

themselves, the little Duke being thrown in as a
side-show.
“Jerome Darnet” samples a
new creed :
Of the making: of religions, as of books,
there is truly no end. Recently I had for
fellow passenger on top of a ’bus a pros-
perous-looking, mild-mannered man who
wore his hair long and “ done up ” in
feminine fashion. On*top of it was a peculiar
half-mast sort of bell-topper. It occurred
to me that I had occasionally seen, in the
different Australian States, a few individuals
who affected the same style of hair-dress and
head-dress. After apologising for my in-
quisitiveness, I asked the reason of "these
peculiarities, and was courteously toid that my
informant, and the other persons I had
noticed, belonged to the Christian Israelite
Sect. This sect professes absolute belief in
the Christian religion, yet denies that the
Mosaic Law has been in any way abrogated by
the newer creed. Hence the abstention from
hair-clipping and shaving, the practice of cir-
cumcision, etc. In reply to a remark of
mine that so far as the Old Testament laws were
concerned the sect seemed more Jewish than
the Jews themselves, I was told that the
modern Jew had greatly fallen from
grace. The long-haired Israelite’s idea is
to so live as to be included in a certain
144,000 elect, whom it is stated,
“ somewhere in Revelations ” said my
friend, are to be translated alive,
somewhat after the manner of the
“chosen number” mentioned in A 1
Koran. There are 170 Christian Israel-
ites in Melbourne, but fewer in
Sydney. No person of great social
importance is numbered in the ranks,
the head clerk of a big Melbourne
timber-firm being their biggest gun in
this respect. Most members of the
sect practise vegetarianism in order
to obviate the necessity of eating
questionabty-prepared meats when
travelling. I was kindly invited to
attend the church of the sect (located
in Campbell-st.), and came to the con-
clusion that the Christian Israelites are a well-
meaning, clean and decent people, whose unob-
trusive attempt to solve the “riddle of the skies”
gives considerable satisfaction to themselves, and affects
a material economy in hair-cutting.
The fulsome flatterers of a live king or
jook are not more irritating to a people’s common
sense than some politicians who pretend to. voice
public sentiment re the assassination of Presi-
dent M'Kinley. The man-in-the-street squirms
over such nonsense as M‘Millan uttered in the
House of Reps, last week -“He said it was im-
possible to depict the feelings of the people of
Australia in their profoundest sympathy with the
millions of people of America who were now
grieving. Language was insufficient to depict
their feelings for the death of the late President.
But under the dark mantle of the cloud which had
fallen upon them there was a small gleam of light.
Such an act as this created the idea of a universal
brotherhood of man.” The fact is that the average
toiler for a living has no grief, or even sincere
regret, to spare for any but his own immediate
troubles. He has no real sympathy with the
orthodox official hysteria of grovel, gloomy or
gay. And he knows that the universal brother-
hood of man is no more advanced by the recent
despicable murder of President M’Kinley than it
was by the cowardly slaughter of Julius Caesar
two thousand years ago.

Of all the outbursts of blather and bathos
elicited by President McKinley’s death—-as
“ messages of sympathy ” to the unfortunate U.S.
Consuls in the various States—the gem, so far,
hails from Ballarat, whose Mayor wired—“ The
flag flies at half-mast on our city hall. The hearts
of our citizens throb in sympathy as their tears
mingle with those of your bereaved nation.”
THE TEMPTATION OF EVE.
HIS NIBS : “ Nicee arpull? ”
The Adelaide national (cohentingenter)
memorial fund is already in a state of stagnation.
An art union, with shilling tickets, was suggested
to help it along, but the pious element on the com-
mittee stopped that, although it had been arranged
that the first prize should be the warriors’ tomb-
stone itself, the winner to have the honor of
handing it back to the nation ! The latest idea is
a street collection, which the members of the
ladies’ committee have volunteered to take up.

ANOTHER MARTYR.
GEORGE: “ Say , Pally, people will think us a bit
mean if ice don’t go to that charity ball.”
POLLY: “ But, dear, I couldn’t think of leaving
baby!”
GEORGE: “No, that’s just what struck me. Look
here — it’s for a good cause! Suppose I go ?"
S.M. Herald publishes with thinly-veiled
approval (it says that “ many people ” agree with
its correspondent) a scream from one Osborne, of
Sydney Union Club, that murderers should be
kept in gaol some months before banging, and
lashed at frequent intervals. The Union Club has
been the home of some queer opinions. Wasn’t it
the Union Club which loyally declined to admit as
an honorary member barrister Aspinall, who
came from Melbourne to perform the treason-
able task of defending lunatic O’Farrell,
charged with wounding the Duke of Edin-
burgh ? It was a deputation formed very
largely of Union Club members, which prayed
Gov. Carrington to by all means stretch the necks
of the Mt. Rennie boys—and that ordinary Smith-
minded person lost the opportunity of a life-time
by failing to kick downstairs men who (instead of
recognising that his function was not to choke
people but to extend mercy wherever it could be
granted) put him in the position of hangman-in-
chief. Behind the classic pillars of the Union
likewise was hatched Reid’s famous Yes-No
Federal policy. But for mediaeval gruesomeness
and general lack of civilised instincts, this instal-
ment-flogging-and-hanging plan takes even a
Union Club record.
By the way, as Mr. Osborne (whoever he may
be) believes in torture, why doesn’t he propose to
do the thing thoroughly ? Flogging is no sort of a
punishment compared with the insertion of
splinters under the nails, or dropping boiling lead
into the nostrils. Besides what about'the rack, the
thumbscrew, the scavenger’s daughter and the
pitch-cap ? The Siamese trick of pinching the
abdominal skin with red-hot nippers is also a
handsome kind of punishment which the good old
Tories of the Union Club must surely have over-
looked.

S. Australian farmers, looking round for
draught stock for the coming harvest, find that
the army remount-man has left his mark behind
him. “Gunners” in the Adelaide market have
been selling at £4O, and medium draughts to £3O
—from 50 to 75 per cent, more than the prices of
two years back.
“ J.M.” to The Bulletin :
As a returned contingenter let me ask “ G.M.” who was
in charge of the Australian camp between Zwartkopies
and Pretoria when rifles were being fired at helmets to
make close-shave war-souvenirs. I belonged during the
campaign to an irregular corps, in which our officers
allowed us more liberties than any of the Australian
officers of regular corps, yet it would have meant certain
“clink” and probable court-martial for anyone firing off
a rifle in camp. And, in any case, few Australians have
any need to make bogus war-souvenirs.
The wharf-strikers at Albany, W.A., thus
appeal, per circular, to recent arrivals:—“Will
you join hands with ours, and strive for a white
man’s wage in a white man’s country ? Or will
you become the means of bringing upon this town
a calamity to be feared as men fear the plague ’
The name ‘ blackleg ’ will be branded upon you •
and you will be shunned as lepers are shunned.’
• • • Let not poverty detain you in semi-prisons.
Come ashore, and walk about as free men in a free
country. . . .” The squabble arose over a reduc-
tion of 3d. per ton in lumping rates. The old
price was Is. 6d., which, considering the Western
living-rate, and irregularity of work, is little
enough.
WHILE SANDS OF ETERNITY RUN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ilark ! A thrush, on a bough, thro’ the leaves
Lets float his round note to the dome
Of the world overhead. And the trees
Shed sweet scented tears on the loam.
The pale Moon is a maiden that loves,
And the sea sighs over the sand,
And the cote it is dumb of its doves,
And the wings of the world expand.
And my darling sleeps in the earth
In silence, awaiting the rain,
Which will bring her in sweet second birth
To me, as a lily, again.
And I, from my cypress-gloomed grave,
Will rise like a rose in the sun,
And will throw her my kisses, and wave
While sands of Eternity run.
N.S.W.
TROUVERE.
THE OLD COUCH.
It stands on the station verandah ;
It is crooked and leans to one side ;
In length it is just quite proper,
And it isn’t a bit too wide.
It has listened to many a love-tale,
Been weighted with numberless forms ,
Has creaked and baked in the sunlight,
And weathered the fiercest of storms;
It has learnt so much in its lifetime
That I am free to confess
It knows more or less than I do,
But I really don’t think that it’s less.
