A WOMANS LETTER.
Sydney, September 24, 1901.
My Dear Arini,—
Sun and sky and dust and flowers. The man
w h° invented weather ought to have a statue
erected to him—by the persons who write about
his invention. I shall evade sending my subscrip-
tion, though, for I’m not going to write anything
about weather, save to remark that, according to a
local doctor, our climate is peculiarly favorable to
the Asiatic cholera germ. So the germ and the
coming of the cricketers will be something to
which we may look forward. There must be a
dearth of topics all round, even in the spot from
whence Britannia rules the waves, for I note that
a big aristocratic Fog-town daily is running a
discussion—“ Have Nurses Flat Feet?” When
the Italian Opera co. and the Broughs are else-
where and the dogstar is here (not before) I shall
Eut a discussion or two on the market: “ Should
obsters be Pedicured or Manicured?” or “Do
Wasps Tight-lace?”
How impressive are often the last words of
great men—if we could be sure that the words
were really spoken by the dying great one: “I
wish I had one of Putnam’s meat-pies,” were
really the last words of the Duke of Wellington.
The sky-filling “Roll up the map of Europe”
was not uttered by the great man who is reputed
to have said it, but by one of his physicians.
And, look here, I won’t be sure that Putnam is the
correct name. Anyhow, it was the name of a man
whose meat-pies the Duke loved. Now, I don’t
intend the slightest irreverence, but the expiring
words of President M'Kinley had a singular
fineness and fitness. The going-out of his light
was a harmonious blend of the dramatic and
religious, and in this case there is no suspicion of
physicians’ or Reuter’s “ fake.” He was religious
by instinct —a lover of Whittier, of nature, and
the devoted husband of his always ailing wife.
They had been married 30 years, and their two
children died in infancy. In a King-street shop
there is a copy of a Buffalo Express of
September, 1880, announcing the death of
the murdered Garfield. “The President is dead.”
He was shot some months earlier, but lingered till
the same month in which President M'Kinley fell.
As one with a September birthday, I cannot help
noticing what a number of great or disastrous
events have occurred in this month. Battles,
shipwrecks, assassinations. People die, too, with
considerable eagerness in September. Awhile ago
I went for a stroll into that pretty little bush
cemetery at Chatswood, and the writing on the
first five headstones in my path proclaimed the
sleepers to be September-reaped.
The Women’s Franchise Bill is agAin checked.
Both Houses profess to desire its passing, but the
cunning Upper House pretends that it wants
women to be eligible to sit in both Houses
of Parliament. The same moth-eaten argu-
ments were advanced against the measure, but.
the feeblest of all was that the exercise of the
franchise will interfere with woman’s domestic
duties. Also, that she’ll have the friction of un-
desirable persons at the polling-booth. Even if
she had to “exercise” once a week, instead of
once in three years, what would it be compared
with golfing and visiting ? I know women whose
whole objective in this life is golf ; whose idea of
Heaven is first-class links. As for the undesirable
friction at the booths, I have watched crowds filing
into polling-booths and I never saw anyone half
so undesirableJoo&mp as the Musical Burglar with
the Polite American Eagle Co. now at the Palace.
He is an insanitary-seeming, versatile ruffian, and
you don’t know what rags are till you see his.
The dead-beats in the Domain are dressed for a
Governor’s levee beside the M.-B. The night I
was at the Palace I had the joy of hearing for the
first time (in the d.c. of a theatre) the phrase “ By
Cripes, but this is an awlright show ! ” accom-
panied by shouts of laughter. What called it
forth was the Tramp’s striking a match on
the nude marble figure that stands on the counter
of the stage bar. I wish Mr. Ambrose Pratt’s
friends would read “Quo Vadis” and get some
choice swears. “ By the white knees of the Graces ”
sounds ever so much better than “By Cripes.”
In one of the stage boxes was the Baronetess
Clarke and a couple of youthful escorts. The
Baronetess long-necked, long-faced, bediamonded,
with style all over her and a lovely theatre-coat
half off her.
She who plays the long, keyless Egyptian
trumpet at the Palace show is a most attractive
bit of womanhood. Such outline, such arms and
shoulders and Dana-Gibson head ! We’re always
being told that the American girl is fragile, and
Bret Harte writes of sickly American wifehood.
I haven’t yet seen one fragile American woman.
Nance O’Neil, Mrs. Clay Clements, Mrs. Van-
derveer Green, Madame Sapio, Maud Jeffries,
the Egyptian trumpet-girl—they are of heroic
build.
When Musgfbve’s opera co. was in Auckland,
the local Savage Club attended a performance of
“Carmen,” and felicitated Harry Musgrove, and
sent up a bouquet to the Carmen of the evening.
When told of the bouquet proposal a couple of
days before, M. said that similar marks of favor
from representative bodies to individual members
of the co. had caused heart-burning; to the residue.
He hinted that it could not be permitted. Whereat
the Savage retort was that, though Musgrove might
fairly claim to boss his own co., he had no control
over the Auckland public—at least while Seddon
was about—and that as the posy gew-gaw had cost
four or five guineas it was going to its destination,
even if it produced real murder in the third aot
instead of tne paste variety. Accordingly it was
handed up to Carmen (Madame Janson). But, to
prevent unnecessary laceration of artistic feelings,
it had previously been addressed to every possible
Carmen in the 00., the right address being affixed
only at the last moment.
In “La Traviata,” at Her Majesty’s, last week,
Madame Sapio was not at all at the top of her
physical condition. She had a touch of influenza.
In the first act, the Verdi-ized Lady of Camellias
wears a Paris gown of brocade, hand-painted, and
crepe de chine. Concerning Paris materials : Prof.
Warren, the engineering authority who had been
sent from Sydney to Europe to study steel
bridges, pumping gear, and such like, was
wandering through an annexe of Paris Exhibition
when he ran across Prof. Gurney. After the
“ fancy meeting you ” greeting the engineer’s first
question was “Have you seen Worth’s exhibit?”
Madame Sapio lost her purse, containing £l5, on
Saturday night, after leaving the theatre. It’s
very amusing to hear people wondering if the
American soprano can speak English. Dani, as
Alfred Germont, didn’t look his best in his three-
quarter brocade coat and black curls. The 26-
year-old tenor gets nearly as many love-letters
from unknown women as the late Charles Ryley,
but he has to take them to the co.’s interpreter to
read, so that rather discounts their charm and
adds to their idiocy. Signor Tondini has got
engaged to one of the chorus (Miss Crawford), a
Melb. girl, whose late father was high up in the
tramway service.
The playing of Madame Buhrow and Mr.
Davies in “Madame Cyprienne” was capital.
Madame was high up the dramatio hill in Vienna
before she married. Mr. Davies is a young solici-
tor, and used to play with the old University
Dramatic Club.
Manager Edwin Geach passed through Sydney
this week en route to Brisbane, in order to play
the parts of a pillar of cloud by day and fire by
night to the Arnold co. He will bring the Arnold
tribe to Sydney Criterion at Xmas.
A public school inspector, watching the physical-
drill items at last week’s annual public school
sports, said that since the introduction of these
■exercises he had noticed a great difference in the
growth and development of the children. Which
is satisfactory hearing. Six thousand children
entered for the different competitions, whilst four
thousand were in the various display events. It
was a splendid spectacular display, and Mr. D. C.
Hook (hon. sec.) deserved the cheers that were
given for him.
“ George Egerton, ” the rather tropical writer,
was married, the other day, for the third time.
She was born Dunne (in Melbourne), and married
first a Mr. Melville, then a Mr. Egerton Clair-
monte ; now she’s Mrs. Bright. Rather a good
record for a woman still under forty. Mrs. Bright
is a little, skinny, dark-eyed woman with a lot of
nice brown hair.
A legislator, whose friends think he’s mostly
dreams and torpid liver, introduced someone to
the Minister, saying, “This is Mrs. Blank, a
journalist of—of—with the best intentions ! ” The
little big man saw the unconscious (?) irony and
promised to put a sum on the estimates to pension
■off tactless legislators.
Lord Somebody’s jewels have been stolen, the
cable is telling us, from the Walsingham Hotel.
Which reminds me that a friend in London writes
to me of the Walsingham as an insignificant-
looking but very swell place. Rupert Clarke
stops there, in the flat his wife occupies when
she’s in London, said flat costing 15 guineas a day.
The late Mr. John Wagner, of the firm of
Robertson and Wagner, left estates in Vic. and
Q. valued at over £133,000. His eldest son, Mr.
Jack Wagner, who some years ago was Mr. J. H.
Want’s junior, is at present in Sydney. He was,
at the time of his father’s death, literary critic of
New York Sun, and will shortly return to his
work there. “Jack ” Wagner is one of the most
modest of men and society bores him as much
as squatting does. You’ll mostly see him with
musicians or music-lovers. His only unmarried
sister has just gone to England. “ Stonington,”
the Wagners’ house in Melb., will be the residence
of State-Governor Clarke.
“Governor Tennyson and his unassuming wife,”
writes an Adelaide friend, “are easily the most
popular couple S.A. Government House has yet
known. Lady Tennyson has now, after a year of
continuous hard work, practically carried through
a scheme for a maternity home for the poor, one
of the most-needed of local charitable schemes.
Absence of “ side ” is the chief trait of the Tenny-
sons, a quality which was not too characteristic of
their immediate predecessors.”
Curious thing how sculptors, male and female,
seem to have, at one time or another, the bust of a
bishop under their chisels. Bishops seem to like
busts—when done in marble. Miss Theodora
Cowan writes to her friends here that she has
just completed a bust of the Bishop of London
and is meeting with great encouragement. Our
local Arohbishops have been busted in marble or
modest plaster again and yet again.
A pasear through rooms filled with bronzes,
porcelains, buhl and marqueterie furniture. Chairs
and cabinets and vases that recall the time of the
freat Louis. You remember the room in Conan
)oyle’s “Tale of Two Continents,” in which
Madame de Maintenon and the King’s discarded
mistress had their ‘ ‘scrap. ” Well, to-day you could
almost furnish a replica of that room from Walter
Bradley’s auction mart. W hat artists have done
with rare woods, the tints they have given to rare
china, makes you think of the phrase, “dreams
in flower.” Among the bronzes, a splendid group,
the “Educationof Bacchus ” will hold you—as the
wine-cup does Bacchus. And there are two vases
of Austrian ware—poppies are they with the figure
of a sleeping woman in the heart. They are
poems—Swinbqrnish poems. Your pasear will
lead you upstairs to the picture-gallery, where
you will see some just-opened pictures. Four
exquisite miniatures by Fred Goff; a water-color,
“ Rough Weather, Boulogne Harbor,” by H.
Caffieri. This, and John Richardson’s “Cheshire
Lane,” and Sheridan Knowles’ “Phyllis is my
only Joy” were exhibited in this year’s Royal
Institute Exhibition of Water-colors.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ At the Findon Harriers’ meeting, last Satur-
day, the North wind blew a hurricane. The
descent from the drags at Moonee Valley course
was the laughable part of the show. Some of the
women, in flapping dust-coat sails and flying
gossamers, resembled draggle-tail kites touching
earth after a boisterous aerial voyage. Others,
minus the protective gossamer and tarpaulin
covering, looked worse, for their hats were askew,
their furbelows in strings, and their back-hair in
straggling ends. Two figures only arrived on
the wings of the wind in immaculate, speckless,
irreproachable attire —His Waxellency and the
Dowager ; the latter came, however, in a closed-up
carriage, letting her girls perch up aloft the ‘ Clive-
den ’ drag. She was the wonder of all; even the
caperish winds whirling over Moonee Valley
respected her person, and left her fine purple
inviolate. She was tricked forth in heliotrope
silk, wonderfully fashioned, perfectly fitting—the
one perfect figure of that buffeted and blown-to-
pieces feminine bevy. A tricky toque, that re-
called her son’s marriage-feast, rode through the
storm unharmed. The smiling hostess of Ruperts-
wood sported a familiar Paddy-green garb and
earrings to match. Sister Hilda held on to her
oilskin moorings with both hands, till her pinkish
face was the color of cochineal. Mrs. Charlie
Ryan, who is ready to die in the cause of chiffons,
presented the new straight-corseted kind of figure
in a check-tweed costume and an assertive what-
do-you-think-of-it toque in ink and paper tones.
Miss Muriel Miller wore a wilful runaway-shaped
hat topped with big blue bows. The Excellency
played her old trick by disappointing people at
the last moment.
“All the theatrical world that could spare the
time and the effort were at the meeting. Marie
Lloyd, louder in her black silken and blue
raiment and jewelled hangings than the dust-
fiend, signalled all eyes every time she moved;
her silk-arrayed companion jingled and frou-
froued in a similar way. Miss Wilson Barrett
looked at the crowd and the racing-track through
severe glasses, and under a hat whose vivid pink
garniture was at variance with a most serious
garb and air. Harrie Ireland’s burnished-gold
head was never far from the villain Steyn’s eye-
glass ; her biscuit coat, with Inverness sleeves,
was a staggerer.
“Wilson Barrett, as ‘Alfred of Engle-land,’
makes no attempt to be like anyone but himself.
Pe»-tmps he thinks that the Wilson Barrett
sufficient unto its owner, the great
‘ author-‘am c '»r-manager,’ as'he habitually calls
himself, more than fulfils the comparatively
modest requirements of Alfred. Does • the
Barrett fancy himself John Bull, Shakespeare,
Garrick, Nelson, Wellington, Franklin, Captain
Cook and Washington rolled into one? I ask
because of his new poster. It shows W.B. as a
beauteous youth, backgrounded by the two
World hemispheres and the flags of England,
America, and Australia. Grouped round Crea-
tion’s hero are the British lion, a grim polar-bear,
Uncle Sam’s eagle, the kangaroo, black swan, and
the mask of drama. In the Alfred play, dark-
haired Lillah M'Carthy, as a handsome Celtic
syren, indulges in various rich color-blends.
■Robes of warm yellow, embroidered with gold and
emeralds, are topped by an Oriental head-scarf of
vivid orange. Later, the same charmer brightens
the Danish camp in a kingfisher-like and beautiful
combination of pale-blue gown and gorgeous
purple cloak.
“For out-door conquest, Miss M‘Carthy still
pins her hopes to the large, innocent white hat
with the chastened feathers. They are not the
bold, hoydenish feathers, nor the dashing woman-
of-fashion feathers, nor the racy kind, nor the coy
kind ; but the innocent, stitched-down, kept-in-
their-place, demure order of plumage. When you
look at Miss M‘Carthy’s beseeching eyes and that
chaste characteristic hat, one of Marcus Stone’s
pictures comes before the mind, and you begin to
liken the lean and rather lovely languisher to
‘ The Peacemaker ’ —but only with the one ex-
pression and the one hat, mind 1
“ Edyth Latimer, a nicely brought-up |Saxon,
wedded to Alfred, shares his exile in the neat-
herd’s cottage, and volunteers to bake cakes for her
lord. You never saw such a poor turn of spec-
tacular cookery. Neophytes should take a lesson
from Mr. and Mrs. Bland Holt. It’s both a
pleasure and profit to watch their deft, convincing
treatment of stage steaks and pancakes. ‘ Queen
Alfred,’ on the contrary, provides a warning
to all amateur bakers. Curls dangle down
her back, she neglects to tie an apron over
her stuff dress and covers herself with flour.
When hubby comes home she prints both
her dear, doughy little hands on his russest vest,
and dandles baby with floury paws. As the result
of all this muddle and litter, she poses prettily
between finger and thumb a dough-pellet, big as a
hazel nut, which never gets as far as the griddle,
so that Alfred has to dine on kisses. As Alfred’s
Saxons must still have been pretty German in
their ways, I wonder that “Elsurtha” was such
a muff at cooking. Teutons of to-day are simplv
mad on that subject. Papa Barrett’s “Alfred”
is overfond of silver armour and white Lohengrin
plumes. Such a dandy king would scarcely have
made his mark in rough Saxon days. A bird which
soars high hasn’t much time for preening its
plumage.
“ Off the boards, Edyth Latimer has much more
to say than the dux of the school. And if Miss
L.’s lip only got rid of that scornful, or tearful, or
painful tilt at the left corner, she might be passing
pretty and dangerously animated.
“At Saturday’s ‘Nadjezda’ matinee Lillah
M‘Carthy and colleagues filled the manager’s box.
Lillah’s expressive face looks much the same off
the stage as on, but like predecessor Maud
Jeffries, her every-day coiffure and dress-methods
seem a shade careless and slap-dash. Edyth
Latimer rustled impressively to and fro m ear-
attracting silken underskirts. From opposite box
round-faced actor Manning looked on with a
friend. In the d.c., Alice Crawford sported the
wind-blown tresses, blouse-like bodice, and limp
muslin and lace collar, affected by Barrett leading
ladies, past, present and future.
“Edith Crane’s most successful ‘Nadjezda’
frock is a plain, well-fitting princess-robe of pale
fawn-cloth cut in a small square at the neck. Its
only trimming is a knot of brown chenille, with
long ends falling from the corsage. The play im-
proves with acquaintance. Principals Crane,
Power and Glover are not to blame for short-
comings, but I think the support is weak.
The young lover, nice as possible in pleas-
ant times, seems almost a stick, when he
should be a modern Ajax, vainly trying to
defy the lightnings of fate. Then there’s an
American girl, dragged in as relief to tragic
‘Nadine.’ Her impersonator, bright and cheeky
enough, is another stage neophyte, not pretty, and
handicapped by badly-cut frocks, all wrinkled
round the hips. Young men, who hate dismals,
can’t bring themselves to fall in love with her even
as a distraction from the general gloom.
“Mrs. G. B. W. Lewis haunts the circle at
‘Nadjezda.’ The disciple of tragedy brings her
most massive and portentous frowns to consider
the play and the actors. Those deep lines of
thought and concentrated study between her eye-
brows were etched in when the Tragic Muse first
won her dramatic affections; they give her, in
these mature days, a somewhat fierce and Gorgon
ook.
“ The ‘ Christian King ’ matinee on Wednesday
last had a bumper house. In one box Tyrone
Power sat, with Edith Crane and young
Hammond, who looked like a bank-clerk consider-
ably perturbed by the presence of play-actors.
Hammond, the hero of ‘Nadjezda,’ is such an
innocent. Tyrone looked as on villainy intent,
bending forward and watching every movement
on the stage, his shaggy black locks tumbling over
his heavy brow. Edith Crane’s hat was the target
for all eyes feminine. It was a strawberry-and-
cream creation, masses of lace foaming about the
crown, strawberry-pink roses blooming forth on
the further side. A tuft of lace, in true French
fashion, flourished at the back of the ruin, and
drooped towards the neck-slope.
“ Frances Ross grows more of the languishing
Lydia every day. Large, floppy picture-hats
crown her dark, neglige thatch. Skirts sweeping
the earth languish about her limbs. She gives the
feminine observer the impression of wearing no
petticoats. But she rustles in up-to-date fashion,
and her heel-taps make the common click-click of
all fashionables abreast of the times. Still,
Frances languishes in her walk and her talk, and
even in the very manner of giving her thrum to
the awed and admiring tram-conductor.
“The latest photos, of the Countess represent
her with a tweed cap, a tailor-made coat, and a
couple of poodles submitting to a petting. Her
predecessor was taken in exactly the same position
here. These sporting Excellencies are » great
salve to the Horstralian conscience.
“A Melb. widow faced the hymeneal rails,
t’other Saturday, in sky-blue silk, low-necked and
no-sleeved. An aigrette stood with an undaunted
air of aggression in her coiled masses of fair hair.
Four short-skirted sprites in buttercup-yellow
backed her up, whilst two rejected and dejected
lovers looked on with lack-lustre eyes at the other
fellow’s capture. Hitching-up was an evening
one, which is to say that all went with a r> extra
show of —er —complexion and chiffon.
“Ever the luck of children ! Among the pas-
sengers in the outgoing Melb. train that came to a
sudden stop—against another—t’other day, were a
husband and wife and their baby girl. The man
was severely bruised on the head, and his wife had
h"r teeth forced through her upper lip, besides
losing one or two, but baby’s sole annoyance was
in being woke-up so suddenly.
“A particularly sinful result of the rise in the
price of Melbourne butchers’-meat is a twopence
per lb. rise in the price of smoked fish. The fish-
supply could easily be doubled or trebled if neces-
sary, but the big dealers prefer to meet the largely-
increased demand with a slightly-increased supply
at “famine prices.”
“Italian warship officers are not given to dis-
tributing buttons to the social sirens of the harbors
they visit. Flowers, boxes of perfume, and rosary
beads are their usual souvenirs. The beads are
incentives to goodness or aids to picturesque
repentances.
“ A prominent State politician never wearies of
telling the story of his love and courtship. The
other day he repeated it over the walnuts to a
large and varied dinner-party : ‘ I never looked at
a woman, orthought of one, till I went to someopen-
air affair, and I was introduced to some ladies,
and sat next to one of them. I thought
a cigar would relieve the situation. I struck
a match. She blew it out. I struck another.
She blew that out. I struck the third, and said :
“ That’s never been done to me before ; if you do
it again I’ll kiss you.” She did—and I did.
That’s all. In a week we were fixed up.’ A
match of this sort, if made in Heaven, at any rate
had a brimstone-and-phosphorus tip.
“ Mr. Jenvey, the nice-looking champion of the
telephone Trilbies—so he constituted himself at a
recent Inquiry Board —is a bachelor, and one of
the politest officers in the service. The girls are
delighted at his championship. It is sweet to be
believed in. ‘The married men,’ they say now,
‘are brutes—they know too much.’
“ By the way, a Toorak matron, the other day,
gave a telephone tea; and everyone on earth
wants to know what that was. It was a mere
ringing up of all the guests in request on the
telephone. Impromptu, quite, and so cheap!
such a saving of stationery and stamps on the
part of hostess and guests. Economy rules the
day.

ported Arabs to his stud.

England, and that there was no need to worry
over the custody of the lady’s only child as he was
21. The man in the question was one Penstone,
an artist whose name was once familiar in Sydney.

William George Cassidy, who died last
week, was for many years caterer to N.S.W. Par-
liament, and “uncle” to its needy members,
Cassidy made much money out of sandwiches and
small loans, and was wont to cut quite a dash in
fashionable holiday resorts when Parliament was
out or season.
A visit was paid last week to Parramatta
by Mr. A. Batman Weire. There, in 1800, was
born his grandfather, John Batman, one of the
founders of Melbourne. Mr. A. Batman Weire is
an official of the Vic. Old Age Pension depart-
ment, and in his duties encounters many Aus-
tfajian pioneers.

news puts out of joint the noses of several
expectant people.

The late Dr. Wright, of Wynyard Square,
Sydney, was not, as stated in the press, »the
oldest medico practising in Sydney. Dr. Crooke,
of Bourke-street, Woolloomooloo, qualified in
1838 and, now almost 90 years of age, is still to
the front. Dr. Bowker, M.L.C., started in the
same year, and Dr. Bassett, of Bathurst (1849),
and several others date professionally from the
forties.
One of the victims of the recent lamp-
explosion in a Melbourne suburban temple of
Venus was, a few years back, one of the beauties
of a very popular comic opera, and the possessor
of a reputation that won her a wealthy husband,
whom the world deemed a lucky man. Like most
of the oldest profession in the world, she changed
her name on assuming her vocation, and, also like
many of them, her last thoughts when dying in
Melbourne hospital were about the child she had
borne when a virtuous wife.
Kingston continues to boss the Customs
dept, with the firmness of a Czar. A few days ago
he received word that aTreasury officer was inspect-
ing the different departments, and would be at
the Customs in a day or two. Charley seized the
telephone and demanded to be switched on to the
Treasurer. “Is that Turner?” he demanded
with a transmitter - fracturing roar. “Yes,”
replied the Treasurer. “Then,” continued
Kingston, “if that man of yours comes along
fooling about this department we’ll chuck him
into the Yarra ! ”

There wasa merry millon board the steamer
which brought a more or less prominent political
personage back to Sydney t’other day. Feeling
combative, and having no other safe vent for his
pugnacity, the politician tackled a dead sheep
which was hanging in the cook’s galley. The car-
case was battered long and furiously. The humor
of the incident developed when the sheep swung
off its hook and gave the personage a dreadful
black eye. Even a dead sheep turns sometimes.

One Yic. Legislative Councillor neither
reads nor writes, and takes most scrupulous care

Wire from Brisbane :
The library of the late Mr. James Tyson was sold by
auction to-day. There was a good attendance, and
bidding was brisk, the sale realising over £3O.
Over £3O ! How much over ? A life of almost
80 years, with the resources of a millionaire, must
have left at least an additional half-crown’s worth
of books. If so, Jimmy should reap the specific
credit. Give him the half-crown.

Obit. : Mr. George Colquhoun, N.S.W.
Crown Solicitor, aged 72 years. He was admitted
to practice in July, 1855, and had very few seniors
in the “lower branch” of the legal profession.
Mr. Colquhoun, an able and well-educated man,
had a stirring experience of the roaring goldfields
days, and once successfully defended bushranger
Ben Hall on a charge of cattle stealing. As a
civil servant, he kept a remarkably stiff upper-lip
and, in consequence, fell out with various
prominent politicians.

who endeavoured to interview Bill Lyne recently,
when the Minister was squirming in the agonies
of rheumatiz. Twice the scribbler sent up his
card, and twice he was told that Long Bill could
not be seen under any circumstances. “ Tell the
Minister,” said the P.M.P., “ that I must see him,
if only for five minutes.” The long-suffering
waiter went back for the third time, and returned
bearing a card and a 3in. grin. On the card was
the simple legend—“ Go to H 1! ”
Brisbane Street :
Meagher, Crick, and Norton illustrate the permutation
of combinations. Each new phase represents a fight to
gaol or death. At the close of the George Dean poisoning-
case it was Crick and Meagher indicated, with John
Norton the bosom friend of both. Next, we had Crick
acquitted, Meagher convicted, and Norton, the friend of
Meagher and sworn foe of Crick. Next, we had Norton
and Meagher in open fight with revolver and sticks in
Sydney streets. Next, Crick and Norton chums, and
Meagher the outcast. Next, Crick and Norton foes, and
Meagher hovering around. Now Crick and Meagher are
friends, and Norton is Ishmael. Meagher supports Crick
in Parliament, Crick invites Meagher to a seat on the
Ooningham select committee inquiry, and Norton’s name
is scored off the list.
English papers resurrect
W. T. Carter, ex-M.L.A. for
Williamstown, Vic. ; this time
with “an invention for propel-
ling ships” under his arm. Car-
ter has “submitted it to Lord
Kelvin, and the verdict is so
favorable that he has had sub-
stantial offers for an interest.”
A remarkable man is Carter.
A Methodist parson in S. Aus.,
he turned up in Vic. because
there was more scope for a man
with nothing to do, fought Wil-
liamstown on a credit balance of
ss. 3d., and won it; invented a
torpedo, got enough support to
send him to England, and for
some time past has been figuring
as a naval expert.

known as “Black Bill,” was, in
his oarly days, an athlete of
mark, and for some time held the
100yds. record (lOAsecs.). He
was clever with nis Lands, too—

Pd. Bulletin. —With regard tu ‘‘Lake
Amadeus ” and a few other places supposed to
have been named by the late Ernest Giles, his
original names were quite different. His inap
was, before publication, handed to the late Baron
Von Mueller, who substituted “ Amadeus ” for
Giles’ name, which, I think, was “Lake Despera-
tion.” “ Mt. Olga” was at first “ Mt. Un-
approachable,” and “ Mt. Liebig” was named
after your humble servant, whose hope of being
thus immortalised was ruthlessly swept away by
the botanist Baron in favor of the bull-broth
Baron.—Soak.
In M'Lean, the Christchurch triple mur-
derer, “Tom” Long (Maoriland’s Government
man-butcher) dispatched his 18th victim. Long
lives with the Maoris at Kawangaroa Pah—the
abode of the late Harry Laing, Maori pugilist—
some eight miles from Wanganui. He receives
£25 per job, and spends his blood-money in
‘ ‘ setting them up ” for ghoulisbly-inclined
acquaintances, to whom he discourses on his grue-
some performances. His money gone, he dis-
appears until again officially required.

The Bulletin’s London correspondent) a “ dear
old sentimental creature.” The spacious room in
which her favorite personal attendant, John
Brown, died was, by H.M.’s order, hermetically
sealed for 18 years. His dates, his virtues, and
his deeds, inscribed on brass, ornamented the wall,
but—the old order giveth way to the new King.
To-day the room, re-decorated, &c., reverberates
to the click of the ivories : J.B.’s death-chamber
is now No. 2 billiard-room of Windsor Castle,

John Madden, the heroic Ballarat miner—
who, after being blinded in an explosion some
months ago at the Star of the East, climbed up
the interminable ladders of the mine to get help
for a dying mate —has, after rather paltry assist-
ance from the Miners’ Association, had his case
taken up by the Ballarat East C«incil, which is
endeavouring to get the A.M.A. pittance in-
creased and to start a public subscription to obtain
enough to support the brave man in reasonable
comfort for the rest of his days.
In his public life 'the late J. P. Abbott,
long-time Speaker of N.S.W. Assembly, went on
the don’t-care-a-hang-for-you principle. When he
stood first for Gunnedah his address was, in effect,
“I’ll condescend to represent you if you like, but
I’d just as soon not.” Afterwards the same plan
worked at Wentworth, and he persisted in it right
through ; he rarely visited the district even at
election time. Same in the Convention election;
he did not make one speech in support of his
candidature. But that was on the surface; the
under-currents were working hard enough. What
made him so “solid” with his Western con-
stituents was that he was prompt and punctilious
in answering correspondence.
Printed that Father Bernard Russell,
a R.C. priest, has been received into the
Anglican Church at St. Paul’s Cathedral,
London. Quite lately, Father “Bernard,”
whose secular name is “William Charles,”
was officiating in Tasmania. He was a typical
giddy little curate, who wore a sac coat
and glasses, and once sang “Fiddle and I,”- with
great effect, at a R.C. concert, in Hobart Town
Hall. He had come out to Tasmania for his
health, which was delicate, and Archbishop
Murphy sent him up to Latrobe, as curate to -
Archdeacon Noone. Little Russell was an Eng-
lish ’vert from Anglicanism ; so he has just gone
back; that’s all.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors.— ln the case of ethnological and
natural history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
Segenhoe, near Scone (N.S.W.), a station,
in its way one of the historic spots of Australia,
anil probably the first settlement on the Hunter
River, was recently cut up and sold by auction.
It was abandoned in the old convict days; and
when, later, a party of pioneers came across it,
the only living thing on it, beside cattle, was an
old convict servant, who was a jabbering imbecile
and could give no coherent story. Many queer
yarns are told of -Segenhoe. It has a fortress-like
homestead, guarded by cannon of ancient type.
The old triangle, where the convicts were
flogged, is still standing. Another relic is a
marble bath, which, the legend says, was
filled with milk every morning by convict
servants (a special herd erf cattle being kept
for the purpose), and in this the squatter’s wife
took her morning dip, the milk being afterwards
served out to the convicts. Her ladyship is said
to have been wondrously beautiful, though not
particularly chaste, and later became a reigning
beauty in London. A little Segenhoe history was
once published, but was suppressed. It is said,
however, that many interesting documents re-
lating to Segenhoe incidents of 100 years ago are
in possession of a N.S.W. solicitor, and may some
day see the light.

‘‘ The finest and rarest collection of Maori
relics” (writes The Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent) “ in or out of Maoriland is in the posses-
sion of Major-General Robley—good name !
who fought to some purpose in the native wars of
Seddonia. No museum in M.L. nor the pick of
all the museums can compete against the British
soldier’s ‘little lot.’ His twenty or so smoke-
praserved heads of Maori chiefs and children are
superb—in the expert antiquarian’s eyes. To the
man-in-the-street—with their eyelids stitched to-
gether, the nqstrils plugged with iridescent halio-
tus shell, and the facial skin hidden beneath
scrolls of perfect tattooing, while the clenohed
teeth grin between the dried, drawn-apart lips,
they are, bunched in their cupboard shelves,
‘devilish scaring.’ (Somebody should tell The
Bulletin readers something of ‘ moko.’) With
these hi.ieousities are greenstones, carved boxes,
weapons, &c., &c., that should never have been
allowed to leave their island home. Recently a
wealthy Austral offered £4OOO odd for the collec-
tion, .and the offer was accepted on condition
that it did not leave England. The Austral
swore and the deal was off. Perhaps King
Seddon, however, can win these treasures back to
their homeland.” Why not send a contingent to
take them ?

The Holy S. A us. police have just decided
that weight-guessing competitions at fat-stock
shows are illegal! And this in spite of the ’fact
that, so far as many of the competitors are con-
cerned, it isn’t mere guessing at all, but a solid
test of a man’s ability to do what every grazier,
stock-dealer and butcher ought to be able to do.
Whether the courts will support the rev. police
is not at all certain.
* * • *
Probably the best-known man on western
N.S.W. rivers, John Egge, died this month. Of
his something over 70 years, 40 had been spent
trading on the Murray and Darling. He was the
Quong Tart of the outback, without the Sydney-
ite’s propensity for making speeches. After Egge
had been trading on the river for yearn, and had
established himself in a pretty big business, the
S.A. Govt, woke up and demanded poll-tax from
him. He paid, of course ; but the amount was
quickly made up by a shilling subscription in
Adelaide and offered to the old man, who, how-
ever, passed it on to a charity. He leaves a
name which thousands of men with whiter skins
might well be glad of.
According to an editorial “reply” in an
English country-paper, the P. and O. steamers
run to Australia, “ whence ferry-boats ply to New
Zealand every quarter of an hour.”

oharacter as their Parliamentary 're-

carrying in its mouth a

affected by the goody-goody
candidate aforementioned.

ner, “scratched
gravel,” then sat down
facing the audience,
and exhibiting the

whereon was chalked,
“Vote for Goody-Goody
and Sunday beer.” There was
, half-a-minute of strained silence,
during which the dog seemed to
have caught the echo of a far-off whistle
for he? pricked up his ears and trotted amiably

The West has developed a new pest for its
flocks in | the common fish-hawk. The hawk’s
specialty lies in ripping up the loins of young
lambs and tearing out their kidneys. On some
stations the lambs are killed in scores.
Quite a feature of Port Augusta
(S.A.)inthe old days was a black
sheep named “King Billy.” He
was used as a decoy to lead mobs of
sheep on board vessels, and when his
woolly friends were safely bestowed,
he would march off the boat and up
street to a friendly publican, who
would fill him up with beer till he
was gloriously drunk. His subse-
quent antics in the back-yard were
the joy of all beholders. He would
sleep off theeffectslike any Christian,
and, when he awoke in his right
frame of mind, would walk home
as decorously as a churchwarden.

“Rep.,” concerning bush
hops: Was recently at one when
the M.C. made himself scarce with
his girl. Another son of the soil
undertook to officiate, but soon the
original M.C. turned up and insisted
on doing battle with his successor. A
ring was formed in the room, and the two
gladiators had a lively five minutes, to be
joined presently by their respective donahs,
who took part in the fray to the apparent
delight of all spectators. The original
M.C. and his partner came off vic-
torious, and the discomfited couple
were “fired.”

“ Gnome ” : At the White Rock mine,
Drake, the main-shaft (abandoned) is down
280 ft., and is “driven” in every direction —there
being about a mile of drives altogether—and the
shaft is full to the brim with water. Well, for
the last four years, every day about ten minutes
to 1 p.m. (time never varies more than two minutes
either way), there is a report like the long rumb-
ling b-r-r oom of a distant explosion sounding
away down in the drives under the water. There
is not, however, the slightest disturbance of the
water-surface; not even a bubble can be seen
rising, although people have watched for an hour
before and after the report. Can any reader
throw a glimmer ?

“ Hannibal ” : The saying “ I’ll chance it,
as Oakes did his oath ” refers to one Oakes, a
Parramatta celebrity, who, tradition tells, was
prosecuting a man for cattle-lifting. Evidence
showed that prisoner had a pair of horns in his
possession, and Oakes, asked if he could swear
that the horns belonged to any beast of his, hesi-
tated for a space—then suddenly burst out :
“ Well, I’ll chance it! Yes ! ”

“ Joe ” to The Bulletin :
Who is looking after the Shearers’ Accommodation Bill
in N.S.W. Assembly? Years ago, that Bill was demanded
—that is to say, a Bill with the same name, and meant to
cover what the words denote. But the Govt.’s scheme
looks like the invention of an enemy, who foresaw some
democrat coming along with a good measure, and winked
to his associates. It goes whelly to machinery-provisions.
Nothing is legislated. The Governor-in-Council may name
districts, may name boards, may name times for applying
this regulation and that—or may go to sleep and not name
anything. No shearing-shed outside districts named by
the Governor-in-Council comes under the Bill. Where
less than six shearers are employed the Bill is not to-apply
at all, whether the Governor-in-Coungil names anything
or anybody or not. It is a solemn fact that this Bill may
pass without making the accommodation of any one
shearer a penn’orth the better. What is the good of it
all?

“ 8.R.” : Re “ Safa’s ” fear (8., 14/9/’01)
that Australia will have to fall back on bear and
’possum. In this town (Junee) we live largely on
rabbits, because, although we are in the centre of
a pastoral district, beef and mutton are “off.”
Also —leaving aside the professional rabbiter —
scores of cookies about here have been making £2
to £3 per week trapping rabbits during the winter
months, and about a quarter-of-a-million pairs
have been sent from here during the last four
months. The people who say that rabbits should
be infected with a contagious disease should be
lynched as enemies of the State. Crick’s legisla-
tion and trapping will keep bunny in sufficient
check.

“Trotter”: In drought-stricken C.Q.,
t’other day, I struck a selector’s humpy; had
with me a sorry, half-starved moke : asked for a
camp. “Well, mister,” was the reply, “I must
charge you a shilling a time; the drought has
ruined me.” He did, too. Flapjack for tea, and
a camp on the hard floor, swarming with fleas.

“ Werrimee ” : Re big cedar (B. 14/9/'01).
The largest cedar log cut in Australia was got in
1885 in the famous cedar scrubs of the upper
Richmond River, NJ3. W. I saw it on the jiggers
in Casino after it was squared, and it was then
12ft. x 12ft.—a girth of 48ft. It was cut from
several feet up the trunk, and its original girth
wouldn’t be much short of 60ft.
A DROUGHT ITEM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Now Paddy was known on Moama side
As a swimmer of great renown,
For he’d beaten the “ Pet ” and the “ Sawmill Pride,”
And most of the blokes in town ;
But Paddy, whose subsequent name was Dunn,
Was slow as a ship on shore,
For he ran to flesh when he tried to run,
And, walking, he ran to more.
Well, things went crook with the Dunn folks’ biz.,
So Paddy procured some stock,
And hawked his wares where the big drought is
With a cart and an old brown crock.
And over the great, grey, bleaching plain
He travelled, without a pal,
Till he camped on the skirt of the Devil’s domain
At the township of Booligal.
Now Paddy had heard that the Lachlan flows
Quite close to tHe~pub. just there,
And he’d dreamed of swimming (without his olothes)
As a luxury rich and rare,
But still (as a true-born Irishman)
He thought of his liquor first.
So he went on his prad to the publican
And started a real good burst.
But, when for the fourteenth time he “ came ”
At a bottle of unknown brand,
A long, lean drover proposed a game
And offered to take a hand ;
And, alas for Paddy, he played and played
Till sobered he seemed to be,
Then-he stopped dead short, and “ Me frind,” he said,
“ It’s the lind yez are havin’ av me ;
“ Ye have bate me bad for a quid or so,
But meself, sure, has played the fool;
Now give me revinge at a game I know
And he wanted to play him pool.
But the drover smiled as he answered Pat—
Whose name at the end was Dunn—
“ I’m out of fettle and rather fat,
Bub I’ll race if you like to run.
11 It's the c nly game that I know save cards,
And I ain’t done much for a while,
But I’ll give you a start of a couple of yards
In anything up to a mile.”
Then Paddy waxed wroth—he was dairy-fed,
And stood only five feet four—
And he called down a curse on the Booligal-bred
As he waddled towards the door.
But, ah 1 a thought of the river came
And a thought of his prowess there,
And, “ Me - frind,” he said, “ Oi will play your game,
And O’ll wallop yez fair and square.
We’ll race from that twisted tree in the bend
’Cross the river and up to the ridge,
Then back to the tree, where the race will end ;
But yez mustn’t go over the bridge."
Then the drover paused ere he placed his stake
In the hands of the publican,
For he wondered awhile if he’d struck a “ fake ”
As he looked at the Irishman ;
But he knew the river and knew the track,
And the doubt cleared away from his brow ;
“ I’m with you,” he said—“ to the ridge and back ;
Will you run the event oft now ? ”
Well, the publican started the race with a “ Go !’’—
The drover away in the lead ;
But Paddy just trotted on easy and slow,
Though the other went on full speed ;
And Ireland was fully ten yards to the bad
As he came to the edge of the bank,
When lo ! with a shout of “ Begorrah 1 Bedad I ”
In the shade of a gum-tree he sank.
’Twas an easy race that the drover won,
And he lifted the stake with glee ;
And he told his pals that “ it took the bun”
When they asked for the historee ;
And they tell of it still in Booligal
When the drovers are “ on the bust
For what in the West a river they call
Is only a streak of dust.

‘ W. 8.” : Met a man t’other day wheeling
His belongings in” a wheelbarrow from Clermont.

“ Veteran ” to The Bulletin :
Re an “ Australian military officer’s ” suggestion
(B. 7/9/’ol)that the perpetrators of the Hawthorn sticking-
up case, and certain other recent crimes of violence
should be sought among returned contingenters. The
S’African war has been carried on in a wav that makes it
an admirable training school for criminals. Europe was
once full of men who had belonged to some “ free
company ” or other (ver3' like an Australian contingent)
and who were “soldiers” when in Government pay, and
robbers when out of it. The men who, in S’Africa, called
themselves “Scouts,” “Fighting Scouts,” or similar
fancy names, are the direct representatives of Trenck’s
Pandours. The regular soldier is habituated to order and
discipline. He fights as a matter of business, does as he
is bid, and does not regard his antagonist as his personal
enemy. As a matter of fact, indeed, when two armies
have been long opposed to each other the soldiers become
quite friendly in the intervals between active hostilities.
They have no enthusiasm. They have ceased to
believe in “glory,” and when that faith is lost, there is
nothing left but “ duty,” which is not a very inspiriting
sentiment.
* * • *
“Ne’er-do-weel” re social gatherings in
the Queensland bush :
I have attended hundreds of social gatherings in the
bush, and can bear testimony that, as a rule, they are
most drearily proper. “ Silvertails ’’congregate at one
end of the room, “ Ooppertails ” at the other, and the
line of demarcation is rarely crossed. The M.O. is, of
course, a free lafice with a roving commission, and, by
virtue of his office, is not so rigidly bound by custom —he
favors either clique if necessary. A good M.O. is an auto-
crat of autocrats. Occasionally circumstances arise to
vary the monotonj' of the proceedings and to provide
every old tabbie in the township and district with a
choice subject of conversation for a week or two.
Once I remember hearing the M.O. —a small, belli-
gerent public official—announce, in a loud tone, that
another couple was wanted to make up the coppertail set
of Lancers. There were only two not dancing at the
time—a dejected-looking coppertail, Who answered to the
name of Smooger, and an auburn-topped damsel, a silver-
tail of the choicest brand of pure merino. The M. 0.,
finding no attention paid to his announcement, glared
round the room and spotted Smooger. From his place in
the silvertail set he yelled Hi! You there 1 Smooger 1
Didn’t you hear me say another couple’s wanted for that
set?” Smooger replied in the same tone : “ Yes, but I
carn’t git no bloomin’ clinah.” “ Gan’t what ? All rot I ”
then, pointing with outstretched arm to Miss Auburn-
head, “ What in thunder’s the matter with Ginger,
there ? ” His strong will conquered, and Smooger and
Miss Aulmrnhead danced the Lancers together in the
coppertail set.
Another time, I remember an indignant coppertail
(male), spitting in the outstretched hand of a silvertail
(female) who had got into the wrong set, and who refused
to “swing” with him. Also, I saw an inebriated male
silvertail prancing round in the ooppertail “ clfain,” gaily
flicking each “ clinah ” in the face with his pockethand-
kerchief and asking her, “ How yer poppin’ up, ducky ?”
These two last incidents led to fights, which were much
enjoyed—the males (of both varieties of metallic tail)
temporarily deserting the ballroom.
To keep a drunken medico away from the hospital ball
w-e put him to bed and removed his clothes. He turned
up at the hall, though, clad only in his singlet, and
wanted to dance. We bad some difficulty in removing
him, but the incident did not seem to seriously disturb
the harmony of the proceedings. I heard one lady re-
mark that she wor‘ ’ never have believed that the doctor
was so’knock-k. ef- if she had not seen fot herself, but
nothing more.’ . welcome these little vagaries J they
make us forgt , nought, dust, dirt, drink, death, and all
the other bush sfoieffi-ior an hour or two.

At Raratonga, a one-horse island annexed
by Bull in 1900, the nigs, have Had prompt ex-
perience of the blessings of civilised rule. Two
officials embezzled between them about £llOO,
which will make a deficit for half-a-century. One
was blown by a rocket to a land where the King’s
writ does not run : t’other has got two years hard
fishing on a small adjacent islet. The stolen cash
was spent in whisky, which probably threw a fur-
ther rosy light on civilisation in Raratonga.
START FOR SPRING STAKES AT KENSINGTON, SYDNEY.
Herewith is given a presentment of the start for the Spring Stakes at the pony meeting of Kensington (Sydney) Race Club To those
never seen a race started with the barrier, the picture will give a good idea of the Australian-minted device by which
sent off with the precision of a troop of cavalry. The term “ pony-race,” as applied to the Kensington event, is a misnomer
the horses which started in the Spring Stakes stand 15 hands, and are sired by the best blood m Australia. The race was won by Freedom,
the grey horse standing on the rails.

“ Vril” : Had a dog poisoned by strych-
nine; his first fit was a “crumpler,” but, on the
advice of a shepherd, I gave the animal \oz. of
tincture of iodine in same amount of water. He
had no more fits, and Recovered. Subsequently
tried this remedy on another poisoned dog after
second fit, and he, too, recovered. What is the
rationale —or is there any ?

“ Scotty ” discourses :
The death-tax is as big a grab as the birth-swindle.
Only lately I have known two cases where the lowest
charges (after haggling) of the undertakers Were £8 and
£6 respectively. The first man was poisoned by raw
whisky on raw oysters, given him by a publican, who
evidently doesn’t know the nature of taking the oath.
Same week a man was billed for £& for a pine-box and
one-horse shay used at the local hospital for his father.
A neighboring squatter was mulct in £lBO for a shift to
Wagga.- That puts one in mind of the Cold Meat Train,
where late revellers sat on coffins so as to save their
leave at Aldershot. But a chum of mine “ had ” them.
He died with a bottle in one hand and a pound in the
other. The inquest collared the quid, but buried him in
Christian ground—if there’s any Christianity on the
Colombo. The cemetery folk make you buy the allot-
ment (30s. in Narandera). I refused to pay for my mate’s
bed, and told them they might dig him up, and I’d plant
him on the Sandhill. Then they let me rail him in (this
was on the Oranko), because it improved the look of the
cemetery.
“ Crespin ” : The
dying away of the boree
and myall trees can
hardly be resultant of
the ring-barking of “the
timber which draws the
rain.” The widespread
theory that timber
draws rain is altogether
unsupported by evi-
dence. The fallacy pro-
bably arises from the
fact that rain draws
timber —a confusion be-
tween cause and effect.
After ring-barking, gut-
ters wash out in the gul-
lies, and all subsequent
developments of the
ground tend to prove
that actually more water
runs off the ground
than when the forests
lived. Consequently it
is hardly possible that
less rain falls. The most
logical conclusion is that
exactly the same amount
falls, but the absence of
living tree-roots renders
it necessary for a certain
proportion of the rain
which was previously
soaked into timber to
now run off the surface
of the ground.

W.H.B. : Your pars might have some chance if
we could read them. Is that your best writing ?... .0.0.:
If there is one ray of light in connection with M’Kinley’s
death it is that the word to rhyme with Ozolgosz has ap-
parently not yet been invented.... Melchior : Moderate
doses of opium are no more hurtful than moderate doses
of tea or tobacco. But how many people are there among
neurotic whites who can regularly take a moderate, com-
forting dose of opium, and not increase it ?... .San Remo:
Any goods imported from, say, England into a State before
the uniform Federal tariff started can’t pass into another
State without paying the difference between the duty
originally paid on them (if any) and the Federal duty (if
any). For instance, the N.S.W. duty on confectionery is
2d. a lb., and, if the Federal duty is 4d., none of the con-
fectionery imported into N.S.W. before the Federal duty
was put on can be sent into any other State without pay-
ing the extra 2d. per lb. duty. This arrangement lasts
for two years, to give time for old stocks to be exhausted.
There must be some such provision, else great stocks of
articles would be laid in wherever they could be imported
free, and sent wholesale into other States when the border
duties were removed, to the damage both of the revenue
and of local industries Jabez : You’re the third person

And thought of the wife she woulds’t be ;
But, alas ! his tale of love he dared not unfurl,

Him into misery.

In the street; a rude shock got he,
Bdr she who once had his brain in whirl
Had grown fat and slovenly.

His escape from misery.

Proclamation (type of a score of obhe»s)
lately issued at Cradock, S’Afriea :
MARTIAL LAW.
NOTICE.
All male adults in the township of Cradock are
hereby ordered to attend in the Market Square to-
morrow morning, at, a quarfcer-to-eleven, to witness
the promulgation of the sentence of death to be
passed on Johannes Petrus Coetzee for high treason
and attempt to murder.
All places of business must be closed from half-past
ten tih after the promulgation of the sentence.
C. C. Wiseman Clarke, Major,
Commandant, Cradock District.
Thursday, July 11,1901.

SYDNEY.


Concerning a much i 'discussed N.S.W. civil-
[vice question
State-Premier See has just promised that .the Govern,
ment will consider the advisableness of bringing in a Bill
this session to authorise repayment of civil servants’ con-
tributions to the Superannuation Fund. The fulfilment
of this promise will he but bare justice. The case stands
thus : The O.S. A,ct of 1884 provided that 4 per cent,
should be deducted from civil service salaries towards a
Superannuation Fund. In ’96, the Govt.—not to benefit
civil servants, but to lessen its responsibilities and to
lighten the burden on the Fund, bankrupt through said
Govt.’s improper use of the money-gave all civil servants
the option of withdrawing by ceasing their contributions.
Many did so, but the Govt., instead of refunding past
contributions, arbitrarily retained and borrowed said
amounts from its employees at 3 per cent, simple interest,
to be paid when the employee leaves the service. It
must be clearly understood that those who retired from
the Fund never reaped any benefit from it while they
were contributing; neither could they, for the 8* Act
provided‘that no pension could be granted on voluntary
retirement from the service, unless the retiring officer
had reached 60 years of age. Therefore, as ex-contributors
didn’t get any benefit in the past, and, furthermore, have
absolutely surrendered all contingent rights or benefits,
why, should their past contributions be withheld 1 No
doubt there are many civil servants who owe money on
which they are paying 10, or perhaps 100, per cent., and
who would therefore be very glad of a refund of the
money on which Govt, generously pays thefn 3 per cent,
simple interest. Even if they lent it to the Government
through the Govt. Savings Bank they would obtain, at
all events, compound interest.
Nervous sufferers \yill find it worth their
while to peruse Herr ltassmussen’s advt. on page
23 of this issue * .

POLITICAL
POINTS
Wanted—lnformation which will lead to
the discovery of the whereabouts of Mr. Gr. H.
Reid.
Kitchener’s stern proclamation—which
was to end the war—has, so far, only been fol-
lowed by two serious British reverses.
Up to the end of July, Britain has paid for
its S’African war this little bill: 15,933 deaths;
558 missing and prisoners ; and 51,828 soldiers
sent home as invalids—total, 68,319.
So far, Australian Old Age Pension Acts
have been most useful to the democracy in showing
with what Cannibal Island callousness most people
in authority regard the infirm and aged.
Cardinal Moran : “We have all the free-
dom and liberty that a Republic can enjoy.”
This, while the papers are filled with daily discus-
sions on the Imperial veto on anti-alien legislation !
A well-known fact. In a Melbourne libel-
action, the other day, Mauger, M.H.R., secretary
of the Protectionist Association, swore that
David Syme was “alwavs consulted” as to the
candidates at elections.
Q. civil servants apprehend an early Black
Wednesday. Only the nearness of the general
elections (early next year) has hitherto stayed
the hand of the destroyer, but the blow will be
forced by a falling revenue and an empty
treasury.
People still wonder why George Adams
so suddenly squelched the agitation against the
Postal Bill started by his Sydney representative.
It is whispered that a “ satisfactory understand-
ing” has been come to, and that Australia will
not lose Tatts., for a while, anyhow.
Straight-goers in Queensland hoped that
the Boodlewraith wouldn’t have the nerve to
reintroduce the “strengthy” Cloncurry-to-Nor-
manton syndicate railway-grab, but, after all,
“me brother Janny ” was only counting noses,
and the late-comers make a certainty of it.
If Cardinal Moran were sincere in his
belief that the State schools are godless instru-
ments of evil, surely he would, by spiritual duresse,
force out of their billets the very many hundreds
of R.C: public-school teachers. It is not logical
to damn the Devil, and then dine with him.
IN THE BY-AND-BY—AT SHADEVILLE
FIRST SPOOK : “ What are the crowd waiting
for? ”
SECOND SPOOK: “Oh, they as mortals were mem-
bers of the first Federal Parliament, and they
are now waiting the long-expected arrival of
one, Turner, who is expected to bring his first
budget.”
Cairns (Q.) Morning Post speaks of the
employment of white men at cane-feeding at the
sugar mills as a “ White Labor Experiment,” and
puts itself away by declaring in a leading article
that though the work is not heavy or arduous, it
has no.faith in the white man’s ability to perform
it. It requires so much “ constant and continuous
attention.” This is a new departure. Hitherto
it was the physical superiority of the piebald
alien that was written up, but now it. turns out
that the trouble lies in the mental incapacity of
the white man and his lack of any fixed purpose.
There is nothing like being cautious.
After President M’Kinley’s death Sydney D.T.’s
financial editor states that it was M ‘Kinley’s
intention to modify the American tariff consider-
ably. The dead President was well into his
second term of office, and it’s curious that he
didn’t mention his intention to anyone but the
D.T. man. Also, when did aTJ.S. President get
power to alter tariffs “ on his own ? ”
Victorians overlook one striking fact about
their awful Leg. Council. Its laziness is appalling,
even for a legislative body, and it is only when
some democratic measure is to be obstructed
that the members trouble to meet seriously.
During the rest of the session they make a
pretence of carrying on business by coming
periodically together, at long intervals, and
adjourning almost immediately afterwards.
This august body met on Tuesday afternoon
of last week, started business about half-an-
hour behind time, and adjourned one-and-
a-half hours later—for a fortnight. Only
one measure, a simple Bill of half-a-dozen
clauses, providing for wild dog destruc-
tion, was dealt with, and the 48 Rich
Landlords were then led out by their
keepers for a 14-days rest, leaving
the country waiting for the legalisa-
tion of half-a-dozen other measures
which lay untouched on the notice-
paper.
Queensland’s Syndicate Ministry last year
passed _ a very liberal Early Closing Act to
square its deal in Syndicate Railways. Now that
the syndicate measures are safe it talks of amend-,
ing the Early Closing Act—in a Tory direction, of
course. The city shop-workers, for their apathy,
deserve to lose the Act altogether.
The Queensland Ministerial organisations
are busy “ Bulcocking ” the electoral rolls. The
metropolitan Bulcocks boast of having knocked
495 Opposition votes off in three electorates, and
put 1300 Ministerial votes on, with 1250 more to
follow. A dissolution after the annual Revision-
court will spell trouble for the Labor Opposition.
Chamberlain’s cable to Barton re the Inter-
State Bill was a crusher to the shipping people.
Australia can thank them for so early establishing
a precedent that she is to be left to manage her
own affairs under the Commonwealth Act. If
anyone has a grievance let him appeal for redress
to the Federal Parliament, not to the Colonial
Office.
West Bourke electorate has returned the
son of its late Tory member, Staughton, to Vic.
Assembly, without opposition, allee same as
though it were an English pocket borough.
Staughton fils, who is a gallant capting, by the
way, “ rejoices to know that his unopposed return
is largely due to the respect felt for his late father,
who left a high standard for him to follow.” The
effect of Capt. Staughton’s trip to S’Africa will be
observed in his figure of speech. One would sup-
pose that the standard he intends to follow is a bit
of bunting on the end of a pole.
Tasmania, having now 11 members to the
Federal Parliament, proposes to practise rigid
economy by taking
four members
from its State
Parliament.
Premier Lewis
suggests reduc-
ing the Assem-
bly from 38 to
35 (five elector-
ates returning
seven members
each), and the
Council from 19
to 18 (the State to
be one electorate).
Now what in the name
of all that is huge and
bulgy and ridiculous
does Tasmania, or
any other State for
that matter, want
with four Houses of
Parliament (two Fe-
deral and two local)
and two governors,
and four general elec-
tions (for the Federal
House of Representa-
tives, Federal Senate,
State House of Assem-
bly and State Legis-
lative Council) ? This
sort of thing is calcu-
lated to bring the
whole Parliamentary
system into derision.
Moreover it is wholly contrary to British prece-
dent. There is only one other Federation in the
British empire (Canada) and six out of its eight
divisions have only one House of State Legisla-
ture. This attempt on the part of ill-disposed
billet-hunters to subvert a good British tradition
and introduce the worst features of American
politics into Australia can’t be too severely jumped
upon.
Again and again the claim of ecclesiastics
to sit in Parliament is advanced. The See-O’Sul-
livan-Crick rule revives the stormy topic in
N.S.W. That clerics should so sit is wholly
within constitutional right, and an Executive
minute is the only thing needed. Bishop
Broughton was a member of Council; Bishop
Polding was nominated.
Let every Australian go down on his knees
and thank Heaven that there was no shooting
whilst the Duke of York was here, for anything of
the sort would have again riven the community
for a generation. However, had the tour been
under advanced Continental stage-management

there would most certainly have been an ‘ ‘ attempt ”
in order to thaw the frost which was blighting

“ T.D.” : Parliamentary adjournments
over the deaths of distant rulers are a holy sham.
When the Victorian Houses expressed formal woe
at President M ‘Kinley’s tragic end, I purposely
made the round of the theatres, and found 27
mourning legislators, 11 of whom had gone to the
Bijou to join with Marie Lloyd in shedding tears.
In addition, a few were, no doubt, weeping in
pubs, and other places.
“It is time to show the Boers,” says
Joseph Chamberlain, “ that they cannot continue
to make war at little risk to themselves and with
no risk whatever to their families.” There was a
time when it .was said to be an Englishman’s
pride that he didn’t make war on women—and so
it is yet with most Englishmen. Joseph, the
Screw-maker, after all, is only a capable savage
who has strayed accidentally into the wrong
century.
It was by the “ ratting ” of two so-called
Labor members—Roberts and Poynton—that the
Kingston Govt, was thrown out in S.A. Yet
now the Labor members are constantly bemoaning
the absence of Kingston from State politics. He
had pledged himself to forego his Federal ambi-
tions until household suffrage was made the basis
of the Legislative Council franchise, and it was
only the dismissal of his Ministry which relieved
him from this undertaking.
Dan O’Connor (his eye on the R.C. vote)
gave notice in N.S.W. Assembly last week of a
motion favoring State aid to religious schools.
Jessep (his eye on the L.O.L. vote) promptly
asked the intentions of the Government. Educa-
tion-Minister Perry (showing that Crick hasn’t
converted them yet) retorted with emphasis—No,
that they were not going to revert to “the cus-
toms of the dark ages”—which was quite a suf-
ficiently offensive way of putting it.
Gov.-General Hopetoun, if a common-
sense interpretation can be put on his words, is
coming back from his brother-in-law’s North
Queensland plantation much impressed with what
he has been told by planters about the
value of nigger labor, and prepared to give the
benefit of his impressions to Ministers. The
Bulletin would be sorry to see Hopetoun make
such an egregious blunder, and hopes that he will
keep his views on black labor carefully to
himself.
A. G. Downer, of S.A., got up the other
day to express his grief—
That miserable fears should be entertained lest after
all their (the Snow tramway grab crowd’s) enterprise
they should make something out of it.
Yet if A. G. Downer found a burglar in the
middle of his sixth hour’s enterprise at breaking
his (Downer’s) safe, he would probably have such
a miserable fear lest the burglar should make
something out of it that he would run like an
antelope in search of the police.
Methven, M.L.A., proposes to ask the
opinion of Vic. Parliament on the proposal that
the House of 48 Rich Landlords shall be reformed
by allowing all Leg. Council electors to be
qualified to sit in the Council. The idea is one
which The Bulletin has advocated for years.
The Council nominally represents 129,000 Vic.
electors. It says proudly that they comprise the
intelligence of the community, and its claim to
consideration rests on the fact that it speaks for
129,000 of the brightest, best-educated, and most
capable citizens. Yet 127,000 of these are abso-
lutely disqualified from sitting in the Council ! If
they are as intelligent as the House of Landlords
alleges, then they are quite fit to sit in that House.
If they are so dull, ignorant, and mentally deficient
that they aren’t fit to sit there, then the House of
48 Rich Landlords, as the representative princi-
pally of 127,000 fools, shouldn’t be listened to.
Somers Vine and his precious Papuan
syndicate have not been heard of for some time
but an indistinct rumble of gossip would indicate
that the gang’s claim for compensa-
tion is to soon re-appear as cur-
rent history. The death of
Premier Byrnes was bad luck
for Vine, and the death of Dick-
son more bad luck, but Philp ia
of the stuff that syndicates trust
to, and so there is a little balm in
Gilead. The “ honor of Queensland”
is to be the cry. But N.S.W. and Vic-
toria did not participate in pledging
Queensland’s “honor,” and are therefore
under no obligation to redeem it. Again,
Queensland did not pledge its own “honor” ;
it was the Nelsons, the Dicksons, and the
Byrnes that, at the back of Queensland, pub
their heads in the exploiters’ halter
Cardinal Moran, vexed at N.S.W. Minister
Perry’s perky remark that denominational educa-
tion was “a relic of the dark ages,” remarks that
in N.S.W. the Minister of Public Instruction is
“ a mere political agent, knowing nothing of the
work of education.” It used to be the gibe that a
man got the Education portfolio in order to attend
to his own education —a gibe justified by one
Education Minister, who wrote “pigs” with two
“g’s”—piggs. By-the-way, Victoria once had an
Education Minister who spelt “ recommend ” with
one “m,” and “until” with two “l’s”—and he
was a “Major,” too.
At the beginning of the Boei war, when
it was reported (though the report proved to be
wholly false) that the Boers were going to destroy
the Johannesburg mines, what a howl of indigna-
tion went up in Britain about the sacredness of
private property, and the rules of modern warfare
which forbade its destruction, and the utter
savagery of any nation which would dream of such
a thing ! And now that the British are destroy-
ing private property wholesale, and making the
whole country a blackened desert—well, there is
no howl now, and the alleged rules of modern
warfare are off, and the utter savagery of the
business has dropped quite out of sight.
As showing what Australia has to expect
from the dear old Mother Country (this half-
brick consists of extracts from a speech by the
British Chancellor of the Exchequer, printed in
Bradstreet’s, on the propoal to give a prefer-
ence to colonial products) :
There was, the Chancellor said, a strong, feeling- on this
subject in Germany, and the acceptance of the amend-
ment would mean running a most serious risk of losing
the favored-nation treatment Great Britain now enjoyed
in Germany. . . . The Chancellor declared that he was
not prepared to risk the loss of Great Britain’s foreign,
trade, which was greater than the trade with the colonies.
When the Empire is again in danger the kawlinies-
had best tell Britain to seek soldiers from her
foreign traders in Deutschland.
The truth is a great thing, but good, solid,
concentrated lying gets ahead of it three-fourths
of the time. The crucial clause in the Federal
Immigration Restriction Bill sets forth that every
immigrant must show his ability to write 50 words
of English WHEN called upon to do so, but every
nigger-loving paper and every Opposition paper-
in Australia declared that the Bill said every
immigrant must show his knowledge of English
whether he was called upon to do so or not,
and lamented about the alleged intention to-
exclude French and German and Scandinavian
immigrants. Apparently, the greater part of
Australia believes this silly invention by now-
all through dull, monotonous, persistent iteration.
Straight talk from Wellington (M.L.)
Post —which, with other Maoriland papers,
strongly suspects Premier Seddon of muting for
an Imperial billet
If it were to become an understood thrng that, che
Imperial Government could and would reward colonial
Ministers over and above what they could obtain from,
the suffrages of their fellow citizens in their own colonies,
an insidious blow would be struck at the independence of
colonial public life. The statesmen of Greater Britain
would not be able to keep a single eye upon the interests
of their own countries ; they would naturally feel that
the Imperial authorities must be considered at least as
much as the people who placed them in office. There
would be a tendency to use colonial politics as a means of
Imperial advancement and not, as they should be, as a
means of increasing the prosperity of the colony in,
question.
And this applies just as strongly to K.C.M.G.-
hunger as it does to Imperial billet-hunting.
An American gentleman, Eugene F. Loud,
transpired in Sydney last week and proceeded,
per medium of Sydney D.T., to give this country
some highly-superior advice
In my opinion you have here in Australia, with your-
State-owned railways, tramways and telegraphic services-
and so on, the embryonic elements which make for the
stifling of this personal incentive towards progress. . . .
I hear that the Government here finds work for the people
out of the public funds. You will, however, find that you
cannot go on doing that sort of thing long.
And this gentleman comes from a country where
nearly one-fourth of the public funds go, not to
find work for the people, but to pay people for
doing nothing. The U.S. bill for war-pensions
runs to nearly £30,000,000 a year—allowance to the
alleged widow of the man who stubbed his toe on a
root in the Mexican war 60 years ago, and such
like. It isn’t finding work for the people out of
the public funds, but the raising of public funds
by loans instead of taxation, that is the matter,
with Australia.

A resident of Magill, S.A., has added his testi-
mony to the many others in praise of this wonder-
ful remedy. He says : “ A member of my family
was suffering from debility, and became so weak
and ill that the carrying out of daily duties became
a burden, and at length impossible. Medicine did
not appear to do any good, and a change of air was
suggested, but as this was impossible under exist-
ing circumstances, I looked for something else to
afford relief. In looking' over a paper one evening
the words ‘Try Vitadatio’ caught my eye. I did
so, and after the sufferer had taken the first bottle
an improvement was noticeable, and three other
bottles effected a complete cure. This was 2 years
ago, and there has been no return of the illness
since. I have recommended Vitadatio to several
similarly afflicted, and the same good results have
followed.” HARRY CRAKER.

S.A. Govt, has so far repurchased 39,281
acres for closer settlement, at a cost of £81,367. It
has sub-divided this among 249 lessees, and gets
£2915 per annum in rent —equal to £3 11s. Bd. per
cent, per annum on its outlay. The return is
really more than this, for the purchase money
included improvements which the new lessees pay
for separately by instalments. Probably trie
State gets rather over £4 per cent, on its outlay,
and gains a number of new taxpayers as well.
From the British Medical Journal:
The open-air treatment of consumption, of which we
are hearing much at the present day, is . . . bidding fair
to come under the baneful influence of routine. . . .
What, think you, does the consumptive and his friend
see in this ? He sees a residence for a few months in a
Home, and a cure at the end of it. Is that what he has
any chance of obtaining? Certainly not, and in propor-
tion to the exaggerated hope will come the bi tterness of the
disappointment to the sick, and the discredit to us. The
benefit to be obtained in these sanatoria is that there will
be learned a habit of life —what we mean by plenty of
good food and plenty of fresh air ; and having learned his
lesson, the tuberculous man will need to practise it all
the rest of his life. There is no cure in this treatment,
as the sick man understands cure ; for although it is true
that there is no disease that is more often arrested than
phthisis, it is equally true that there is no disease that
has a more inveterate tendency to relapse, and I very
much fear that when you come to strike the balance
between arrest and relapse, the latter has the best of it.
Therefore, if the open-air treatment is to take its real
place, and be of any abiding value, the principles of the
sanatorium must be introduced into the home.
