SPORTING NOTIONS.
There was. a lovely mix-up at Randwick on
Saturday. Eulowrie finished first in the Flying Stakes,
and was subsequently disqualified because trained by
an unlicensed trainer. But he was not disqualified
till he had weighed in and the few cash bets
made—he was a rank outsider—had been settled.
Oxenham’s T.M. S., the second horse, was given the
stake, and ’twas decided that bets remain unaffected.
Rule 28 makes it clear Eulowrie should never have been
allowed to start, but rule 2, governing bets, compli-
cated the matter somewhat. Nevertheless, Eulowrie had
“transgressed the rules of racing” by starting when he
had no right to, and he was legally disqualified from com-
pet'ng at all. So why Eulowrie’s backers should receive
and T.M.S.’s pay when their horse was adjudged the
winner, and beat all horses who were qualified to start,
is a question that puzzles the racing public greatl}'.
The A.J.C. will probably constitute itself a Full Court
to hear the appeal ; but in the meantime Mo has paid
out the few sovs. he laid against Eulowrie, and if the
judgment goes against him there will be a howl that,
having paid out on one winner, it is calculated to scare
capital out of the country, and lead to the utter destruc-
tion of racing and the absolute ruin of the ring, and is
also against all principles of justice and religion to be
compelled,to pay out on two horses—when he has already
paid out on the cheapest.
• • * »
Barragunda (Rand wick’s second sensation on
Saturday—Eulowrie was the other) is a large, handsome,
four-year-old by Bill of Portland from Melodious, the dam
of Wallace, and fetched a big price at the breaking-up of
the late W. R. Wilson’s stud. Many judges picked him
as the best of the lot. Saturday’s was his initial win.
* * * »■
The recent accidental drowning of the owner
and one of the crew of the ketch Rachel Thompson, on
the Tas. east coast, recalls a few old-time happenings.
The ketch, a 15-ton ner, was built for bookmaker Joe
Thompson as a cruising-yacht, and named after his wife.
Joe subsequently gave the craft to his wife’s brothers,
Lou and Dan Jones, two daredevil young Tas. fisher-
men, and Lou sailed the Rachel continuously until
drowned the other day. During a big blow they anchored
the ketch, and he, with two others, tried to reach shore
in the dingey, which was swamped—two of the three
perishing. The genial Lou was a great favorite with the
fisher-folk of the Flyspeck, and many a yarn is told about
him. On one occasion he sailed from Tas. for Sydney
with several tons of trumpeter, all-alive-oh in the “ well.”
Lou had never been in Sydney before, and knew nothing
of scientific navigation, so, like Captain Cook, he missed
the Heads altogether and wandered away up North for
about a week. Then he came South again, and, seeing a
steamer which was ahead of him suddenly disappear,
stem-on, into the cliffs, he decided to follow “on spec.”
Next morning the cargo was in Sydney Fish Market;
and, as the trumpeter had been all along carefully fed,
they were in good order and realised high prices.
♦ ♦ * •
Holy Church took a deep interest in the recent
A.J.C. Spring Meeting. If Mr. Eden George’s Kenley
won the Epsom £lOO was, as per the owner’s promise, to
go to the Cardinal. If Mr. Crick’s First Consul scored a
win his Eminence benefited to the same extent The
Church lost on Kenley, but won on First Consul. This
sort of thing should be encouraged to extend. No R.O.
horse-owner would imperil his immortal soul by running
stiff a horse on which the Church had got its beans.
* • ♦ •
Luck again. A Riverina Mo got a license to
bet at recent A.J.C. meeting, but jibbed at the last
moment, an.d punted instead. His haul totted up ’tween
six and eight thousand, and he reckons changing his
mind enriched him to the extent of at least £lO,OOO, as,
had he donned the bag, it would have cost him all £2OOO.
* * * •
The practice of naming racehorses after pro-
minent local men is increasing and should be stopped at
once. “ John See” and “ Crick” were prominently before
the public on the race-track during the last week or two.
A future Melbourne Cup might be run for by “ Cardinal
Moran,” “ Dill Macky,” and “ John Norton.” A man has
a right to his own name, and a right to demand that its
pirating should not make him liable .to ridicule and
derision.
“Ancient”: Jockey Frank Kuhn’s nine wins
at recent A.J.C. gathering recalls that T. Hales, at V.R.O.
Autumn meeting in ’BB, had 14 mounts and got home 11
times. Four of them were on the opening day, and in
succession. At Sydney City Tatts.’ races, Saturday last,
Kuhn was out four times, but didn’t get a “drum” till
he won the last on Barragunda. His big crowd of
followers conseauently unloaded much of their A.J.C.
winnings.
• » • *
Kogarah trainer-owner Andy Gollan, who
brought Australian Star to light and then had a parlous
period till he won a Tatt’s sweep on S.A. Cup, has another
promising nag in Lady Grafton—half sister to Crosslake.
She owned City Tatt.’s Trial Handicap, Saturday, from
end to end, and will do heaps better when asked.
.** * »
San Fran was sold last week for £2500. Late
owner. Smith offered the horse to an English buyer for
£l7OO, but the vet.’s report was adverse. Now the man
from Tucka Tucka has got £BOO more and won £3OOO
Erize-money in the interim. The price is big, but unless
a Carabine is at her zenith, San Fran promises well for
most of the coming Vic. long distance w.f.a. races.
■fr * ♦ *
Lucknow, Kelso’s latest acquisition, is fancied
by many for Caulfield Cup. Was bred by the Rev.
Winter, of Rylstone (N S.W.), out of a buggy mare, whose
origin, except that her sire was a son of Kingston, is com-
pletely lost. He first attracted attention racing for
bridles and such. Original owner sold him for £l2 ;
Kelso bought him for £4OO, and has already done well out
of the investment. ,
**■ • »
Thoroughbred stallion Correze dropped dead
at Cumnock (N.S.W.), after being in his new
owner’s possession only a few days. He was by Newmin-
ster from Ouida, by Yattendon from My Fancy, sister
to My Love and Lady Emma. His career was brilliant:
A.J.C. December Stakes at two years, and following
season he ran third to Carbine in Melb. Cup ; won V.R.O.
Handicap (lfm.) in record time,of 3.3 ;an extra fast run
A.J.C. St. Leger; won Sydney Mail champion prize of £l5O
at N.S.W. Agricultural Society’s Show in 1900. Best of
his progeny, so far, are First Consul, T.M.S. and Zurich,
winner of Q.T.O. St. Leger. Correze was a hot-pot forMal-
volio’s Cup. Had the big thing pome off, then owner
Jack M'Laughlin would have landed quite a big fortune.
But it didn’t.
* • ■* *
Wren’s sly tote, conducted so successfully at
Melbourne despite the law, must often make the gentle
punter yearn for its legalisation. When Tilbury won at
Caulfield t’other day the books’ price was Bto 1. The
tote paid 45 to 1, and 24 to 1 for a place ; and when, pre-
viously, another horse won on same course, the books
laid Bto 1, and the tote paid 44. No wonder Ikey Mo culti-
vates the parson, and sometimes even goes to church.
• * • *
Adelaidesportsmen are wondering whether, after
the Federal Govt, has suppressed Tasmanian Tattersall’s,
it will be encouraged to try its hand on the destruction
of the S.A. Totalisator. One thing seems just about as
rational as the other. But The Bulletin is almost
tempted to predict that “ Tatt ” will not be suppressed
after all.
• • • *
The sly-tote has reached such dimensions in
Adelaide that the racing clubs begin to fear loss of busi-
ness. Clients of the sly-tote don’t attend meetings, but
collect their divs. directly after the names are posted at
the news-offices. Whereas the racing machine only pays
on first horse, the sly-tote pays on one, two, and three.
As this is apparently more popular with the public,
S.A.J.C. and A.R.O. have decided to split divs. between
first and second at a ratio of 66 and 33 per cent, as an
experiment, provided the police raise no objection.
V.R.C. Sec. Byron Moore, summing up racing
in Victoria, says when he first joined the club, the
owners pooled £13,585 to get £lO,OOO added money. Last
season, they contributed £11,287 to win £32,807, which
means that where, one time, every £ won cost owners
about 275., it now only calls for an outlay of 7s.
* • * *
Sydney cricket season has started. Charley
Turner will bowl again for the Sydney team. Paddington
should have a mortgage on the premiership. But if the
idea of carrying out the programme irrespective of what
“ big ’’ matches are going on is adhered to, the team will
be handicapped, since Noble, Trumper and Kelly, who
form its backbone, will be picked in any inter-State or
international fixtures.
* * • *
Bowler Blythe, of Maclaren’s team, promises to
be the merest passenger, as far as their batting is con-
cerned. The 10th man in of most All-England elevens
has rarely given the Australians any trouble, but Blythe
is a phenomenal duffer. During the past English season,
when big scores were the rule rather than the exception,
Blythe made an average of six.
* • • *
The only W.G., at 54, and scaling 18st., is still
a marvel. Recently, for Crystal Palace against Wiltshire,
he knocked up 188 of his side’s 366.
* * * *
The startlingly-sudden death of footballer
W. A. Shortland, was ascribed to influenza and kidney
trouble, but it has since transpired that he was kicked in
the back at the Invercargill match, and often had to be
rubbed to ease the stiffness. He played for City against
Country in ’97, ’9B, ’99 ; for N.S.W. against M.L. in ’97 and
present season, and against Queensland in ’97, ’9B, ’99, ’OO
and ’01; also against England. His field position was
five-eighths. He was also a very capable cricketer, having
put up 155 for Sydney against Melbourne ’Varsity three
years ago. A Bachelor of Engineering with honors, he
held a good position on the staff of the Sydney W. and S.
Board.
* * • •
Costello, captain of N.S.W. football team to
M. was kicked on almost the same spot in the head in
the first and last matches at Wellington, and on the last
occasion so badly injured that he had to remain behind.
When accidents happen about once in 10 years in boxing
there’s a yowl for the suppression of the sport, but
nobody ever dreams of suppressing football, though every
season has its dead and wounded roll.
* * * *
“Spring”: The disasters which overtook the
N. footballers in Maoriland were expected by
the few who knew that the selectors aimed at making
the tour a “social success.” The M.L. forwards
are weighty bullockers, whose methods smashed
up the N.S.W. men—lamb-like gentlemen whom N.S.W.
sent over because they could talk good “ grammar.”
Poor Shortland got a kick which went a long
way to kill nim Costello lies dangerously ill in Welling-
ton from cranial injuries ; and several prominent players
swear they will never play the game again. Next tour
the N.S.W. forwards should be men of the carcase-butcher
type who won’t distinguish between collaring a Maori-
land forward and the side of a guessing-competition
bullock.
* * • ♦
Crack ’Murkan middle-weight, Jim Scanlon,
descended upon Sydney, last week, in search of scalps.
He carried those of two 11.4 men, Mick Oreedon
and Jim Ryan. Oreedon has been on the down-grade
for years, and Ryan was not what he used to be, there-
fore these victories don’t prove very much. Still, Scanlon
looks remarkably fit, and (good sign) skites not at all.
He is willing to fight anyone for anything, stake, gate, or
both combined. Armidale pug., M'Coll, who had gone
back to maintenance work, has stowed away his tools,
and will meet the invader at Newcastle, where a good
gate is assured. Mac will scale 11.8. Scanlon is willing,
and, if he wipes the floor with Mac, will reach for Stur-
geon’s hair. After him, Tut Ryan and heavy premier Bill
Doherty, physical disparity notwithstanding'. Altogether,
Scanlan has a delightful little programme before him.
* • • *
Some weeks ago, Pyrmont (Syd.) pug. Frost
made mincemeat of the lightweight Riley, who, fresh
from ’Frisco, lacked preparation. On Saturday night
they again collided cheerfully at the Golden Gate
(Sydney). Riley was bigger, but not stronger, than
the Cornstalk, and he bashed Frost heavily about the
ribs, and bustled him with Yankee tricks. Frost
retorted with smashes across the lower countenance
generally. The ’Friscan ma’de picturesque, bull-like
rushes, which bore Frost on to the ropes, where the
Yankee smashed savagely at a rampart of forearms and
elbows around the face, regardless of the defenceless
bingey below. Frost’s head bumped the ring-post, and,
after squinting round to see who’d struck him, he got his
face back just in time to stop Riley’s elbow. There was
a whirling scramble on the ropes, and a wild hook just
skimmed the face of an enthusiastic barracker who had
put his nose too far into the scrum. In round eight Riley
commenced to tire, and in the 11th a swirling right
smashed him all up. Frost fights too intelligently for the
Yankee.
* * • *
Bruising management is a low-down game. In
Melbourne, recently, Abe Hicken was matched against
George O’Neil. Abe is a tottering old man now, who
ought to be worrying about old-age pensions instead of
boxing matches. Over 40 years ago he won the ’Murkan
lightweight championship, and nearly 25 years ago Larry
Foley downed him at Echuca (Vic.). O’Neil is younger
and more vigorous, but a 30s. gate squelched the dis-
graceful affair—the ancients were not going to fight for
barely enough to pay for steak and sticking-plaster.
* * * •
London Referee’s tribute to poor, black-bodied,
white-hearted Peter : “Mostßefereereaderswereprepared
to hear of Peter Jackson’s death. He was, so far as we
knew him in England, a gentleman—a nobleman in all his
dealings ; one of the finest boxers ever seen, and as brave
as he was strong. Few heavy-weights nowadays ever are
as good after a big fight as before it. One hard one is
enough. Jackson might have gone on but for being feted
so greatly. Probably he never recovered from that awful
set-to with Slavin.”
* * * *
One-time top-notch bruiser Tom Lees has re-
appeared in the British ring, and at a trial match badly
clawed an English amateur champion. Lee is now well
into the 40’s, and when he fought Jackson in the White
Horse'Gym., George-st., Sydney, he showed splendid
sprinting powers, powers that would have made a fortune
in the then-flourishing Carrington and Botany Handicaps.
His last bashing was from English middleweight Jack
Scales, who simply walloped him flat.
• • * •
Judging by N.S.W. Rowing Association’s
Regatta, Saturday, amateur aquatics are done, although
Towns’victory may boom the professional branch some-
what. On Saturday interest was tepid, entries poor, and
racing very mediocre. Few clubs were represented, and
the Champion Eights were won in a hollow fashion from
’Varsity by Glebe. Ten-stone Daley’s win of the Premier
Sculls was a mere paddle all the way from his club chum,
Lichtscheindl (10.4) of the Mercs.
* * * •
Raymond Terrace (N.S.W.), Towns’ habitat,
jubilated twice over its man’s defeat of Gaudaur. On the
day originally fixed for the race a Sydney daily got a wire
from its local correspondent announcing that “ Raymond
Terrace received with great joy the news of the victory” ;
yet the race had not been rowed. When the event had
been decided in Towns’ favor, the Terrace filled ’em up
again. *
* * » *
N.S.W. long-distance runner “Wally” York
won the three-mile supremacy at Redfern (Sydney)
Saturday. Grindal led until the last quarter, when York
came with a spurt that set /OQO onlookers cheering madly
as he went home 50 yards in front. Time, 16.9,-42 seconds
slower than W. J. Burk’s Dunedin record of eight years
ago. Only a few days previously he won the N.S.W.
Cross Country Championship, and yet a year or so ago he
was bedridden with rheumatism and seemed a confirmed
invalid for life.
Spider-legged, pancake-chested section of Vic.
athletes had a day out at Caulfield, Saturday, in the 10-
miles Cross-country Premiership. Essendon rep. Stillwell
got home in 62min. 4sec., with R. A. Ferguson ssec.
slower (second), and D. M'Donald lmin. 4sec. further
away, third. Last year’s premier team (East Melbourne)
scores again—B points better than Essendon.

Professional wheeling may revive this season.
A big contingent of Yankee wheelers are coming per s.s.
Ventura, including champion Kramer, Canuck M‘Leod,
Floyd M'Farland, Ivor Lawson, J. Chapman and W. B.
Vaughan, in company with Australian and English cracks,
Walne and Green.
• * * *
Ponderous Sydney D.T. bikist, Arthur Smythe,
on Saturday did the half-mile in 1.3 1-5, which is 2-5
inside his own previous best. He has now all unpaced
records up to 10 miles, and is training for a raid on the
amateur paced ones.
At Poverty Point.
The red-haired super, came out to adjust the concert
grand. A woman in the stalls : “ That’s him ! ” Her
escort: “No fear ! He’s red-headed. He can’t play ! ”
Query : Oan’t red-headed men play pianos ? Any instances
to the contrary available?
Adorable Catherine Bartho, formerly primibre danseuse
in the Firm’s “ Blue Ballet,” made her first appearance
at London Palace Theatre, t’other week, and
knocked ’em with “ some excellent cake-walk business on
her toes.” The Russian girl is billed as Miss Bartho, and
one supposes that she is not so Russian nowadays as when
she used to be interviewed through her duenna and in-
terpretess by Australian pressmen. A Melb. Age press-
man told a pleasing story of how his interview with
Bartho led to a trial of her alleged wonderful wrist-
strength, which ended in the wily charmer falling
“ beaten ” against his manly chest. She was wearing her
scanty ballet costume, and the journalist enjoyed his
victory immensely, but he fancied that Bartho let him
win because it suited her better to fail against his chest
than to reverse the result.
A benefit for Tom Kennedy, sec. of the Melb. Distressed
Actors’ Fund, is now afoot. The injury that Kennedy
sustained through falling down a restaurant cellar-hole
was even more serious than it seemed at first, for, after
being about for some months with his arm in a sling, he
has had to undergo a serious operation.
If Melba’s voice were as big as her head ! She has been
telling the papers that her fee for an “at-home” is 500
guineas, and her condition—“ I don’t want to meet any
young man. I don’t want to meet any poor man ; I don’t
want to meet any stupid man. I don’t want to meet
many women, and I don’t want to meet any who are not
lively, well-dressed, and brilliant.” From manager J. C.
Williamson she asks £5OO a night to come to Australia—
her own dear country, her beloved Australia!—and
though J.O.W. is a great sportsman, he doesn’t feel
equal to that.
“0.C.” : An original touch about “ Lady Huntworth’s
Experiment ” is that which makes the heroine 40 years of
age, and still sought after. When the disguised cook
begins her story of marital misery with the remark
“ When I married Lord Huntworth, I was” —the astute
theatre-goer expects the next words tobe—“ ayoung and
innocent girl,” and is agreeably disappointed when the
concluding observation is “a woman of 30 who had spent
her youth in nursing a sick father.” A realistic touch.
The mature woman who has lived a secluded life is far
more likely to make a foolish marriage than the aforesaid
young and innocent girl, who has only the ignorance of
youth to mislead her, and not the added ignorance
acquired by 10 years’ reading of mental “ soft tack,” to a
decaying wreck. Lady Huntworth seems not to have had
any proposals till she was 30, and then an unsteady peer,
who used coarse metaphors, such as “ taking one’s pigs to
market,” annexed her, no one else apparently being to the
fore. Disguised as a cook, however, and with 10 more
years to her age, Lady Huntworth has whole households
at her feet. Lady Huntworth is a good cook, though
absent-minded. The moral is obvious. Hope is never
wholly lost for the woman who can cook.
Harry Leader, “ the well-known Australian dramatist,”
has a 20min. “sketch” at a London hall. The sketch
seems quite new and original in its powerful absurdity
It shows how a young husband shoots his mother dead,
she having broken into his wife’s bedroom disguised as a
man, with intent to wreck the bride’s reputation ! But
who is the well-known H. Leader, anyhow ?
One Great and Majestic Actor, now in Australia, has
not endeared himself to his brethren by his method of
receiving (and dismissing) the mere ordinary actor who
applies for a billet. The interviews usually take place
in the Great One’s dressing-room what time he is
“ making up ” and are punctuated by hand-mirror exer-
cises and the flourishing of grease-paint sticks. A recent
encounter was not without humor. Ordinary Actor
enters. The Great One does not turn rou> d, but con-
tinues to dab his face with a towel-end. O.A. : “Good
evening, Mr. Greatactor. I hear you are in need of a
second ‘lead.’ I beg to, &c.” G.A. : “Ah! um—yes.
(To dresser) Heat another No. 2. Who have you been
with?” O.A. : “Mr. Soanso and Mr. Suchausuch.”
G.A.: “Ah! um—slippers—red ones !—Yes ; what
salary?” O.A. : “Well, I’ve never got less than £lO a
week.” (Pause.) G.A. : “Ye-es {rouge). Ah! (towel).
Shut—the door after you, will you?”
Elliot Galer, the one-time popular English operatic
tenor, who died in June last, made his first essay with a
co. which included Lucy Escott, Aug. Braham, and
Aynsley Cook. When the Pyne and Harrison Opera Co.
was being formed Galer wrote to Harrison with commend-
able brevity : “ Dear Harrison, I want an engagement.
Yours truly, Elliot Galer.” Harrison was equally short:
“ Dear Galer, I’m sorry to. hear it. Yours truly, William
Harrison."
Strange that the paragraphists have made little or no
allusion to Walter Everard in connection with “ The
Message from Mars ” co. The dapper Walter, who is finan-
cially interested in the tour, came out from London some
six months ago, and his irreproachable belltopper adorns
the front of the house. Seems that after playing for four
consecutive years in Penle.v’s original London production
of “ Charley’s Aunt,” he broke loose from the stage, and
ran a military journal for three years on the noblest
advertising principles. Time flies. The Everard who
made his first appearance at the old Melbourne Opera
House (Oct. 9, 1886) in “ Turned Up,” under the manage-
ment of Brough, Boucicault, and Harwood, has allowed
his hair to grow white over the ears, but he looks capab'e
of being just as young as ever behind the footlights. By
the way, how comes it that this brisk comedian has been
back in Australia for six months without notifying his
presence from the stage ? If Walter Everard were to re-
introduce himself to a Melbourne audience, he would
probably be surprised at the warmth of his reception.
Lots of people remember “ Turned Up ” and “ Our Regi-
ment,” and the lapses into Boucicaultian drama of the
pioneer B. and B. Co. Everard was the interesting young
man in those early shows, and the [girls viewed him
approvingly. Also, he appeared as a highly genteel
Ferdinand in Rignold’s production of “ The Tempest ” at
Melb. Royal.

The usual protracted stone - throwing
mystery at Manilla (N.S.W.) has been cleared
up in the usual way by the confession of the usual
little girls. At a certain age the spirit moves
very many little girls to throw stones (or accusa-
tions in regard to their honor) about in a careless
and unreasoning fashion. Psychological medicos
will soon have to give very serious attention
indeed to the young-hussey problem.
The Yic. Pecksniff Govt., threatened with
further trouble over its Convention Bill, talks of
bringing in a proposal to test the sincerity of the
numerous Tories who “want Parliamentary
reform, but don’t want to be interfered with by a
lot of outsiders.” Of course, the reform will be
jumped on just as heavily as the Convention Bill
itself, but its refusal would strengthen Smiler
Peacock’s hand wonderfully, if he really wants it
strengthened—a matter of considerable doubt.
“ Housework.’
Housework seems easy to a man, but there is a
great deal of lifting and reaching to do, a great
many trips up and down stairs to make in the
course of a day’s housework. It’s hard when a
women is well—for a woman suffering from some
form of disease it is daily torment. There are
thousands of such women struggling along, day by
day, in increasing misery. There are other
thousands who have found
a complete cure of their
ailments in the use of Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills.

These pains not only caused

and confined me to my bed.

a different woman. I find
they are a very good pill,
acting mildly and search-
ingly, benefiting the whole

needing sucli a medicine.”

Editor Bulletin. —Re “G.A.P’s” par.
(B. 14/9/01). I will allow you, or any other
responsible party, to select for treatment a person
of unquestionable baldness—no matter of how

The Rotorua district (M.L.) has developed
a new geyser of huge proportions. The liquid
surface is a quarter-acre in extent, and the up-
heaval, when the thing “performs,” can be seen
miles away.
The prohibition of the adulteration of wine
with potato-spirit was only passed by the Federal
House of Reps, by a majority of one ! Which
shows that if the people want to get rid of adulter-
ation they’ll have to make the matter a hustings
question.

The Ides of September have passed, but
De Wet is still at large.
The Salvation Army farewell to Herbert
Booth was rightly described as the most successful
Army ; meeting ever held in Vie. —the most suc-
cessful from Mr. Worldly Wiseman’s new point,
with Barton, Lyne, Deakin, and Isaacs as the
stars. Nevertheless, the public didn’t rush the
show. There were 500 paid officers present, and
all the Melb. and suburban barracks were closed
that Harmy adherents might muster in force, but
the Hall was not full, and the old-time enthusiasm
was wanting. No “ Amens,” no “ Hallelujahs ” !
Scarcely any singing or praying. All was “piz-
ness,” under the management of the amiable
Shylock, junior.
If Australia wants a military commandant
of the proper sort (a late cable tells that Gen.
Hildyard is unwilling to accept the post) it pro-
bably couldn’t do better than get hold of Gen.
Hutton, the smart officer and hot curser
who earned the enmity of many fossilised
swashbucklers when he bossed N. S. W. de-
fences. Hutton severely tested the nerves of
various fat field-marshals, &e , who whiskied pro-
fusely on their forage-allowances and hired their
chargers from livery-stables. His favorite diver-
sion was to make them take their cab-nags over
stiff fences and fall off for the edification of a
soldier-loving public. The latest characteristic
news of Hutton is that he has run foul of the
War Office for cheeking some old-lady General up
Canada way.

How easy is the path of the detective !
A “notorious forger and oft-convicted criminal,”
sentenced at Melbourne the other day, found him-
self in court because he “casually saw Detective
M ‘Manamy in the street, and, taking him fora
fellow-criminal, revealed a number of fraudulent
schemes to him.” No wonder such a botchful
malefactor was “oft-convicted.”
Some inconvenient members of W.A. Par-
liament have been asking that a preliminary trial
should be made of the Coolgardie water-supply
scheme, as far as it has gone. They allege that the
pipes will not be able to stand the pressure put on
them ; and there is a wild rumor that, when a
half-mile length of pipe was tested, there were
more leaks than a person who was not strong on
mathematics could count. But the Govt, abso-
lutely refuses a preliminary trial. Hundreds of
thousands sterling have been spent already, and
the final cost will almost run to millions ; but
there is a cheerful go-the-whole-hog spirit in such
matters in Groperland. If the thing is going to
fail, let it fail completely, handsomely, dramati-
cally.
There was some curious lobbying in and
around N.S.W. Leg. Council last week when
lovely woman got her vote, with a tag (the right
to sit in Parliament) tied to it which may yet ruin
the gift. Jack Want and Pilcher on one side,
Suttor and Barney Wise on the other, fought a
good fight, not because of any particular pre-
judices one way or the other, but just for the fun
of the thing, and to try and dish the other fellow.
T. M. Slattery was entrusted with the ladies’ log-
rolling, and, moved deeply by the compliment to
his powers of political management, worked like
a bazaar-attendant. One curious result of the
division wa3 to bring into being a solid Labor-party
among the fossils. Hitherto there have been
Labor members, but not a Labor-party. Some of
these Labor members, such as Flowers and Stuart,
either intended to vote nay or not to vote at all,!
but they were urged not to “desert the party,
reminded that adult suffrage was the first plank of
the “party’s” platform, and over-persuaded. If
the Council has much longer to live, this new
“solid Labor party ” may vex its grey hairs.
Joseph Cook, has written at
great length to Sydney Telegraph pointing out
that the “educational test ’’proposed by the
Federal Govt, as a means of keeping out aliens is
a feeble expedient, that we should demand the
straight-out exclusion of all coloured races, and
that Britain will grant this if we stand firm. If
only Joe Cook had been half as valiant in office
as out of office, the position might be much easier.
He belonged to the Reid Government, which
passed a straight-out exclusion measure, saw it
rejected by the British Government, and didn’t
stand firm, but fell back on the educational test.
The result was that the educational test was
adopted by other States, and a precedent was
thus established which the British Government
apparently intends to adhere to. Joseph Cook
probably couldn’t prevent the Reid Ministry
backing-down in this manner, but if he had valued
his principles more than his portfolio he might at
least have thrown the latter up by way of protest.
Having saved one portfolio by adopting the
educational test, Cook may now get another by
damning the other fellow for adopting it likewise.

“In my opinion, the only practical way to
reduce the evils of alcohol to a minimum would be
to make the retail liquor traffic a State monopoly.”
Dr. Creed, M.L.C. (N.S.W.).
Connor, M.L.A., of Westralia, is to the
fore with an extraordinary Bill for the suppression
of newspapers. It provides that all weekly
newspapers, except those mentioned in a schedule
attached to the Bill, must deposit a large sum with
the W.A. Treasurer before they are allowed
to publish. No daily or bi- or tri-weekly has
been making itself especially unpleasant to Mr.
Connor, so they are excepted from this clause,
and those weeklies which have been respectful
will presumably be mentioned in the schedule,
but one which has been eminently disrespectful
will assuredly have to put up its deposit if the Bill
passes. Further it is proposed, among a few
other trifles, that if a libel verdict is given against
a paper, and all the getatable assets won’t pay it
in full, the proprietor or editor may be imprisoned
till the debt is paid, even if it takes 45 years to
raise the money, the bankruptcy law being sus-
pended in his case. Plainly Connor, M.L.A., is
very angry about something.
Last session, the Opposition proper in
Tasmanian Assembly comprised, all told, one
man. He was a host in himself—Edward Braddon
—still, he was only one man. Braddon went to
the Federal Parliament, and, when the present
session opened, there was no one on the front
Opposition bench. The House was adjourned for
a few weeks to let the Government prepare its
work, and, meanwhile, ex-Honorary Minister
Reibey sent out a circular, inviting disaffected
members to join in making an Opposition. A
queer crowd responded. Lyne (brother of Wil-
liam) strode right across from the Ministerial
cross-bench to the back Opposition. Nicholls,
who had sworn everlasting attachment to the
Government for its support of the Great Western
Railway syndicate, and Sadler both appeared on
the front Opposition bench. And the ancient
Reibey sat in Braddon’s old corner. “My vener-
able friend,” said Treasurer Bird, talking of the
new leader of the Opposition. Whether this was
sarcasm or serious is not certain. Years ago,
Reibey was the “ Venerable Archdeacon Reibey.”
Now, he’s an owner of racehorses and a fighting
man in Parliament, and, at the age of 80, he wants
to be Premier. He was Premier for 12 months a
long 24 years ago, and he has been waiting ever
since for the wheel to come round again.
An Argus interview with Alderman Cor-
nelius Job Ham is an interesting sketch-history of
Melbourne. Young Corny Job arrived in that
city with his saintly father Ham in 1842 (the year
when the City Council was founded), and he
remembers the old man preaching in the
Mechanics’ Institute, Collins-street, a building
that developed into the Athenaeum. One can’t
take a drink in the Athenaeum Club even now
without feeling that worshippers must have for-
merly assembled "thereabouts; and as for the
Athenaeum Hall, it will ever seem sacred to the
memory of R. S. Smythe’s parsons and serious
lecturers. One of the earliest Govt, institutions
established in Melbourne, says Ham, was “the
stocks.” True, they were not mining-stocks at
first, but thick wooden lockers with holes in them
and the feet of early offenders sticking through
the apertures. All the same, they were stocks
which provided temporary investment for the
rogues of the period No doubt they were
fixed where the Exchange now stands. Ham
reckons that the lawyers’ quarter of Melbourne
grew out of the fact that a popular solicitor named
Moore had his office somewhere near where Sel-
borne Chambers eventuated. A Mr. Ogilvie, and
other pioneer sharks, lay in wait for prey at the
same part of the young city, and eventually the
Law Courts came for the convenience of adjacent
practitioners. The medical quarter, at the east
end of Collins-street, had a similar inception. Dr.
Howitt and Dr. Wilmott were the big men of
the profession when Melbourne was shaping itself.
Patients flocked to the top of Collins-street with
their diseases, and other doctors came after them.
Old Dr. Youl, the late coroner, used to speak
of the time when his house in the present doctors’
quarter was confronted by green paddocks where
cows browsed promiscuously. Of the northern
suburbs of the city, Richmond and Collingwood
started long before Fitzroy, because the Eastern
Hill, now dominated by St. Patrick’s, had an en-
campment of blacks on it, and settlers fought shy
of the blacks. Brighton, Toorak, Hawthorn and
Glenferrie were each of them one-man suburbs at
the outset. Hawthorn grew from St James’s
Park, the estate of Sir James Palmer; Toorak
House, standing in 150 acres of park, was the
beginning of Melbourne’s plutocratic district, and
Glenferrie was originally the wooded property
appertaining to the mansion of rich man Ferrie.
Ham’s historical statement disposes of the natural
fiction that Glenferrie took its name from a ferry
across the Yarra.

ASTOUNDING CHALLENGE.
mSE Proprietor of Capilla Hair Tonic being desirous of having a public test made to
prove the infallibility of his now famous Hair Restorer, known as Capilla Hair
Tonic, is prepared to treat any gentleman troubled with absolute Baldness (it is
immaterial of how long standing) Free of Charge for a period of 12 or 16 weeks,
and guarantees in that time to produce- Hair long (by the aid of Capilla alone, used
according to the Direction supplied with bottle) on the scalp affected by Baldness.
Failing which, he is prepared to forfeit to the Sydney Hospital the sum of £25 sterling.
The conditions to be-::—list. That a committee of'six Sydney medical men, or well-
known Sydney gentlemen, be appointed to substantiate the fact of CAPILLA having
done what we claim for it. 2nd. That the gentleman upon whom the test is to be made
has upon the scalp a down or slight fluff. It is immaterial whether it is discernible with
the naked eye or not, so long as a microscopical examination proves it to exist.
We can procure actual living Proof of Capilla
having cured actual Baldness.
If the above challenge be accepted, we particularly Tequest the attendance of
Newspaper Reporters, Medical Gentlemen, or any person interested, to watch
the progress made day by day.

strengh, by about 1 inch to lh per month-

WHY WE ADVERTISE !
Because Advertising is the legitimate means vouchsafed to enlighten people,
to acquaint eacli other with their avocations and wants. If a man has a
Speciality, and has devoted years of his life and expended large sums of money in studying that Speciality and in
purchasing the necessary appliances, etc., for treating certain diseases, it would be absurd for him not to announce
the fact, and disseminate the knowledge universally, We have no apology to offer for informing the world through
this advertisement and otherwise of the means of relief we can afford to sufferers who need our help. Therefore,
however opposed to your own preconceived ideas and to those of your medical advisers, our method of treatment
may be, the fact still remains that it is successful, and this is verified by the thousands of testimonials received from
grateful patients. We make no secret of our mode of practice. We only claim to have utilised in a common-sense
way the result of scientific research and long experience. If you listen to the counsel of your sceptical friends and
jealous physician you may omit consulting us, but remember if you allow the disease to run riot in your system, you
are guilty of culpable neglect, and are alone responsible for your future pitiful condition.
Nine Diplomas and Certificates of Qualification and Registration by Government
medical Boards of New South. Wales, Victoria, South Australia, Great Britain
and America Open to Inspection by Dr. Wallace, our Physician-in-Chief.
Late Consulting; Staff, Homeopathic Hospital, Melbourne.

HOME TREATMENT.
-If you live out of town and cannot visit us, this need not deprive (
you of the benefits of our wonderful treatment, because in the new '
system of combining medicine and electricity a complete Electro-Medical home treatment is provided for. Scarcely a
day passes that the news does not reach us of some wonderful home cure of a person living so far away that a per-
sonal visit could not be made. Write a plain statement of your condition, explaining your feelings 'and symptoms as
clearly as possible, and from it we will be able to accurately diagnose your case. It only costs you the trouble of
writing, and twopence postage to get the opinion and advice of Australia’s Leading Specialists.
DO NOT BE DRUGGED TO DEATH BY QUACKS
When there are so many quacks and impudent pretenders in the field of Medicine, who arrogate to themselves
the monstrosity of giving counsel and advice in matters of which they are profoundly ignorant, and who have
nothing to recommend them but their audacity, sufferers cannot be too careful whom they consult. Although
liberty of choice belongs to all, discrimination is necessary. In consulting THE FREEMAN AND WALLACE
ELECTRO-MEDICAL AND SURGICAL INSTITUTE patients may feel assured that they shall receive the advice
of those fitted by superior skill and education, the result of special study. Our reputation and unparalleled
success as Specialists entitle us to your confidence.

The Darling Downs Federal election gave
the bumptious Philp Government a nasty knock.
The Philp Government backed candidate Bell
for all it was worth ; the leading Ministerial
organ in Brisbane supported him vigorously, and
wires were pulled in all possible directions to
secure his return. Groom, Having given in his
adhesion to the White Australia policy, received
the individual support of the Labor-party which
is waxing a big power on the Downs, and (thanks
to the blundering policy of the Courier, which
tried to rouse up the German and Scandinavian
farmers on a false cry) the almost solid vote of the
Teutonic settlers all over the district. A majority
of almost two to one in favor of Groom was
the answer of the Downs to Philp and
his wire-pullers. Out of seven State con-
stituencies in the Darling Downs electorate, six
voted for Barton and his White Australia policy,
by enormous majorities— six constituencies, by
the way, all represented by Philpites or semi-
Philpites in the present State Parliament; to
crown all, Bell’s own constituency of Dalby re-
turned a heavy majority against him. Now, the
Philp Government is trying to demonstrate that
it didn’t back Bell at all, but only sat on the fence
and wished him luck. And the Bananaland public
smiles.

ANOTHER OBSTINATE
t.s'VEin.
Another Effectual Cure.
Tha Oom of
Mr. E. TUCKER.
(a special REPORT.)
In consequence of information received, a
reporter interviewed Mr. Edwin Tucker, of
No. 108 Wilson-atreet, Redfern. A cordial
reception was extended to him, when a de
sire was expressed to let us know the follow-
ing facts :
“It is now fifteen years ago since my
physical well-being was upset for the first
time in my life,” sid Mr. Tucker. “ I was
then living in Goulburn, where many friends
of mine are still located. My liver asserted
undeniable proof that it was seriously de-
ranged, although I experienced more
anxiety from attacks of palpitation of the
heart, which I thought was diseased. It was
a common thing to be seized with sharp
■ipasms in my left breast, the violence of
wdiich almost doubled me up in agony.
These fits took away my breath for a while,
leaving me gasping and in an exhausted
condition. I observed great care in regard
t'' f ->od, everything that I ate and drank
oeiug of an easily-digested character, with
a view to obviating the development of my
complaint.”
“ Was your carefulness rewarded?”
“No; notin the least degree. Instead
of allaying the progress of the disease, my
sufferings became more intense every day.
Pains in the stomach after meals were daily
increasing in severity. The wind arising
from the same quarter was in greater abun-
dance, belching continuing long after meals
were concluded, i Vomiting often took
p’ace, the matter generally being green and
sour in taste, which was a source of un-
pleasantness in my mouth. My tongue was
never free from a coating of an unhealthy
rubstance, do whatever I may to try and
remove it, and I often had ringing sensations
in the ears, accompanied by dizziness in the
head, which made my sight quite weak,
with black spebks sometimes appearing
before my eyes. My complexion was
yi llow, and, with the thin, drawn aspect of
my face, I looked frightful. Behind the
shoulders there were continual pricking
p ins, and I had a lot of trouble with my
i idneys also. There was a never-ending,
dull, heavy, dragging pain in the lower
portion of my back, which was particularly
bad after lying down.”
“ How did your strength keep up ? ”
“ Whilst I could eat a little food I was
fairly stro g, taking everything into con-
sideration; but eventually my appetite left*
me altogether. I used to try very hard to
take something sustaining, but my efforts
were fruitless. Things that I could have
eaten ravenously before my illness came on
were obnoxious, and the smell produced a
feeling of sickness. Sometimes I had a
feeble desire for food, but the fulness in my
chest afterwards, besides the aggravation of
other pains, made me loathe the idea of eat-
ing for days. And I slept very indifferently
—turning from side to side in a restless
spirit of vexation, usually falling asleep
when it was time to get up. I felt more
dead than alive, with aching limbs and
leaden eyes. Just fancy a man getting up
in that condition to go to work ! No
- wonder I was always drowsy and disliked
exertion. Why, on Sundays and holidays
I made the most of my opportunities to rest,
'but the same old feelings of lassitude and
fatigue were always apparent. Relief from i
occupation did not diminish the acutenesß
of my Ailments. I believe, on the other
hand, my miseries were intensified when I
was not at business. Being too restless to
read or interest myself in anything, I spent
the leisure time in reflecting upon the
seriousness of my illness.”
“ Such ruminations would not benefit
you ! ”
‘‘ Of course not. But my general weak-
ness took all pluck out of me. I was too
low-spirited to take a hopeful view of my
case. My future seemed dark and cheer-
less, having been invalided so long. A
feeling of melancholy took possession of my
brain, and the unhappy thoughts w hich ran
through my aching head were enough to
distract me. Nervousness was one of the
most conspicuous symptoms of my disease.
Without knowing the reason, I frequently
found myself trembling most violently. J
had shocks to the system from causes which
were so trifling and insignificant that they
would have escaped a healthy person’s ob-
servation. My health was irretrievably
lost, I thought, as every medicine I had
taken did not show any beneficial effect.
Pills also proved equally useless. Then a
friend told me to try Clements Tonic. I
told him it would be folly to waste any more
money on medicines, as they could not cure
me. His persuasive eloquence at length
prevailed, and when I had taken about two-
thirds of a bottle of Clements Tonic, I could
see I was making rapid headway towards
recovery. After taking a couple of doses I
had a really refreshing sleep—the first I had
enjoyed for weeks. The distressing agonies
•of my liver complaint began to ease off, and
the palpitation of my heart ceased to trouble
me. As a matter of fact, there was soon a
wonderful change in my condition. I com-
menced to eat my food again with a relish,
and the disappearance of pains from the
stomach and shoulders intimated that my
digestive organs were improving. Giddi-
ness, nervousness, and headaches submitted
before many days to the remarkable sooth-
ing qualities of Clements Tonic, and my de-
nuded frame regained its lost flesh" and
healthy vigour.”
“ Has your health failed you since ? ”
“ With the exception of a bad turn whilst
working in the mines at Mount Zeehan,
Tasmania, seven years ago, my health has
been grand. The dampness in the mines
accounted for a severe cold, which resulted
in distressing complications setting in. I
took on my old remedy—Clements Tonic —
and again it cured me. It is the only medi-
cine that ever did me good, and at a time,
too, when all other physics had failed.
Clements Tonic worked my complaint light
out of my system, as I have not been
troubled with any of the symptoms since.
For the benefit of others, you are at liberty
to publish my statement how you like.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Edwin Tuckkr, of 108 Wilson-street, Redfern
Syrtney, in the Colony of New South Wales, do
solemnly and sincerely declare that I have care-
fully read the annexed document, consisting of three
folios, and consecutively numbered from one to three,
and that it contains and is a true and faithful account
of my illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also
contains my full permission to publish in any way
my statements—which I give voluntarily, without
receiving any payment; and I make this solemn
declaration conscientiously believing the same to be
true, and by virtue of the provisions of an Act made
and passed in the ninth year of the reign of her present
Majesty, intituled “An Act for the more effectual
abolition of Oaths and Affirmations taken and made
in the various Departments of the Government of
New South Wales, and to substitute Declarations in
lieu thereof, and for the suppression of voluntary and
Extra-judicial Oaths and Affidavits.”
Declared at Redfern t this 27th day of September, one
thousand nine hundred, before me,
J. GABBETT BOURCHIER, J.P.

Sydney Cooper, the veteran cattle-painter,
has given a nasty blow to certain writers who
ascribed to his “ temperance principles ” his being
able to do good work at 95 and over. He writes
to say that he has not touched tea or coffee for
over half-a-century, and latterly he has sworn off
milk. “Old Scotch” is his drink at luncheon
dinner and bed-time —“with an occasional glass
of champagne, or old port,” adds the cheerful
patriarch.
When Carnegie’s mother left Scotland
for Yankeeland she had to borrow 11s. to make
up her family’s fare. Andrew was only abairnthen.
The other day, 54 years after, he retired from
bu iness to his Scottish castle with a fortune of 70
millions and began to throw it round. So the
children of the woman who had advanced the 11s.
wrote to him to say they had none too much
siller and while he was splashing gold it wouldn’t
be a bad idea if he paid off the “wee bit score.”
Andrew did, with interest, and gave the two old
ladies enough-to live on comfortably till the end
of their days ; also he endowed the children of the
married one with a substantial amount. Carnegie
is a weary old dyspeptic, on whose soul the blood
of the murdered workers, shot by Pinkerton’s
police'll the Homestead riots, lies heavy as lead,
and now he is pouring out money like water to
wash that stain awaj and to get a fresh digestive
apparatus. The indigestion became acute and
chronic just after the Homestead affair, and
Andrew, a dour Scot at heart, has a supersti-
tious dread that it is a joodgement, ye ken.

“A funny incident relieved the monotony of
Melbourne Block one recent afternoon. Much
attention had been attracted by a very pretty and
evidently happy young girl in a daring but
successful gown, who was promenading with a
long, lean young actor. Suddenly a cab drew up
at the kerb, a stout, flushed, white-haired citizen
bounced out, charged down on the pair, took the
radiant little girl by her sweet, pink ear, and
bundled her aboard. The long, lean genius was
left without explanation gazing after the wake of
the vanished charmer.
“Re that story about the man who climbed the
railway-carriage partition to escape the dainty little
maiden who wanted her shoelace tied. A damsel
of that sort, travelling on the Brighton (Vio.) line,
saw a certain prominent business man enter one
compartment and promptly jumped into the next.
On arriving at the next station the dear little
thing complained, in heart-broken accents, to the
station-master that the capitalist had attempted
gross familiarities, her game evidently being to get
the matter settled on the spot for a lump sum.
But the young lady overlooked the delusiveness
of the ordinary mirror, and was astounded when
the officials pointed out that the partition between
the compartments went right up to the roof, and
that what appeared to be a space through which
even a Fatman could clamber was solid wood-
work covered with looking-glass. In the seclusion
of the ticket-office the railway authorities held
that young lady over the counter and impressed a
lesson in optics upon her by means of a strap,
which some evil-disposed person had out from an
earlier train.
“A well-known family in R.C. circles, out St.
Kilda way, have paid toll to Charon, one after
another, in extraordinary and most pathetic
fashion. One elderly married sister fell ill, and
another went to nurse her. The patient died, and
a few days later the sister who had nursed her
became seriously ill. A third sister, who has teen
a Sister of Charity in Sydney almost since the
establishment of the order, as a matter of privi-
lege was allowed to come to Melbourne to nurse
the patient. A few days later that unfortunate
succumbed ; then the nun became ill, and she, too,
died some days after. The three sisters perished
in less than a fortnight.
“ Two nurses in a Melbourne hospital have re-
cently netted gold-fish—and other desirable
patients at this present time are casting con-
valescent and grateful eyes at their ministering
angels. No wonder girls are crowding in shoals
to take up nursing. Hundreds clamor to be ad-
mitted to the Melbourne, Alfred, and Homoeo-
pathic Hospitals. There’s tremendous luck in the
calling. Given a wealthy widower, a serious
operation, and some time for convalescence
(another term for melting sentimentality), and ten
to one the sweet-voiced nurse carries the patient
to the haven of Hymen.
“ The secret of a leading lady’s many dress-
changes has leaked out. She wears her smart
frocks three or four times, then sells them,
and the proceeds help to get in fresh ones.
Of course these days every sensible sassiety
whirler disposes of her past frooks to the old-clo’
dealer. Nowadays the dealer is a very respectable
and presentable person, whom one is not ashamed
to ask into the sitting - room or the boudoir.
Appointments are generally made beforehand and
the coast cleared to allow of the deal being nego-
tiated privately. But cases have occurred when,
callers having arrived and encountered the minion
of the Three Balls, open confession under the seal
of secrecy has been considered the better part of
valour. .Sometimes the fashionable caller and the
old-clo’ dealer have exchanged smiles of recogni-
tion, and then the matter turns to laughter and
candid confidence. 9
“ Crematorium urns are the latest fad of curio-
collectors. A Toorak matron (one of the so-called
souls of academic and aesthetic circles) has been
the first to get hers in readiness —a beautiful gem
of mosaic and Greek lettering, in which the heroic
ashes of Dido might have rested. As a receptacle
for the remains of a modern dear departed, the urn
fails to justify its presence in the drawing-room.
The effect is bad—one seems to smell death in the
afternoon-tea.
“ Fashionable golfers are sporting these days
waterproof boots that reach to the knee. The
boots are a blessed boon. Heretofore, when fences
had to be climbed and creeks negotiated, the high
lift of the skirts, disclosing heterogeneous hues of
hose, bloomers, &c , led to some blushing and
giggling on the part of the caddies. Now
these young rascals need no longer risk
their moral propriety, for the high boots
will do away with all calf distinctfulness.
Yet it seems rather a shame to rob the caddies of
the joy when a specially lovely limb bursts into
view. They used to watch fence-climbing as one
looks on pyrotechnics, with a lively sense of grati-
tude for the expected.
“ A little while ago a Melbourne champion
golfer won a three-guinea trophy. The club asked
him to forward the account for the trophy he chose
to that amount. He did so, with thanks. But
the club scarcely-considered the account, ‘Hat—
three guineas,’ from a fashionable milliner’s estab-
lishment, the proper or legitimate kind of club
trophy to show for the money. Besides it was a
lady’s hat. So the champion was appealed to for
an explanation. He acknowledged that it was
for his best girl—‘and what’s hers is mine, and
what’s mine’s hers, don’t you know ? ’ But the
club didn’t know anything of the kind, and he had
to make the present of the hat out of his own
pocket, and content himself with a masculine
memento.
“Guy Madden, son of His Wax., is lieutenant
in the Melb. Scotch Regiment. G.M. is a
Pickwickianly fat boy, and in kilts is comically
reminiscent of the gargantuan ‘Evangeline ’ Rice
in ballet-skirts. Tust now he is the most popular
local leg-show.
“ In Robert Gordon Hyndman (who died Sept.
sth, at Miss Kelly’s Hospital, Melb ), Victoria
loses one of her most artistic architects. His
colored elevations came as a pleasant surprise
after the stiff mechanical productions of the ordi-
nary house-designer. He was also one of the best
and straightest of men, and his vocation was his
delight; no one worked harder. His face was in-
tensely artistic, yet full of virility, with strongly-
marked brows and deep-set eyes. He was an in-
timate of Phil. May and Spong, and was for
some time secretary to Vic. Artists’ Association.
After serving articles with Reed, Tappin and
Smart, he set up in business with one of his fellow-
pupils, E. T. Bates. His success was enormous,
and his office was considered a shining example of
good taste, and certainly of industry. The new
Agk office was one of his last big works. He
died in his 37th year—“fatal thirty-seven”
and quite suddenly.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.
The Father.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A child wrapped in blankets sat before the log*
fire.
“ Are you wanting anything, Sonny?” the m’nn
asked, pleadingly. The child did not stir or lift
the head that rested wearily on the pillow, b..t
gazed dreamily in the fire and murmured;
“I want Mums.”
A look of disappointment twitched the man’s
features; in a coaxing voice he continued :
“Won’t I do, Sonny? Your Mum’s busy
baking. Do you want a drink ? Shall I get you
a drink ? ”
“ I want Mums.”
Again, the reply seemed to hurt the man ; he
tried to take one of the child’s shrunken hands
in his own big palm, but the child pulled it
impatiently away.
“Shall I tell you a yarn about little pigs—or
the horse that wouldn’t go—or the ’possum with
three tails ? ”
“I want Mums.”
The man said no more, but sat silently by the
child, his eyes hungering. At the sound of a step
in the doorway the child turned his head about,
and the pinched features gave a flash of welcome.
“ I want you, Mums ! ”
The man clenched his teeth, as though to stifle
an imprecation.
“ What is it, my sweet ? ”
“ I want a drink.”
The mother held up the cup, with a glance, half
of triumph, at her husband. Whenever the father
spoke to the child his voice had a softer tone,
his face took a new expression. The wife had
noticed this change in him, a change she had never
seen at other times, not even at their courting.
Yet she said:
“You know the child does not like you to come
near him.” Perhaps the sharp look of pain in the
man’s eyes affected her, for she added—“ Not
when he is sick.”
He replied with a savage glance, behind the
child’s back. The woman returned it defiantly.
“ What would my sweet like to eat?”
The child would not say, nor, for much plead-
ing, give a sign of inclination, till, with a sort of
weariness, he answered :
“ Mums, I should like a little fish like Tom
caught.”
“But we have not got fish, sweetie ! ”
The child began to whimper. The father started
to the door, prompted by a sudden resolve.
• * * '9
Outside, the day had changed ; big drops of rain
fell, and a black bank of clouds in the south gave
token of a storm. The man did not heed this ;
but, reaching down a fishing-pole from under the
verandah, strode away towards the ereelc. On his
way he turned over some bark rotting on the
ground, and gathered up the black crickets that
sprang out.
On the bank of a big waterhole he unfastened
the line, baited, and threw it in. By this time
the rain was sheeting down, drenching him
through and through, but he did not heed it ; he
fished resolutely. He was not a skilled angler;
many times he caught himself, the bushes on the
banks, the boughs overhead; fish he could not
catch. He swore terribly, yet with a certain reti-
cence, so as not to scare the fish. Hours lie stood
there, numbed with cold and wet. It was nearly
dark ; his last cricket was on the hook ; when the
cork went under with a sudden tug. It was a
good bite, and he landed the two-pound perch
with joy and pride as though it had been so
much gold.
“ That’ll please him ; won’t he be glad ! ” he
cried aloud as he hurried home. He opened the
door with confidence, and hurried to where the
child sat.
“ I’ve got one, Sonny ! such a beauty ! ” Ex-
pectant joy beamed out of the man’s face.
With languid glances the child looked at the
prize.
“ I don’t like it; it’s too big ; I want a little
fish like Tom’s.” And he turned his face away.
There was scorn even in the mother’s eyes, and
they said as plainly as spoken words, “ What did
I tell you ? ” Night and darkness in his face, the
father laid the fisn down without a word and stole
out of the room—then on to the stable, where he
commenced to groom the horses, swearing as he
brushed and combed. And Tom, coming home
with the cows, knew his father was “crabbed,”
for it was always a vent for his rage to groom
his beasts.
Presently the wife came out and called across
the yard authoritatively, “Dad ! Sonny wants to
go to sleep; don’t make such a row.”
“I’ll deal with you!” he answered, threaten-
ingly; but it was under his oath, and, though
he continued to curse, his words were hissing
whispers,
The mother cunningly prepared the fish by
cutting off the middle and piecing head and tail
together, and so gave it to the child at supper-
time.
“ Mother’s got a nice little fish for her Lovey ! ”
The child smiled gratefully.
“ That’s better than Daddy’s fish. Dad’s fish
nasty ! ”
The father saw and heard all ; and blood
trickled from the lip his teeth clenched.
* • * •
Next day the child grew worse. He had pre-
viously been taken to the doctor, but there could
be now no question of that. Besides, it rained
torrents.
“I’ll go and fetch the doctor, mother!” the
father said resolutely.
“Perhaps'you’d better, Jack ; but think of the
money, think of the cost of it ! ”
The man went out into the storm, taking his
way to Pierson, who, like himself, was a surly,
dictatorial man. They were neighbours, and
Pierson was the last man from whom he cared to
ask a favor.
But the neighbor owned the only pole-buggy in
the district. “ Yes, you can have it, and welcome,
Harris,” said Pierson ; “do you want horses ?”
“ No,” said Harris, “ I will drive my own.”
Arrived at the township, the doctor was not at
home —was not expected home for twd hours. No
help but to wait." Harris drove into the store-
keeper’s yard and fed his horses, swearing the
while.
The doctor had not alighted when the waiting
father met him.
“The boy’s worse, is he? That’s a bad job.
Want me to come to your place now? Nine
miles, isn’t it ? I thought so. You know, it’s a
pound a mile, paid in advance. (Strictly in ad-
vance ! ” repeated the doctor, with emphasis.
“My word, doctor ! Surely you’ll trust a man
till the corn’s sold ? ”
“Not my own brother,” said the doctor, tum-
ing on his Heel; “ besides, as I told you when you
(Continued on next page.)

brought him in, medicine can do no good in this
case.”
“ Give him a chance, doc. ! give him a chance ! ”
the man pleaded, following. But the doctor was
inexorable, not appearing desirous of the journey,
even with the fee forthcoming, as he glanced at
the black sky and still-pouring rain.
The man crossed the road and entered the
store.
“Wet to-day, Harris. What can Ido for you ? ”
said the storekeeper, blandly.
“ I came to fetch the doctor, and he wants nine
quid; can you let me have it, Jamieson? You
know it’s right.”
“No, I can’t,” said the storekeeper, decidedly.
“I never lend money.”
“But it’s for my little Sonny, I tell yer; he’s
sick to dying.” The man’s words choked in his
throat.
“ Can’t help it. Very sorry, I’m sure. Glad,
as you know, to let you have a few months’ credit
for any stores you want; but money is out of the
question.”
“ Then curse you for a mean Scotch hound ! you
—you son of a cow ! you cold-blooded gohanna ! ”
said the man, bursting with wrath.
. As though the speaker had merely “ passed the
time o’ day,” the storekeeper, without the least
show of annoyance, continued weighing-out tea.
“Come outside, you wretch, and I’ll fight you
for it.” The man shook his fist across the
counter, and strode up and down the shop.
“ Come out in the yard, and I’ll fight you with
one hand, you dog ! ”
For half-an-hour the man stormed, threatened,
and pleaded with monotonous iteration ; finally
he drifted into the street, and to the public-house
bar at the corner. He drank two glasses of raw
spirit rapidly, one after the other. At ordinary
times such indulgence would have made him
drunk; now the nobblers seemed to sober him.
Declining an offered “shout,” he went into the
rain and the stables again, and he walked as
though with a sudden resolve.
“It’s a bad day for us. Bob,” he said, patting
his big horse and rubbing its forehead; “ I’d
rather chop my hand off, and you as good a horse,
Bob, as ever looked through a collar. My oath !
it’s rough, old horse : but you’ll have to go, Bob,
so help me ! You’ll have to go ! ”
The man entered the store again, speaking
hurriedly :
“ I’ll tell you what it is, boss. You know my
shafter; let me have him to drive the doc. to-day,
and he’s yours for the nine quid ; and you know
he is well worth twenty.”
The storekeeper did know, but gave no sign as
he quietly went to the ledger and turned up the
account of Dr. Cheeseman.
“I tell you what I might do,” he said, after a
slight pause ; “perhaps I can make it right for
you with the doctor ; and here’s a pen and ink—
you can make out a receipt while Igo over.” He
was at the doorway, when he paused and turned
back. “ Now, are there any groceries you want ?
Tea—flour—sugar salt rice—sago tapioca—
mustard vinegar oatmeal ? ” He rested a
moment interrogatively on each item.
“ For God’s sake, go over to the dootor ! Don’t
I tell you Sonny’s sick to dying ! ”
Paying no heed to the man’s violent negations,
the storekeeper continued: “Jams pickles—
sauce—salad oil—castor oil—tinned fish—herring-
salmon—sardines—and there’s a nice line of ling,
just opened, look at that! ”
For answer, the man took him by the shoulders
and forced him from the store.
• * • •
A few minutes later and Harris was at the
doctor’s door. “Come along, doc.! The trap’s
waiting outside.”
“ All right,” replied the doctor, who appeared
troubled; “but just come with me and look at
this room. Look at that wet now ! all streaming
down the wall, and that miserable Jamieson won’t
have the roof mended ! ”
“ Smother him ! Get another house, then ! ”
siid Harris with fierce impatience, stamping his
feet and grinding his teeth.
“ But there is no other house to be had in the
township, and Jamieson knows it. He wants me
to get it mended. To mend his house ! what do
you think of that ? ”
“Come along, doc. ! for Heaven’s sake come
along ! Sonny’s real bad, I tell you ! ”
“ Well, it’s a robbery, and that’s what I just
told him. He agreed to do all repairs, and now I
shall have to get it done. Now, if you are quite
ready, Harris, we’ll make a start.”
“Have you got your box of chemicals?” the
man inquired anxiously, as the dootor climbed
into the buggy.
“ All I require,” replied the doctor, touching a
bulky pocket.
A crack of the whip like a pistol-shot, a plunge,
a vast scattering of mud and slush, and they were
off. Leaving the township and the formed road,
they followed a bush-track at a hand-gallop. Al-
ready it was dusk, the rain continuing unabated.
“He didn’t eat his tea last night; nor his
breakfast this morning, doc. Does that count for
much?”
“Be oareful, man ! Look at the ruts ahead ! we
shall be thrown out.”
“ Never you worry ! we’re as right as the bank.
He was talking in his sleep all night—talking sort
of silly-like—didn’t know his mother. Is that bad
doc ? I’m thinking the like o’ that don’t count,
does it ? ”
“We were nearly over then! Shaved that
stump to an inch ! ” groaned the doctor, olutching
the seat as it danced with the jolts and ruts of the
road. The driving looked to him a sort of mad-
ness, for, the usual track being full of sloughs and
bog-holes, the man drove through the bush, turn-
ing and twisting as stumps and trees compelled,
and, now and then, going fairly, over some
prostrate log not otherwise to be avoided, and all
this —except the log-crossing—at full speed.
“ I’ve heard say’lectricity is a great thing; do
you think, now, it would do for Sonny ? I feel
sort o’ certain ’lectricity 'ud fix him up.”
The doctor’s answer was not audible above the
noise of the storm; he was tossing about as though
on agitated springs. The man did not turn his
head ; his eyes were in front, picking his road ; he
urged the horses by calling to them, and looked at
the sky anxiously; he was racing to get through
the bush before night came and utter darkness.
“ For mercy’s sake, go slow ! ” ejaculated the
doctor, suddenly, as he clutched the man’s arm ;
‘ l l cannot see my hand in front of me.” But the
driver, recalling his memory of the bush, was
watching the black foliage overhead as it pat-
terned on the starless sky, and so guided the
unchecked horses.
“ We are nearly there ! ” cried the driver at last,
as thev emerged from under the trees.
* • « •
The doctor could see nothing but a solitary
gleam of light blinking from some unknown dis-
tance, and, presently, the gleam and froth of a
flooded creek just ahead. If he had put his hand
on the driver’s pulse he would have felt it beat
then; for what seemed shrouded and dim to the
doctor was clear to Harris: there could not be
less than six or seven feet of water on the cross-
ing, with a current like a sluice. Knowing the
full danger, he did not waver for a moment; the
whip cracked with a volley of reports ; there was
a churning, splashing sound; and they were in
the flood.
“ We are not going to cross this ? ”
“ Oh, that’s nothing; only a bit of a fresh ! ”
“Stop, man ! Stop ! I wouldn’t cross this for
a, thousand pounds!” And the dootor clutched
ineffectually at the reins.
“ You sit quiet, doc. ; you’re as safe as the
bank. Feel if your chemicals are all right.”
. The doctor had to submit; they were so far
in the stream that it appeared safer to go on than
turn back; but presently, as he tried to peer
ahead in the gloom, it seemed to him the horses
sank suddenly into the shining flood, and he felt
the water round his feet.
“ Stop, you madman ! ” he yelled, as he felt the
buggy sway and toss under his feet like a rocking
boat. At the same moment the driver lifted him
bodily out of the trap into the stream.
“Hold on tight, doc! on that side; and I’ll
balance her on this ! ” So saying, Harris slipped
into the water, and, holding on witli one hand,
drove with the other. Now came a string of
oaths and curses, for the swimming horses wanted
to make straight for the landing, while the driver
headed them up stream, for only so was there
hope of not being swept away.
The doctor, clinging desperately to the floating
buggy, up to his neck in water, saw darker masses
loom up in the darkness, and with a swish and
swirl.roll by. White, glistening froth raced by in
eddying circles, and the boughs of floating trees
brushed him, hung to him for a moment, then
twisted away. And all this time Harris, with his
one free hand, was fighting a desperate battle for
leadership with the horses ; his only guide the ray
of light from the distant window of his own house.
The horses swam well; the danger was not in
them, but in the current that bore all down
stream. The doctor, after the first shock of
immersion, finding life still within him, had hope.
He prayed silently. The man, judging from
where they were that all was over, yelled the
curses of the damned. The buggy swayed and
nearly rolled over as a snag bumped it in its
course ; both men for a moment were pushed
under. Then they felt the stamp of feet, the
grate of wheels upon the stones. With a spring
Harris was in the buggy, helping the doctor to
climb up.
“ Are the chemicals all right, doc?” were his
first words.
“My God, man! you did all but drown us.
If I had only known ! ”
“Only a bit of a fresh, doc ; and now we’re
there. Come in the house gently; he may be
asleep. Perhaps he’s better. Do you think he’ll
be better ? ”
The child was in a chair-bed by the log fire, the
mother by his side crying silently. The two men
crossed the room on tiptoes. The doctor gave
one long glance at the little, wasted face and
sunken eyes, and said, gently, “I can give you no
hope.”
“ Don’t say that, doc—don’t say that ! ” the
man pleaded, desperately. The doctor shook his
head.
“Why, man!” said the father, with savage
madness, “I gave you nine pounds.” Still the
doctor shook his head. “ Well, make it twenty !
—make it all I’ve got; only give the kid a show! ”
The mother made a gesture, the child
moved, and the man was quiet suddenly. The
child parted hi 3 lips to speak; the father stooped
forward, eagerly.
“ Mums ! ” was the faint cry. The mother
waved the man back and took the child’s hand, as
though her share was all and undivided. Broken
sobs shook the man.
Still holding the mother’s hand, the child turned
his head wearily on the pillow, and motioned
slightly with feeble fingers. “ Dada! ” he
whispered ; and a light, almost of love, was in his
eyes.
It was the first, the final recognition.
HENRY FLETORER
The late Dowager Empress of Germany
was onetime the heroic child of whom Queen
Victoria wrote in her diary—“ Little Vicky sat
on a wasps’ nest, but was not in the least flurried
or put out.”
A journalist whose signature is sup-
pressed on principle writes to The Bulletin
on the Signed Article (a Bill introduced by P.
E. Quinn to N.S.W. Assembly, provides that all
leading-articles be signed with the real name of
the writers): —
“ Rappelle ! rappelle 1 et encore, rappelle /" This
phrase sounds quite convincingly in French, as the cap-
tion of a French article, signed by a French journalist.
And all France answered to the call—at least, all Paris
did, and that amounts to the same thing. Emilb db
Girardin was, moreover, commissioned to wait upon a
king, and make terms in the name of a People, and db
Girardin was the father of modern French journalism
and the creator of the authority of the signed article—in
France. But this thing would never work in an
English-speaking community, for the simple reason that
the literary mandate has never been accepted or obeyed
by the Anglo-Kelt as it has been by the Franco-Kelt.
American communities have tried the signed article
with varying results, modified by horse-whippings and
street brawls. The signed article can never flourish in a
nation where the duello is unacclimatised. Besides,
what value would signatures have among a people who
recognise newspaper politics as the affair of a board oi
directors, with an editor as middleman ? The writer of a
political leader is a journalistic servant who voices the
opinions he is told to voice ; just as a barrister is briefed
to plead, independently of his own abstract opinions on
the merits of a case. The writer as voter, and the
writer as professional, are two different persons. The!
signed article would simply limit his journalistic
opportunities and decrease his bread-and-butter)
wages. Perhaps, however, the public would be betten
served by a division of professional writers into
sheep and goats, Protectionists and Freetraders ; but
would it be just? The politician changes his politics!
according to the rules of political expediency. Why)
penalise the professional journalist ? When all is said, the
editor is the author of everything to which he gives edij
torial sanction. He issues his instructions like a Scriptural
centurion, and behold! that which he commands is!
obeyed. A modifying sentence or paragraph scored from
an article destroys its meaning as an expression of indi-j
vidual opinion. It becomes then the utterance of the;
editor—the most autocratic of all press-censors. Nay, it!
becomes the utterance of the board of management, of
which the editor is only the servant. But article-signing)
would do positive harm to the average pressman, without
benefiting the public one jot. Under such a system the
work of journalism would soon be run by a clique bearing
well-known names. The imprimatur of the mighty
“We” would only be authoritative when affixed by a)
widely-advertised writer, who would be singled out by!
the irritated recipient of his verbal castigation, and held
responsible for the sins of his employers. Plain-speaking
and honest comment would die, or else become the
usurped privilege of physical bullydom. No, the signed)
article is an impossibility without its natural concomitant
of the legalised duel.
Which suggests an idea : “ The writer of a politi
cal leader is a journalistic servant who voices tht
opinions he is told to voice; just as a barrister i(
briefed to plead, independently of his owi)
abstract opinions on the merits of the case.” Then
plainly we should have anonymous
appearing in disguise.
A Newcastle Miner Strikes Gold.
Mr. John Perry, a miner who lives at Albert-
street, Wickham, Newcastle, N.S. Wales, has
i’ust had a stroke of excellent luck. For years he
las worked in a local mine, handicapped amongst
his fellow-workers by attacks of Liver disorder,
Indigestion, and excruciating pains in the chest,
back, and shoulders, as well as many other ills.
Recently he struck gold in the form of Bile Beans
for Biliousness, and to-day he is amongst the
healthiest men that go down the pit. To an in-
terviewer he said : —“ I have suffered enough in
my time, and am not ungrateful enough to refuse
telling you that Bile Beans for Biliousness cured
me, as it may be the means of getting others to
take the Beans, and thus succeed in getting cured
like me. I have suffered more than anyone
could imagine. For years I had a bad liver,
and the doctors were unable to afford me any
relief. I became a martyr to indigestion, which
caused me great pain in the chest, on the back
and shoulders. It became almost impossible for
me to stoop to drive a nail or drill. Then I
became a victim to piles, and suffered from con-
stipation. Nothing I took seemed to do me any
real good. After the doctors failed, I tried vari-
ous patent medicines, but still I did not improve
in health. At length I became so distressed that
I could not eat my meals with relish, and I passed
many a miserable night for want of sleep. I
really did not know what was going to become of
me, I felt so frightfully ill and distressed. My
sight became quite dim, and I had dizziness in the
head. My mother read about Bile Beans for
Biliousness, and it was on her advice I took them.
My condition began to improve after I had taken
the first four doses of the Beans, and by the
time I had finished one box, I felt that I had
been cured. Bile Beans for Biliousness have done
more for me, at the cost of a few shillings, than
all the doctors, upon whom I have spent
pounds. It is a pleasure for me to work
now, and I shall ever be grateful for what
Bile Beans for Biliousness have done for me.”
Bile Beans, which cured Mr. Perry, are the
world’s specific for biliousness, indigestion, liver
and kidney disorders, constipation, piles, head-
ache, debility, nervousness, female ailments,
change of season ailments, colds, chill, rheumatism,
and as a preventative or curing the after-effects of
influenza. All chemists stock them, or you may
obtain direct from the Proprietors, 39 Pitt-street,
Sydney, by sending price—thirteen pence half-
penny. If it is still true that health is fortune, Mr.
Perry may now be regarded as one of the richest
men around Newcastle.
Starch Eating by Young Ladies.
A FOOLISH AND DANGEROUS HABIT.
It has recently befcn stated on the authority of a lady-
doctor that the eating of starch is a habit practised by
many young ladies in the hope of improving their com-
plexions. Many young ladies are led to contract this
dangerous habit because they overlook altogether the
fact that the complexion is a matter which the liver and
digestive organs govern by keeping the blood pure. To
have good blood the liver must be in excellent working
order, the stomach able to perform its part of the con-
tract, and the system must be absolutely free from all
digestive troubles. Bile Beans for Biliousness bring
about this condition of things, and girls with a pasty
white skin cannot do better than try them. Once the
organs of the body are in good order, the skin will soon
assume a beautiful color without aid from harmful
cosmetics.
